Philosophy Zoo

Fifty-five thinkers, each instantiated as a sub-algebra of the Idea Theory 10-tuple \((\mathcal{H};\,{\mathcal I},\circ,e,\rho,\sigma,\nu,\eta,\omega,\deg)\), where \(\mathcal{H}\) is the horizon category over which the original 8-tuple is fibered as a presheaf (the horizon functor ξ).

The ξ Extension (10-tuple)

Every entry below has been extended with a Horizon Structure (ξ) section that exhibits the philosopher's specific horizon category \(\mathcal{H}\), the morphisms of horizon-translation that matter for them, and which of the three horizon-functor theorems (T13 Hermeneutic Sheaf, T14 Indexical Pullback, T15 Profundity / Trans-horizonal Stability) they instantiate.

A number of philosophical primitives that other formalisms posit separately are derived from the 10-tuple rather than added to it: τ (temporality) is a totally-ordered subcategory of \(\mathcal{H}\); ι (intentionality / aboutness) is the trans-horizonal section \(\big(\rho_h(I)\big)_{h}\); π (perspective / agent) is a connected sub-category \(\mathcal{H}_\pi\subseteq\mathcal{H}\); φ (affect / conatus) is the gradient of \(\deg\) along \(\mathcal{H}\); δ (Saussurean differentiation) is the negative trace of \(\sigma_h\); β (rigid reference) is a constant global section; λ (potentiality) is the colimit of \(\rho\) along future-refinements; χ (schema / paradigm) is a connected component of \(\mathcal{H}\); μ (credence) is a measure on \(\mathcal{F}(h)\); ψ (signed valence) is the symmetric+antisymmetric decomposition of \(\sigma_h(\text{self},I)\); R (reflexion) is fixed-point self-application of ξ. None are independent primitives — all are read off the 10-tuple.

Theorem Guide For This Page

Philosophy entries often cite theorem labels in compressed form. Read them as follows: T1 means a claim about stable composition and identity; T2 means a claim about fit, similarity, payoff, or semantic support; T3 means preservation-plus-negation-plus-synthesis; T4 means emergence behaves coherently across longer compositions; T5 means asymmetry or directional tension matters; T6 means context or reframing changes the presentation while preserving structure; T7 means iteration or transmission over a chain; T8 means deep structural sameness beneath different surface forms; T9 means levels, hierarchy, rank, or abstraction.

If an entry says a philosopher's central claim is "T6" or "T8," do not read that as a mere cross-reference. Read it as a strong statement: once the philosopher's concepts are instantiated into the algebra, the cited claim is no longer an isolated historical intuition but a consequence of a reusable structure that also appears elsewhere in the zoo.

How to read this zoo

Each entry follows five fixed rubrics:

  1. The Position — what the philosopher actually claimed.
  2. The Instantiation — how each component \((\circ,\rho,\sigma,\nu,\eta,\omega,\deg)\) is concretely interpreted.
  3. Central Claim as Theorem — the position restated as a provable (or falsifiable) assertion about the algebra, keyed to theorems T1–T9.
  4. What the Formalism Adds — novel structure, predictions, or illuminations the algebraic view provides.
  5. Where It Diverges — what the formalism forces open or cannot capture.

The base algebra is recalled here for reference:

\[ ({\mathcal I},\circ,e,\rho,\sigma,\nu,\eta,\omega,\deg) \]
  • \(\circ\) — composition (synthesis of ideas)
  • \(e\) — identity idea (empty content)
  • \(\rho\) — resonance pairing \(\rho:{\mathcal I}\times{\mathcal I}\to\mathbb{R}\)
  • \(\sigma\) — curvature map \(\sigma:{\mathcal I}\to\Omega^2\)
  • \(\nu\) — negation / dialectical complement
  • \(\eta\) — reframing conjugation
  • \(\omega\) — transmission / propagation operator
  • \(\deg\) — grading (complexity, scope, abstraction level)

Pre-Socratic

Heraclitus

c. 535–475 BCE · Process Philosopher

The Position

Heraclitus of Ephesus, called "the obscure," writes in compressed aphorisms that resist paraphrase. His central insight is that the world is in perpetual flux: "all things flow," and "you cannot step twice into the same river." Stability is appearance; change is reality. Yet the flux is not arbitrary; it is governed by an underlying logos, a measure or rational principle that orders the changing.

The unity of opposites is his second major theme. Day and night, life and death, hot and cold, war and peace are not separate items in opposition but two phases of one process. The road up and the road down are one and the same. Strife (polemos) is the father of all things; without the tension between opposites, the world would not exist.

Fire is the privileged element — not as one stuff among others, but as the symbol of the transformative principle that animates all things. The world-order is "an ever-living fire, kindling in measures and going out in measures." Material change is the visible face of an underlying logos that persists through every transformation.

Knowledge of the logos is rare and difficult; most people live as if asleep, treating their private sense-experience as the whole. The wise discern the common logos that orders the kosmos and align themselves with it. Heraclitus is contemptuous of the polymathy of his contemporaries: knowing many things is not understanding the one thing.

Heraclitus has been read variously as proto-dialectician (by Hegel), proto-process philosopher (by Whitehead), and proto-nihilist (by Nietzsche, partly). His insistence on flux, his unity of opposites, and his emphasis on the logos as the ordering principle make him one of the most fertile early sources for Western metaphysics.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of momentary states or aspects of the cosmos: items insofar as they are phases of an ongoing transformation. Each element is tagged by its position in a process and by its opposed correlate. The identity \(e\) is the logos itself — the formal measure that persists through every change, the structural invariant of the flux.

Composition \(\circ\) is transformation: the process by which one phase passes into the next. \(\circ\) is governed by the measures (metra) the logos imposes; not every transition is admissible, and the admissible transitions exhibit the deep ordering of the kosmos.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is processual continuity: the degree to which two phases belong to a common transformation. Curvature \(\sigma\) is the curvature of opposition: non-zero precisely between paired opposites, marking the irreducible tension that drives change.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the opposite of \(a\) within the unity of opposites: not external destruction but the correlate phase that constitutes \(a\)'s identity. Hot and cold are each other's \(\nu\); without the opposition, neither would have determinate content.

Reframing \(\eta\) is the recognition of the logos beneath the flux: the operator that takes a momentary phase and exhibits its place in the overall measure. Transmission \(\omega\) is the cosmic process — the ongoing kindling and going-out of fire by which the world maintains itself in change. The grading \(\deg\) measures depth of insight: surface flux at the bottom, recognized logos at the top.

\[ \nu(a) \text{ co-constitutes } a, \qquad \omega = \text{ever-living fire kindling in measures}, \qquad e = \text{logos} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that change is universal: every element of \({\mathcal I}\) is a phase of a transformation, none is stable. \(\circ\) is everywhere active; the algebra has no static elements that escape the process. This is a T1+T7 claim with strong dynamism.

The second claim is the unity of opposites: \(\nu(a)\) is co-constitutive with \(a\). Removing \(\nu(a)\) does not enlarge \(a\) but destroys the very tension that gives \(a\) its content. This is a T1+\(\nu\) claim with deep structural implications, anticipating Hegel.

The third claim is that the logos is the invariant of \(\circ\): the measure preserved through every transformation. Although every element changes, the structural law of change does not. This is a T8 claim of unusual reach: structural sameness beneath every difference.

The fourth claim is that \(\sigma \neq 0\) at every locus of opposition. The curvature is not a defect to be smoothed but the engine of the world; without the asymmetry, no transformation would occur. This is a T5 claim with dynamical content: curvature drives \(\omega\).

The fifth claim is the epistemic asymmetry between the wise and the sleeping. Most observe only their own dream-fragments of the flux; the wise discern the common logos. Algebraically, most observers occupy small, disconnected sub-algebras; understanding consists in recognizing the global structure. This is a T9 claim with a strong cognitive ordering.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the unity of opposites becomes the structural assertion that \(\nu\) is not subtractive but co-constitutive. The Heraclitean polemic against treating opposites as separable items gains an algebraic form: in this algebra, opposition is internal.

Second, the logos as invariant of transformation is the assertion that \(\omega\) preserves a non-trivial structural feature. The algebra hosts a Heraclitean fire that keeps a measure across all its kindlings.

Third, the contempt for polymathy becomes a structural fact: knowing many disconnected elements is knowing the algebra at low \(\deg\); the wise occupy a higher \(\deg\) where structural relations are visible.

Fourth, the river-image — never the same river twice — becomes the assertion that the elements of \({\mathcal I}\) at successive times are non-identical, even when they share a name. The structural identity is at the level of process, not of element.

Fifth, the cosmological role of fire as transformative principle is given algebraic location as the global \(\omega\)-operator. The cosmos is the orbit of \(\omega\); fire is what \(\omega\) is, viewed materially.

Where It Diverges

First, the aphoristic compression of Heraclitus's prose is part of his philosophical method; the algebra's articulated structure is exactly the systematic form Heraclitus avoided. Translating his oracular brevity into structural relations risks losing the koan-like force.

Second, the ethical and political content of Heraclitus's surviving fragments — his critique of his fellow Ephesians, his praise of waking lawfulness — depends on substantive cultural commitments the algebra does not generate.

Third, the religious dimension — Heraclitus speaks of "the one wise" and treats the logos with reverence — invokes a register of sacredness the algebra is silent about.

Fourth, the precise content of the logos remains famously obscure. The algebra's identification of \(e\) with the logos is a structural placeholder; what the logos materially is, in Heraclitus, is not settled by structural facts.

Fifth, the tendency of later readers to systematize Heraclitus into a doctrine he himself never offered is a hazard the formalism magnifies. Heraclitus's gift was the provocative fragment; the algebra is the antithesis of fragmentary provocation.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

For Heraclitus, \(\mathcal{H}\) is the totally ordered τ-subcategory of time-slices through which the river, the city, and the cosmos pass. Morphisms \(f:t'\to t\) are translations from a later phase back to an earlier measure: they preserve the logos as form while almost never preserving the local \(\sigma\) of hot/cold, waking/sleeping, war/peace. The crucial derived primitives are τ for flux and χ for the common world shared against private dream-horizons.

This is T15 in archaic form: the logos is precisely the trans-horizonal stability of measure under the otherwise unstable support of every finite phase.

Parmenides

c. 515–450 BCE · Eleatic Monist

The Position

Parmenides of Elea, in his philosophical poem On Nature, presents a goddess's revelation of two ways of inquiry: the way of truth and the way of opinion. Only the way of truth leads to genuine knowledge; the way of opinion follows the senses and ends in confusion.

The way of truth begins with a single rigorous principle: what is, is; what is not, cannot be thought or spoken. To say of something that it is not is to attempt to think a non-being, which is impossible. From this Parmenides derives the consequence that being is one, ungenerated, indestructible, indivisible, motionless, and complete — a perfect sphere of pure existence.

Generation and destruction are illusory. For something to come into being would require it to come from non-being (impossible) or from being (in which case it already was). Likewise for change: change requires a passage from what is to what is not, which the principle forbids. The world the senses present is an illusion; reality is changeless unity.

The way of opinion (doxa) is the second part of the poem, in which the goddess offers a cosmology of mortal belief — light and night, the heavens, generation — but presents it explicitly as deceptive. Why the goddess offers this section at all is one of the great interpretive puzzles of ancient philosophy.

Parmenides is the founding figure of Western metaphysics insofar as he poses the question of being as a question, and he is the foundational rationalist insofar as he subordinates the senses to the demands of pure thought. Plato, Aristotle, and the entire subsequent tradition contend with the Parmenidean inheritance.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be a set with at most one element: being itself (\(\tau o \, \dot e \dot o \nu\)). The radical Parmenidean ontology compresses \({\mathcal I}\) to a singleton in the way of truth. The identity \(e\) coincides with this single element; in the way of truth, every operation collapses to identity.

Composition \(\circ\) is trivial in the way of truth: the only available composition is \(e \circ e = e\). The algebra is degenerate by design. In the way of opinion, \(\circ\) is rich but illusory; the apparent compositions of mortal belief have no ontological standing.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is identity: \(\rho(e,e) = 1\), and there are no other pairs to resonate. Curvature \(\sigma = 0\) absolutely: being has no curvature, no asymmetry, no irreducible tension; it is perfectly self-identical, a featureless sphere of pure existence.

Negation \(\nu\) is undefined or empty in the way of truth: there is no non-being to which \(\nu\) could send anything. The forbidden gesture of attempting \(\nu(e)\) is the very illusion the goddess warns against.

Reframing \(\eta\) collapses to identity in the way of truth (there is nothing to reframe to). Transmission \(\omega\) is also trivial: being does not transmit anywhere, since there is nowhere else. The grading \(\deg\) is constant. The way-of-opinion cosmology projects a richer apparent algebra, but it is a structural illusion.

\[ {\mathcal I}_{\text{truth}} = \{e\}, \qquad \sigma = 0, \qquad \nu \text{ undefined}, \qquad \omega = \text{identity} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is the radical monism: \({\mathcal I}\) has at most one element. All apparent multiplicity is illusion. This is a structural claim of unusual austerity — the algebra is collapsed to a point.

The second claim is that \(\sigma = 0\) absolutely. There is no curvature, no asymmetry, no irreducible tension; being is perfectly homogeneous. This is a T5 claim of negative form: the algebra has no genuine T5 content.

The third claim is that \(\nu\) is forbidden: there is no non-being. Attempting to apply \(\nu\) is the path of error. This is a structural claim that the algebra has no negation operator at all in its true form.

The fourth claim is that \(\omega\) is trivial. Transmission requires somewhere to transmit to; in the way of truth, there is nowhere else, and \(\omega\) collapses to identity. This is a T7 claim with degenerate content.

The fifth claim is that the way of opinion presents a richer apparent algebra that is structurally false. The cosmology of mortal belief is a structural illusion projected by the senses; the formalism would represent it as a phantom algebra distinct from \({\mathcal I}_{\text{truth}}\). This is a meta-claim about which algebra is the real one.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the radical monism becomes the structural fact that \({\mathcal I}\) is a singleton. The algebraic content of Parmenidean monism is degenerate by construction; the formalism makes the degeneracy precise rather than mysterious.

Second, the impossibility of change becomes the structural fact that \(\omega\) is trivial. There is nowhere for being to go; transmission has no target. The Parmenidean immobility is given algebraic form.

Third, the impossibility of generation and destruction becomes the structural fact that \(\nu\) is undefined. The algebra has no place where being could pass into non-being or be generated from it.

Fourth, the way-of-opinion cosmology becomes the projection of a phantom algebra. The formalism allows comparing \({\mathcal I}_{\text{truth}}\) with \({\mathcal I}_{\text{opinion}}\) as two algebras, with the latter recognized as illusory by the goddess.

Fifth, the positioning of Parmenides in the philosopher zoo as the limit case of monism gives subsequent thinkers (Plato, Aristotle, Heraclitus) something definite to react against. The other algebras can be compared with the Parmenidean degenerate one as variations away from it.

Where It Diverges

First, Parmenides's poem is a religious revelation, not a philosophical argument in the modern sense. The structural form of the claim does not honor the oracular mode of presentation, in which the goddess speaks with an authority no algebra can carry.

Second, the puzzle of why the goddess offers the way of opinion at all — whether as a propaedeutic, as a charitable accommodation, as an exhibition of error — is a substantive interpretive question that the bare structural collapse cannot illuminate.

Third, the relation between thought, speech, and being that Parmenides presupposes — that what cannot be thought cannot be — invokes a substantive philosophy of mind and language outside the algebra.

Fourth, the historical influence of Parmenides on Plato (the Eleatic stranger, the Sophist's parricide), on Aristotle (the response in Physics), and on later metaphysics is a richness the algebra collapses by treating Parmenides as just one structural option.

Fifth, the felt force of the Parmenidean "you cannot say of what is not that it is" is a logical-modal intuition that the algebra schematizes but does not vindicate. Whether the principle should be accepted is precisely the question Parmenides wants the reader to confront.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Parmenides splits \(\mathcal{H}\) into two radically unequal horizons: the way of truth and the way of mortal opinion. The only admissible morphism of philosophical ascent is from doxa to alētheia, where every apparent plurality, motion, and negation is restricted to the singleton algebra of Being; no reverse morphism can preserve truth. τ is denied any ultimate standing, while χ marks the gulf between the mortal schema and the goddess's disclosure.

This is T14 as a metaphysical prohibition: the inferential and perceptual \(\sigma\) of the opinion-horizon fails to pull back into the truth-horizon, leaving change as obstruction rather than knowledge.

Ancient

Plato

428–348 BCE · Idealist

The Position

Plato's central conviction is that the world disclosed to the senses is not the world of true being. Sensible things — a beautiful body, a just act, a triangular figure drawn in sand — are at best shadows or imitations of something more fundamental: the Forms (\(\varepsilon\tilde\iota\delta\eta\)). Each Form is a single, eternal, unchanging archetype that many sensible particulars can imperfectly resemble. To know what beauty is, one must grasp the Form of Beauty itself, not the changing instances that come to be and pass away.

The most famous statement of the doctrine appears in the central books of the Republic, where the Allegory of the Cave, the Divided Line and the Sun together stage a climb from images, through sensible objects, through mathematical objects, up to the Forms, and finally to the Form of the Good — the unhypothetical first principle that gives intelligibility and being to everything below it. The Good is not just one Form among others; it is what makes the other Forms knowable, in the way the sun makes things visible.

Knowledge in this scheme is not empirical accumulation but anamnesis — the soul's recollection of Forms it grasped before its embodiment. The Meno dramatizes this: an unschooled slave-boy, prompted by Socratic questioning, recovers a geometrical truth he was never taught. Learning is therefore a kind of return, not a kind of construction; the soul already possesses what it comes to know, and the dialectical encounter merely awakens it.

The instrument of this awakening is the elenchus, the cross-examination practiced by Socrates throughout the early dialogues. The elenchus does not assert: it refutes. It strips away the merely conventional, the inherited, the half-understood definition, and exposes the inquirer to aporia — the productive embarrassment that opens the way to genuine recollection. Philosophy, for Plato, is thus essentially purgative before it is ever positive.

Politically and erotically, the same vertical movement repeats. In the Symposium, eros ascends from the love of one body to the love of all beautiful bodies, then to beautiful souls, then to beautiful institutions and sciences, and finally to the Form of Beauty itself. In the Republic, the philosopher-king has made this ascent and is therefore competent to rule, because he has seen what justice and goodness are, not merely what they are conventionally said to be.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the lattice of Platonic Forms together with their sensible images. Each element of \({\mathcal I}\) is tagged by an ontological rank in \(\{\text{image}, \text{sensible}, \text{mathematical}, \text{Form}, \text{Good}\}\), so the underlying carrier already encodes the Divided Line as a discrete grading. The identity element \(e\) is naturally identified with the Form of the Good: it is the Form that all other Forms presuppose and by which they are made intelligible, hence the unit relative to which everything else is measured.

The composition \(\circ\) is interpreted as participation: \(a \circ b\) is the complex Form (or sensible composite) in which \(b\) participates in \(a\). Thus \(\text{Justice} \circ \text{Polity}\) is "a polity participating in Justice," and \(\text{Beauty} \circ \text{Body}\) is "a body participating in Beauty." Composition is not symmetric — the participating term is asymmetric to the participated Form — and it is associative only modulo the higher-ranked term absorbing the lower; this matches Plato's repeated insistence that the Form is "in" the particular without being exhausted by it.

The resonance pairing \(\rho(a,b)\) measures kinship between Forms: how much of a's intelligible content is also content of b. Thus \(\rho(\text{Justice},\text{Temperance})\) is high (both belong to the cardinal virtues, both flow from the Good), while \(\rho(\text{Justice},\text{Triangle})\) is low. Resonance is high precisely when two Forms cluster under a common higher Form, so \(\rho\) is essentially a similarity inherited from the upward filtration toward the Good.

Curvature \(\sigma\) is the geometry of the Divided Line itself: the discontinuity between sensible and intelligible regions, and the further discontinuity between mathematical objects and Forms proper. \(\sigma\) is large precisely where the line "bends" — at the cuts where one cannot pass smoothly from one segment to the next without a change of cognitive faculty (eikasia, pistis, dianoia, noesis). The negation \(\nu(a)\) is the material shadow of \(a\): the deficient, becoming-image cast of an unchanging Form, so that \(\nu(\text{Form}) = \text{shadow}\) and \(\nu(\nu(\text{Form}))\) returns not the Form itself but only the soul's recollection of it.

The reframing \(\eta\) is the elenchus: an operator that takes a confused composite \(a \circ b\) and returns the purer term, \(\eta(a \circ b) = a\) when \(a\) is the higher Form. Transmission \(\omega\) is pedagogical ascent: applied repeatedly to a learner's beliefs, \(\omega\) drives them up the line, with the Forms appearing as fixed points. Finally, \(\deg\) is just the integer-valued ontological rank read off the Divided Line, so that \(\deg(\text{Good}) > \deg(\text{Form}) > \deg(\text{mathematical}) > \deg(\text{sensible}) > \deg(\text{image})\).

\[ \deg(\text{Form}) > \deg(\text{shadow}), \qquad \omega(\text{Form}) = \text{Form}, \qquad \eta^n(\text{opinion}) \xrightarrow{n\to\infty} \text{Form} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that the Forms are exactly the fixed points of the transmission operator \(\omega\). Pedagogical transmission, dialectical examination, and the soul's own movement upward all leave a Form unchanged precisely because there is nothing accidental in it to be stripped away. This is a T7 claim: under iterated propagation along a transmission chain, the only stable carriers are the Forms, while sensible images and unexamined opinions are dissipated. Anamnesis is then the assertion that the soul, considered as a transmission medium, already terminates on these fixed points.

The second claim concerns the elenchus. Socratic refutation is not a destructive act but a structural one: it lowers \(\deg\) by removing exactly the components that fail to participate in a higher Form. Formally, if \(a = f \circ m\) with \(f\) a Form and \(m\) accidental matter, then \(\eta(a) = f\) and \(\deg(\eta(a)) \leq \deg(a)\), with equality only when \(a\) was already a Form. This is a T6 claim: reframing changes presentation while preserving — indeed isolating — the underlying intelligible structure.

The third claim is that the Divided Line is a genuine filtration of \({\mathcal I}\), not merely a metaphor. The four segments correspond to four nested subalgebras \({\mathcal I}_0 \subset {\mathcal I}_1 \subset {\mathcal I}_2 \subset {\mathcal I}_3\), with \(\circ\) closing each level to itself and \(\eta\) carrying each level into the next one above. This is a T9 claim about hierarchy and abstraction: cognitive faculties are not freely chosen vocabularies but are forced by the level at which one's ideas live.

The fourth claim is that the Form of the Good plays the role of the identity element \(e\). It is the only Form such that, for every \(a\), \(\text{Good} \circ a = a \circ \text{Good} = a\): every Form remains itself when situated under the Good, because the Good is what made it a Form in the first place. This is a T1 claim about stable composition and identity: the entire monoid is built around a single, hyper-stable, intelligibility-conferring unit.

The fifth claim is that anamnesis is a recovery theorem for the resonance \(\rho\). When the soul recollects a Form, it does not generate new content; it locates a high-\(\rho\) match between a present sensible occasion and an antecedently possessed Form. This is a T2 claim: knowledge consists in the discovery that some perceived configuration already pairs maximally with a Form already present in the soul, so learning is the elimination of distance in \(\rho\) rather than the addition of new vocabulary.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the formalism exposes participation as a genuine algebraic operation rather than a metaphysical mystery. Plato is famously vague about how a sensible thing can "share in" a Form; once \(\circ\) is given an associative monoid structure with the Good as identity, participation becomes a definite arithmetic of composition, and the long history of objections about "self-predication" can be re-examined as questions about whether \(a \circ a = a\) or \(a \circ a \neq a\), i.e. whether Forms are idempotents.

Second, the resonance pairing \(\rho\) supplies the quantitative account of kinship that Plato gestures at but never delivers. He repeatedly asserts that some Forms are "akin" (\(\sigma\upsilon\gamma\gamma\varepsilon\nu\eta\varsigma\)) to others — Justice and Temperance, Beauty and Goodness — but he provides no measure. The pairing makes kinship a real number, allowing claims like "Justice is closer to Goodness than to Triangle" to be cashed out and tested against the rest of the dialogues.

Third, the curvature \(\sigma\) gives a precise location to the famous "discontinuities" of the Divided Line. Where Plato says the segments differ in clarity and being, the formalism says \(\sigma\) is large at exactly two cuts and small elsewhere. This converts a metaphor into a structural prediction: cognitive transitions across these cuts should require qualitatively different operations, which matches Plato's insistence that one cannot pass from dianoia to noesis by mere accumulation of mathematical knowledge.

Fourth, by treating the elenchus as the operator \(\eta\), the formalism explains why Socratic refutation is productive even when it ends in aporia. \(\eta\) is a retraction: it can lower \(\deg\) without ever increasing it, but its fixed points are exactly the Forms. Aporia is the algebraic moment in which \(\eta(a) = a\) but \(a\) is not yet a Form — i.e., the elenchus has no further accidents to strip, but the soul has not yet performed the upward leap. The formalism therefore predicts the experiential character of aporia as a stagnation point of \(\eta\).

Fifth, the transmission operator \(\omega\) reframes the cosmology of the Timaeus and the educational program of the Republic as the same thing seen at different scales. Demiurgic shaping of matter, pedagogical formation of the guardian class, and erotic ascent in the Symposium all become applications of one operator in different domains. Plato himself never explicitly unifies these; the formalism does, and so makes visible what was implicit in his architectonic.

Where It Diverges

First, the formalism treats \({\mathcal I}\) as a set with a cardinality. Plato's Forms are not a set: they form a hierarchy whose top member, the Good, is "beyond being" (\(\dot\varepsilon\pi\dot\varepsilon\kappa\varepsilon\iota\nu\alpha \tau\eta\varsigma o\dot\upsilon\sigma\dot\iota\alpha\varsigma\)) and so cannot be a set-theoretic element on the same level as the others. Treating the Good as a unit \(e\) inside the monoid normalizes precisely what Plato most insists is not normal.

Second, participation is one-directional and many-to-one in Plato — many sensible triangles participate in one Form of Triangle — whereas the monoid \(\circ\) is in principle a two-sided operation. The formalism can record asymmetry by demanding non-commutativity, but it cannot capture the deep ontological irreversibility of participation: a sensible can never become a Form, no matter how it is composed.

Third, the negation \(\nu\) in the formalism is at most an involution (\(\nu \circ \nu = e\)). For Plato, the relation between a Form and its shadow is not an involution but an asymmetric casting downward: applying it twice does not recover the original. The formalism can suppress this only by abandoning the involutive interpretation of \(\nu\), at the cost of losing some of its structural identities.

Fourth, anamnesis in Plato presupposes a pre-existing soul with a pre-existing acquaintance with Forms. The formalism is silent about the carrier of \(\omega\): it treats transmission as an operation on \({\mathcal I}\) without committing to who or what does the transmitting. Plato's psychological and theological commitments — the soul's preexistence, its kinship with the divine, its tripartite structure — are simply absent from the algebra.

Fifth, the very existence of the Form of the Good as the identity \(e\) sits uncomfortably. Plato's Good is not a neutral operation-leaver but the source of intelligibility and being for every other Form; it acts as much like a measure or a generator as like a unit. The formalism collapses this generative role into the bland algebraic property of "leaves everything unchanged," and so loses the most distinctive — and most metaphysically loaded — feature of Plato's mature ontology.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Plato's \(\mathcal{H}\) is a stratified ascent from cave-images, to sensible objects, to mathematical dianoia, to the intelligible Forms and the Good. Morphisms are dialectical conversions of the soul: each restriction drops shadow-dependent content and preserves only what can survive eidetic clarification. The derived primitives are χ for the divided line's strata, ι for the soul's directedness toward Forms, and β for Form-terms that function as stable references across changing instances.

This is T15: the Form is the idea whose \(\deg\) is high because its support remains stable across sensible, mathematical, and dialectical horizons.

Aristotle

384–322 BCE · Hylomorphist

The Position

Aristotle inverts the Platonic priority. For him, Forms do not exist in a separate intelligible realm; they exist in matter, as the structuring principle of concrete substances. A bronze statue is not the imperfect imitation of a Form of Statue floating elsewhere; it is matter (bronze) informed by form (the shape and function of a statue), and these two factors are inseparable in any actual being. Substance is composite — \(\sigma\upsilon\nu o\lambda o\nu\), the "taken-together."

This hylomorphic doctrine drives Aristotle's account of change. To explain how something can come into being or pass away without violating the Parmenidean prohibition on coming-from-nothing, he distinguishes potentiality (\(\delta\dot\upsilon\nu\alpha\mu\iota\varsigma\)) from actuality (\(\dot\varepsilon\nu\dot\varepsilon\rho\gamma\varepsilon\iota\alpha\)). The bronze is potentially a statue and actually a lump; the sculptor's activity actualizes what the matter already harbored as possibility. Change is the road from potency to act, not the appearance of a wholly new thing.

Knowledge follows the same architecture. The intellect (\(\nu o\tilde\upsilon\varsigma\)) does not recall; it abstracts. Beginning from sense perception of particulars, the soul performs an upward operation that strips away the accidents of matter and recovers the universal form embedded in them. Universals exist, but only in the particulars and in the mind that has abstracted them. There is no separate Platonic heaven of Forms.

This abstraction is supported by the four causes — material, formal, efficient, and final — which together exhaust the kinds of "because" answer one can give about any substance. The bronze is the material cause of the statue, its shape is the formal cause, the sculptor is the efficient cause, and the purpose for which it was made is the final cause. Causal explanation is therefore plural and irreducible, and the metaphysics of substance is at the same time a theory of explanation.

The categories — substance, quantity, quality, relation, place, time, position, state, action, passion — supply the basic kinds of being. Substance is primary; the others exist as modifications of substance. The syllogistic, Aristotle's invention, then formalizes how predications across the categories may legitimately compose into demonstrations. The whole edifice — physics, metaphysics, logic — coheres through a single insistence: form is real, but only as form-of-something.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of hylomorphic composites: each idea is a pair (form, matter) with a category tag. The identity \(e\) is interpreted as pure form-without-matter — the unmoved mover, the "thought thinking itself," which on Aristotle's own account is the only being whose actuality is unmixed with potency. Every other element of \({\mathcal I}\) lives below \(e\) in the sense that some matter still adheres to it.

The composition \(\circ\) is predication: \(a \circ b\) is the substance \(a\) modified by the accident or form \(b\). Thus \(\text{Socrates} \circ \text{wise} = \text{wise Socrates}\). Composition is associative when predications stack within compatible categories, but it fails when one tries to predicate across category boundaries — one cannot legitimately compose a substance with a relation in the same way one composes it with a quality. The category structure is therefore a partial groupoid sitting inside the monoid.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) measures similarity of form: two substances resonate when they share a common formal essence even though their matter differs. \(\rho(\text{Socrates},\text{Callias})\) is high because both are humans (same form), even though their bodies are distinct (different matter). Resonance is therefore essentially a measure of co-membership in a natural kind, and Aristotle's biological taxonomy can be read as the empirical mapping of \(\rho\) across living substances.

Curvature \(\sigma\) encodes the tension between potentiality and actuality. A substance with much unactualized potential — an acorn, a sleeping geometer — has high \(\sigma\), because there is a real gap between what it is now and what it can become. A substance whose potential is fully actualized — the unmoved mover, or, locally, the geometer actively contemplating — has \(\sigma = 0\). The negation \(\nu(a)\) is privation: the lack proper to \(a\), so \(\nu(\text{sighted}) = \text{blindness}\), and \(\nu\) is not a free involution but is determined by the form of \(a\) itself.

The reframing \(\eta\) is abstraction: \(\eta(a \circ m) = a\), where \(m\) is the matter and \(a\) the form, so abstraction kills exactly what is material in the composite. Transmission \(\omega\) is inductive generalization (\(\dot\varepsilon\pi\alpha\gamma\omega\gamma\eta\)): repeated application carries one from particulars to universals to first principles. The grading \(\deg\) is the categorial hierarchy: \(\deg(\text{substance}) > \deg(\text{quantity}), \deg(\text{quality}) > \cdots\), with substance topmost because every other category presupposes it.

\[ \eta(a \circ m) = a, \qquad \sigma(a) = 0 \iff a \text{ is in pure act}, \qquad \omega^n(\text{particulars}) \xrightarrow{n\to\infty} \text{first principles} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that predication is the monoid operation. Aristotle's logic depends on the legitimacy of repeatedly attaching predicates to a subject without losing track of the subject; this is exactly the associativity and identity behavior of \(\circ\). The categories are then a typing discipline that restricts which compositions are well-formed. This is a T1 claim about stable composition: the monoid laws underwrite the very possibility of demonstrative science.

The second claim is that abstraction is a retraction. The map \(\eta\) carries a hylomorphic composite onto its formal component, and applying \(\eta\) again returns the same form — abstraction does not have a residue to abstract from twice. This is a T6 claim: reframing changes presentation while preserving the underlying intelligible structure, and the kernel of \(\eta\) is precisely matter.

The third claim is that the categorial scheme is a genuine grading. Substance, quantity, quality, and the rest are not merely a list but a hierarchy in which substance is logically prior, and every other category is a modification of it. Formally, \(\deg\) sorts \({\mathcal I}\) into ten levels with substance at the top, and every legitimate predication moves within a level or descends to a more dependent one. This is a T9 claim about hierarchy and rank.

The fourth claim concerns the unmoved mover. The identity \(e\) is identified with pure act — the only entity for which \(\sigma(e) = 0\) and which has no privation, \(\nu(e) = e\). This is a T1 claim of unusual strength: not merely "there is an identity element" but "the identity element is metaphysically unique and is the cause of motion in everything else." The eternal contemplation of the unmoved mover thus appears in the algebra as the unique fixed point of every operation simultaneously.

The fifth claim is that induction is a transmission chain. \(\omega\) carries the soul from sense perception of particulars upward to the universal, then to the principles. For Aristotle this is a real psychological and biological process, not a merely logical one. As a T7 claim, it asserts that the iterated application of \(\omega\) along the chain of cognitive acts converges on first principles, which then function as fixed points stable under further inductive pressure.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the formalism makes explicit that the form–matter distinction is the kernel–image decomposition of a single map \(\eta\). Aristotle treats form and matter as ontologically primitive; the algebra reveals them as two faces of one operation. This is a clarification rather than a revision: it shows that hylomorphism is structurally what one would expect any retraction to look like in a theory of composite ideas.

Second, the curvature \(\sigma\) gives a quantitative reading to the potency/act distinction. Aristotle's own discussions are qualitative; \(\sigma\) lets one ask which substances have how much unactualized potential, and how that potential is distributed across the dimensions of change (qualitative, quantitative, locomotive, generative). Biology, embryology, and developmental teleology can then be re-cast as the study of how \(\sigma\) decreases over the life of a substance.

Third, the resonance \(\rho\) makes the logic of natural kinds precise. Aristotle insists that biology must respect species and genera, but his criteria are sometimes informal; \(\rho\) supplies a similarity measure that respects formal essence and ignores accidental matter. The taxonomy of the History of Animals can then be reconstructed as a clustering with respect to \(\rho\), with predictions about which species cluster more tightly than others.

Fourth, the formalism provides a clean account of the category mistake. A category mistake is not a falsehood but an ill-typed composition: an attempt to apply \(\circ\) across categories where the operation is undefined. This explains why category mistakes feel different from ordinary errors — they fail at the syntactic level, before any semantic question can be asked — and connects Aristotle's categorial logic to modern type-theoretic concerns.

Fifth, identifying the unmoved mover with the algebraic identity \(e\) clarifies its peculiar role. The unmoved mover is not a substance like other substances; it is the unit of the entire composition. Aristotle is famously embarrassed by the question of whether the unmoved mover is "one or many"; the formalism answers cleanly that the unit of a monoid is unique, and that any plurality of unmoved movers (as in Metaphysics Lambda) must therefore be reread as a plurality of operations sharing one unit.

Where It Diverges

First, Aristotle's syllogistic is far richer than monoid composition. The figures and moods of the syllogism encode constraints on quantification and distribution that no associative binary operation can capture. The formalism preserves the predicative skeleton but loses the inferential machinery; an algebraic Aristotle is a logically impoverished Aristotle.

Second, the four causes are not four operations on \({\mathcal I}\) but four kinds of explanatory question that any substance can answer. The formalism collapses material and formal causes into the kernel/image of \(\eta\), but efficient and final causes have no natural algebraic home. Teleology in particular — the irreducibility of the final cause — resists the formalism's neutral, non-purposive composition.

Third, the act/potency distinction is dynamic in a way \(\sigma\) cannot fully render. Potency is not just a measure of unactualized possibility; it is a directional, end-oriented possibility — the acorn's potency is for oak, not for fern. The formalism can record \(\sigma\) as a magnitude but not as a vector pointing toward a specific actuality, and so loses the teleological content of Aristotelian potency.

Fourth, Aristotle's biology presupposes that natural kinds carve the world at its joints. The formalism's resonance \(\rho\) is continuous and admits intermediate values, which fits modern phylogenetics better than it fits Aristotle's own essentialism. To recover strict essentialism one must threshold \(\rho\), and the choice of threshold has no principled algebraic motivation.

Fifth, the unmoved mover is supposed to cause motion without itself moving, by being the object of desire and contemplation. The algebraic identity \(e\) is causally inert — it leaves things unchanged when composed with them, but it does not pull them toward itself. Capturing the eros of the cosmos for the unmoved mover would require equipping \({\mathcal I}\) with an additional gradient structure that the bare monoid does not provide.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Aristotle's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the ordered field of explanatory contexts: logical predication, natural change, biological function, ethical practice, and first philosophy. Morphisms translate from more concrete inquiries to more formal causes, preserving hylomorphic structure while changing which cause is salient. λ matters because potentiality is the colimit of realisations under future refinements; τ orders motion from potency to act, and δ distinguishes substances by their differentiae within genera.

This is T13 in explanatory mode: knowledge of a substance glues local causal accounts—material, formal, efficient, and final—into one intelligible whole.

Plotinus

204–270 CE · Neoplatonist

The Position

Plotinus reorganizes the Platonic inheritance into a strict hierarchy of three hypostases. At the summit is the One (\(\tau\dot o \tilde\varepsilon\nu\)), absolute simplicity, beyond being and predication; from the One overflows Nous (Intellect), the realm of Forms thinking themselves; from Nous overflows Soul, the principle of life and motion; and from Soul, finally, comes matter, the formless limit of emanation.

The whole structure is generated by procession (\(\pi\rho\dot o o\delta o\varsigma\)), a non-temporal overflowing in which each higher level gives rise to the next without being diminished. The One does not "decide" to create; emanation is metaphysically necessary, like light from the sun. Each lower level is a less unified, more dispersed image of the level above it.

Procession is mirrored by return (\(\dot\varepsilon\pi\iota\sigma\tau\rho o\phi\dot\eta\)). Every emanated being, by the very fact that it is an image, contains within itself a desire to return to its source. The soul's spiritual life, the philosopher's contemplation, and the mystic's union are all moments of this return — a journey upward against the current of emanation.

The One itself is unsayable. Plotinus is consistent: any predicate applied to the One falsifies it, because predication implies multiplicity (subject and predicate, knower and known). One can only say what the One is not, and approach it through negation. The famous "flight of the alone to the alone" is what lies past the last predicate.

This architecture decisively shaped late antique and medieval thought. Augustine, Pseudo-Dionysius, Eriugena, and the Islamic Neoplatonists all inherit Plotinus's basic move: a tiered cosmos, a downward emanation, an upward return, and an apex beyond speech. The dialectic of procession and return becomes the master rhythm of Western mysticism.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the union of ideas at each hypostatic level — Nous-ideas (Forms thinking themselves), Soul-ideas (intentional acts), and Matter-ideas (privative limits) — together with a formal symbol \(e\) standing in for the One at the boundary of the algebra. The element \(e\) is honorary: it is the would-be identity, but Plotinus's own doctrine forbids it from being a genuine member, so the algebra is best understood as having \(e\) only as a limit point.

Composition \(\circ\) is emanation–participation: \(a \circ b\) is the lower idea \(b\) emanating from or participating in the higher idea \(a\). Composition is strictly downward in \(\deg\): \(\deg(a \circ b) \leq \min(\deg(a),\deg(b))\), reflecting the dispersive character of procession. There is no way to compose two lower ideas to recover a higher one — emanation is irreversible from inside the algebra.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) measures ontological proximity to the One: \(\rho(a,b)\) is high when \(a\) and \(b\) live near the same hypostasis, and approaches its maximum as both approach \(e\). Curvature \(\sigma\) records the directional tension between procession and return: \(\sigma\) is positive in the procession direction (entropy of being), negative in the return direction (concentration toward the One), and crosses zero at the moment of mystical union.

Negation \(\nu\) is the casting of an idea downward as a shadow: \(\nu(a)\) is the image of \(a\) at the next-lower hypostasis. Iterating \(\nu\) produces the descent into matter; \(\nu^n(\text{Form}) \to \text{Matter}\) as \(n \to \infty\). Reframing \(\eta\) is the soul's return: \(\eta\) carries each idea upward, with the One as its limit. Iterated, \(\eta^n(a) \to e\) for every \(a\), but the limit is achieved only at infinity — there is no finite path to the One.

Transmission \(\omega\) is emanation considered as a propagating process: \(\omega\) acts on \(e\) (treated as a boundary input) and produces successive layers of being. The grading \(\deg\) is the hypostatic level, with \(\deg(e) = +\infty\) (or undefined), \(\deg(\text{Nous}) = 3\), \(\deg(\text{Soul}) = 2\), \(\deg(\text{Matter}) = 1\), and \(\deg(\text{privation}) = 0\).

\[ \omega^n(e) \xrightarrow{n\to\infty} \text{Matter}, \qquad \eta^n(a) \xrightarrow{n\to\infty} e_{\text{One}}, \qquad \sigma > 0 \text{ on procession}, \quad \sigma < 0 \text{ on return} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that emanation is iterated transmission. Each hypostasis arises from the previous one by a single application of \(\omega\), so the cosmos is the orbit of \(e\) under \(\omega\). This is a T7 claim: the entire ontological hierarchy is the trajectory of one operator applied to one boundary input, and the structure of the cosmos is therefore the structure of this trajectory.

The second claim is that return is iterated reframing converging on the identity. The mystical ascent is not an exotic exception to ordinary cognition; it is what \(\eta\), iterated indefinitely, must produce. This is a T6 claim of unusual strength: reframing not only changes presentation but, when iterated without limit, converges to the unique fixed point that is the source of all presentation.

The third claim is that the grading \(\deg\) strictly decreases under \(\omega\) and strictly increases under \(\eta\). The hypostases are not arbitrarily ordered; they are forced by the action of these operators. This is a T9 claim: hierarchy is not a layer added on top of the algebra but an intrinsic consequence of the operators that build it.

The fourth claim concerns the curvature. Procession and return are not symmetric — they have opposite signs of \(\sigma\). This is a T5 claim about asymmetry: there is a directional tension built into the cosmos, and the entire spiritual life is the soul's effort to reverse the sign of \(\sigma\) on its own trajectory.

The fifth claim is that the One, although not a member of \({\mathcal I}\), is recoverable as the limit of every \(\eta\)-orbit. Mystical union is a real phenomenon precisely because the algebra has a unique attractor under \(\eta\). This is a T1-shaped claim about identity, but with the twist that the identity is not in the algebra; it is the boundary the algebra approaches but never includes.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the formalism makes mystical convergence a theorem rather than a testimony. Plotinus reports the experience of union; the algebra explains why union is structurally inevitable for any sufficiently iterated \(\eta\)-orbit. This dignifies the mystic's report as an empirical observation of an algebraic limit, rather than dismissing it as private revelation.

Second, the curvature distinguishes procession from return with a single sign. Plotinus has to use elaborate metaphors (light, overflow, mirror) to keep the two directions apart; the algebra does it with the sign of \(\sigma\), and so unifies a multitude of metaphors into one geometric fact about the same operator running forward or backward.

Third, treating the One as a limit point rather than a member resolves a long-standing exegetical difficulty. Commentators argue endlessly about whether the One "exists" or "is beyond existence." The formalism cleanly distinguishes membership (no) from limit-status (yes), and so preserves Plotinus's apophatic insistence without reducing the One to mere nothing.

Fourth, the formalism predicts the existence of intermediate hypostases that Plotinus does not explicitly recognize. If \(\omega\) acts continuously, then between any two hypostases there must be a continuum of intermediate states. Later Neoplatonists (Iamblichus, Proclus) elaborated exactly such intermediates; the algebra retroactively justifies their elaborations as filling in a continuum that Plotinus's discrete hypostases only sampled.

Fifth, the operator \(\nu\) gives a precise account of why matter is "evil" without making evil substantial. Matter is not a positive being competing with the Good; it is the limit of \(\nu\), the asymptotic shadow at the bottom of the descent. The formalism therefore vindicates the privation theory of evil without metaphysical contortions.

Where It Diverges

First, the One is not a member of \({\mathcal I}\) at all, and the formalism's habit of treating identity as a member must be modified. Any algebraist used to having \(e \in {\mathcal I}\) will instinctively try to write equations involving the One; Plotinus forbids exactly this, and the formalism only respects the prohibition by adding boundary structure ad hoc.

Second, emanation in Plotinus is non-temporal and non-decremental: the higher hypostasis is not diminished by giving rise to the lower one. The algebraic \(\omega\) typically does decrement something (degree, complexity, energy); preserving the "no diminution" doctrine requires extra care that the formalism does not naturally encode.

Third, the experiential character of return is largely lost. \(\eta\) describes the structural fact that an idea moves toward higher unity, but it does not capture the affective texture — purification, ecstasy, the dark night — that Plotinus and his successors take to be essential. The algebra is a skeleton of the spiritual life, not its flesh.

Fourth, the apophatic tradition that Plotinus inaugurates insists that one must unsay as well as say. The formalism is monotone in its expressivity: once an equation is written, it stays written. There is no algebraic operator for "and now retract this very statement," which is exactly the move Pseudo-Dionysius and the later mystics demand.

Fifth, the role of love and beauty in driving the return is structural in Plotinus but invisible in the algebra. He insists that the soul is drawn upward by eros, by recognition of the beautiful; the algebra provides a gradient (\(\sigma\)) but not an attraction. Distinguishing "the soul moves upward because the curvature pushes it" from "the soul moves upward because it loves the One" requires a theological supplement the formalism does not contain.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Plotinus' \(\mathcal{H}\) is an emanative hierarchy: the One, Intellect, Soul, and the sensible world form descending horizons of articulation. Morphisms downward are processions that multiply unity into determinate forms; morphisms upward are contemplative returns that restrict multiplicity toward its source. χ marks hypostatic levels, ι is the soul's orientation to its intelligible origin, and φ is the eros-like ascent of \(\deg\) toward unity.

This is T15: the One is not an item inside a horizon but the limiting stability whose support is presupposed by every lower horizon's intelligibility.

Augustine of Hippo

354–430 CE · Christian Platonist

The Position

Augustine adapts the Platonic doctrine of Forms to the requirements of Christian creation. The Forms do not float in an independent intelligible heaven; they are ideas in the mind of God, the eternal archetypes by reference to which God creates and sustains the world. Creation is the externalization of these divine ideas in time and matter.

Human knowledge of intelligible truth is therefore not anamnesis but illumination. The mind does not recall what it knew in a previous life; it sees what God shows it now, as the eye sees by the light of the sun. The "interior teacher" — Christ as Logos — is what makes intelligible content available to the soul; without illumination, the soul could rummage among sense-impressions forever and never recover a genuine universal.

The corresponding spiritual movement is interiority. "Do not go outside; return into yourself; in the inner man dwells truth." Augustine's Confessions is the literary form of this turn: a relentless interrogation of the self that uncovers, beneath the layers of habit and disordered love, the trace of God in the imago Dei. The inward turn is at the same time an upward turn.

Time, memory, and language receive sustained algebraic-looking treatments. Time is a distention of the soul; memory is the site of the imago Dei; language is at once a means of transmission and a perpetual reminder of fallen multiplicity. The doctrine of original sin, by contrast, asserts a pervasive distortion of the will that intellect alone cannot correct: grace is needed.

The mature theology insists that the Trinity is not just a doctrine but a structural feature of every created being — every thing bears traces (vestigia) of its triune source: being, intelligibility, and love; or memory, understanding, and will in the soul. Augustine's late work effectively reads created order as a vast resonance with the inner life of God.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of ideas, partitioned into divine ideas (the eternal archetypes in God's mind) and created ideas (their temporal images in souls and things). The identity \(e\) is interpreted as God himself, the source whose mind contains the divine ideas; \(e\) is more than a unit, since it is the generator of the rest of \({\mathcal I}\), but its monoid behavior — leaving everything unchanged on composition — captures God's role as the "in whom we live and move and have our being."

Composition \(\circ\) is creative participation: \(a \circ b\) is the created instance arising when divine archetype \(a\) is participated by creature \(b\). Resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) measures analogical distance from God; \(\rho(a,e)\) is the principal axis of the algebra, with the soul's destiny being to maximize it.

Curvature \(\sigma\) records the discontinuity between temporal and eternal. Created things live in time, where \(\sigma > 0\) (they are in motion, distended, becoming); divine ideas live in eternity, where \(\sigma = 0\). The discrepancy is not bridged by any continuous operation; it requires the descent of grace, which the formalism cannot directly model and must encode as an external input.

Negation \(\nu\) is the fall: \(\nu(a)\) is the disordered image of \(a\) under fallen will, the privative deformation that arises when love is misdirected. Reframing \(\eta\) is illumination, the interior teacher's act of presenting an idea to the mind in its true light; \(\eta\) is a retraction onto the imago Dei in the soul.

Transmission \(\omega\) is providential ordering: God's continuous arrangement of created beings toward their proper end. \(\deg\) is the degree of participation in divine being, with \(\deg(e) = \infty\), \(\deg(\text{angel}) > \deg(\text{soul}) > \deg(\text{body}) > \deg(\text{matter})\), and \(\deg(\text{nothing}) = 0\) for pure privation.

\[ \rho(a, e_{\text{God}}) \;\geq\; \rho(a, b) \quad \forall b \in {\mathcal I}, \qquad \eta = \text{retraction onto imago Dei} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that God is the global resonance maximum. No created idea can resonate with the soul more deeply than its source does, because every other resonance is derivative. This is a T2 claim: the genuine optimum of \(\rho\) is unique, and the soul's restlessness ("our heart is restless until it rests in thee") is the algebraic fact that any local maximum short of \(e\) is unstable.

The second claim is that illumination is a conjugation that aligns human ideas with divine archetypes. \(\eta\) does not produce content; it presents content already implicit in the imago Dei in a form the soul can recognize. This is a T6 claim: reframing under \(\eta\) preserves structural content while making it cognitively available, and the kernel of \(\eta\) is exactly the contribution of fallen distortion.

The third claim is that providence is a transmission ordering creation toward its end. \(\omega\) carries each idea toward its God-given telos; iterated, it moves the entire created order toward the eschatological consummation. This is a T7 claim about transmission chains: history is a single, providentially ordered orbit under \(\omega\).

The fourth claim concerns the imago Dei. Augustine's analysis of memory–understanding–will as a created trace of the Trinity becomes the assertion that the soul has the same internal structure as \(e\), at lower \(\deg\). This is a T8 claim of structural equivalence: under conjugation by \(\eta\), the soul and God exhibit the same triadic pattern.

The fifth claim is that original sin is a global distortion of \(\nu\). Without sin, \(\nu\) would be a clean involution (love and its proper object); with sin, \(\nu\) becomes systematically misdirected, so that the soul's negations land elsewhere than where they should. This is a T5 claim about asymmetry: the fall is not the addition of new content but the bending of the algebra's existing operator into a non-recoverable shape.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the formalism makes the doctrine of divine ideas operationally precise. To say that the Forms are "in the mind of God" is to say that they are generated by \(e\) under the algebra's own operations, rather than enjoying an independent existence. This dissolves a long-standing puzzle about whether divine ideas compromise divine simplicity — they do not, because they are derived rather than added.

Second, the priority of interiority becomes the assertion that \(\eta\) is a retraction onto an internal subalgebra. Augustine's "return into yourself" is not advice to navel-gaze; it is the recognition that the genuine fixed points of \(\eta\) lie inside the soul, in the imago Dei, and not in external sense-impressions.

Third, the analogical distance from God acquires a measure. Augustine constantly speaks of degrees of being, of likeness and unlikeness to God; \(\rho\) gives these a quantitative reading. One can in principle ask which created beings are closer to God than which others, and the answers are no longer impressionistic.

Fourth, the relation between time and eternity is captured by the curvature jump. Time is distention; eternity is concentration; the discontinuity between them is exactly where \(\sigma\) is undefined or singular. Augustine's celebrated meditations on time in Confessions XI become structural observations about a singular locus in the algebra.

Fifth, the trinitarian structure of every created being — vestigia trinitatis — is given precise expression as a recurring triadic motif under \(\eta\). The soul's memory–understanding–will, the world's being–intelligibility–love, and the Trinity itself appear as conjugate copies of one structural pattern, vindicating Augustine's analogical method.

Where It Diverges

First, the formalism has no operator for grace. Grace is, by Augustine's own insistence, not part of nature; it is the gratuitous, asymmetric act of God reaching into the algebra from outside. Any attempt to render grace as an internal operation \(g: {\mathcal I} \to {\mathcal I}\) Pelagianizes the doctrine by making it intrinsic.

Second, original sin is more than a misdirection of \(\nu\); it is a hereditary, communal, and historically inaugurated condition transmitted through generation. The algebra has no notion of biological lineage or shared guilt; it can only encode sin as a per-element distortion, missing the corporate dimension Augustine insists on.

Third, the asymmetry of divine love — God loves first, and our love is always response — has no algebraic mirror. The algebra is symmetric in its operations; the radical asymmetry of caritas requires an external direction that the formalism cannot supply without external annotation.

Fourth, the personal and confessional character of Augustine's theology is opaque to the algebra. Augustine speaks to God in the second person; the algebra knows only third-person facts about elements and operations. The vocative dimension — the soul addressing the source — is lost.

Fifth, the eschatological future, the resurrection of the body, and the beatific vision are end-states the algebra can gesture at as limits but cannot fully model. The two cities of De Civitate Dei are sustained by loves directed at incompatible ends; encoding "love" as a directional gradient requires structure beyond the bare operations \((\circ,\rho,\sigma,\nu,\eta,\omega,\deg)\).

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Augustine's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the drama of temporal, inward, and divine horizons: memory, present attention, expectation, fallen history, and eternal illumination. Morphisms are conversions of attention, especially the turn from exterior signs to the inner teacher and from temporal dispersion to God's eternity. τ is central for distentio animi, ι for the soul's restless directedness toward God, and R for confessional self-reading before a divine witness.

This is T13: understanding Scripture, self, and creation requires gluing fragmentary temporal signs into the illuminated horizon where their unity becomes readable.

Thomas Aquinas

1225–1274 · Scholastic

The Position

Aquinas's distinctive metaphysical move is the real distinction between essence (essentia) and act of being (esse). For every creature, what it is and that it is are genuinely distinct: the essence specifies what kind of being a thing would be, but it requires an additional, ontologically distinct act of existence to be at all. Only in God do essence and existence coincide; God's essence simply is to-be (ipsum esse subsistens).

This distinction supports a metaphysics of participation. Creatures have being; God is being. Creatures participate in esse as receiving from a source; God communicates esse as the source. The chain of causes therefore terminates in a being whose existence is not received, and this is the metaphysical core of the Five Ways: each begins from some feature of finite being and traces it back to a non-derivative ground.

Mediating the distance between God and creatures is the analogy of being (analogia entis). Univocal predication would collapse God into the creaturely order; equivocal predication would make God-talk meaningless. Analogy is the middle way: the same term applies to God and to creatures with related but proportionally distinct meanings, and so allows substantive theological speech without ontological confusion.

Epistemologically, Aquinas accepts the Aristotelian doctrine that all knowledge begins in sense and that the intellect abstracts universals from phantasms. He combines this with the Augustinian insistence that the natural light of intellect is itself a participation in the divine light. The result is a careful integration: revelation completes but does not replace natural reason, and theology proceeds scientifically from articles of faith treated as first principles.

The whole system is articulated through an architectonic of acts, habits, virtues, ends, and laws. Human action is ordered to a final end (beatitude), the virtues are stable dispositions toward that end, the natural law is reason's participation in eternal law, and grace elevates nature without destroying it. The Summa is the cathedral-like display of this single ordered structure.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of beings, each represented as a pair (essence, esse-degree), with \(e\) interpreted as ipsum esse subsistens: the unique being whose essence-component is empty (no limiting essence) and whose esse-component is maximal. Every other element of \({\mathcal I}\) has a non-trivial essence that limits its participation in being.

Composition \(\circ\) is participation: \(a \circ b\) is the resulting being when essence \(a\) receives esse from source \(b\). The asymmetry — essence is recipient, esse is given — yields a non-commutative monoid in which composition with \(e\) on the right strictly raises the esse-content while leaving the essence intact. This is Aquinas's "creation" considered as an algebraic operation.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is analogical proportion: \(\rho(a,b) = \deg(a)/\deg(b)\) when both are non-zero, encoding the proportionate relation between two participated beings. \(\rho(a,e)\) measures a creature's likeness to God, and is bounded above by 1, reflecting the unbridgeable proportionate distance between any creature and its source.

Curvature \(\sigma\) records the act–potency tension: \(\sigma(a)\) is high when essence \(a\) has much unrealized potential (most creatures, most of the time) and zero only at \(e\). Negation \(\nu(a)\) is privation: the absence of being proper to a given essence (e.g., blindness as privation of sight in an animal whose essence includes sight).

Reframing \(\eta\) is analogical predication: \(\eta\) carries a creaturely term to its analogically corresponding divine term, preserving structure while shifting reference. Transmission \(\omega\) is causal dependence: \(\omega\) propagates being and form from primary cause through secondary causes. The grading \(\deg\) is degree of actuality: \(\deg(e) = \infty\) (pure act), descending through angels, rational souls, sentient life, vegetative life, mixed bodies, elements, prime matter \((\deg = 0)\).

\[ \rho(a, b) = \frac{\deg(a)}{\deg(b)}, \qquad \deg(e) = \sup_a \deg(a), \qquad \omega(a) = a \circ e \;\Longrightarrow\; \deg(\omega(a)) > \deg(a) \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that the Five Ways collectively demonstrate that \(\deg\) attains a unique maximum, and that this maximum is the identity \(e\). Each Way exhibits some chain (motion, efficient causation, contingency, gradation, finality) that cannot be infinite if it is to ground the phenomena, and so terminates in a non-derivative element. This is a T9-and-T1 claim: the grading has a top, and the top is the unit of the algebra.

The second claim is that analogical predication preserves partial structure while admitting difference. \(\eta\) is not identity (univocity) and not noise (equivocity); it is a structure-preserving map between subalgebras of different \(\deg\). This is a T6 claim: reframing changes presentation, but in a way that respects the operational signature of the source domain.

The third claim is that the act–potency distinction is captured by non-trivial \(\sigma\). Every creature exhibits some gap between what it is and what it could be; only \(e\) closes the gap. This is a T5 claim about asymmetry: the directional tension toward actualization is built into every non-divine element of \({\mathcal I}\).

The fourth claim is that secondary causation is genuine but derivative. \(\omega\) factors through \(e\) — every transmission of being is ultimately God's, but creatures are real causes of their own effects under \(\omega\). This is a T7 claim about transmission chains, with the additional structure that all chains pass through a common origin.

The fifth claim is that the natural law is a fixed point of \(\eta\) in the practical domain. The eternal law in God, when conjugated through human reason, yields the natural law; conjugated again through positive promulgation, yields human law. Each step of \(\eta\) preserves practical normativity while changing presentation, and the fixed point of the iteration is the eternal law itself.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the formalism makes the analogy of being a precise metric. Aquinas's discussions of analogy of attribution and analogy of proportionality are notoriously slippery; \(\rho\) gives a definite numerical reading that lets one compare different analogical predications and ask which preserves more structure.

Second, the compatibility of faith and reason becomes the assertion that \(\eta\) (theological reframing) and \(\circ\) (philosophical composition) commute. Where they fail to commute, one has either heresy (theology overreaching) or rationalism (philosophy overreaching); the algebraic frame supplies a precise diagnostic for these failures.

Third, the real distinction between essence and esse appears as the decomposition of every element into an essence-part and a degree-part. The formalism does not collapse them; it explicitly factors every \(a \in {\mathcal I}\) along these two axes, vindicating the metaphysical insistence that the distinction is real and not merely conceptual.

Fourth, the doctrine of secondary causes — that creatures are genuine causes although God is the primary cause — is rendered as the fact that \(\omega\) factors through \(e\) without thereby making creaturely transmissions illusory. Occasionalism is excluded: secondary causes do real work, even though their efficacy is sustained by the primary cause.

Fifth, the natural law tradition acquires algebraic backing. Reading natural law as a fixed point of \(\eta\) in the practical domain explains why natural law is both knowable by reason (it is in \({\mathcal I}\)) and binding independently of human choice (it is a fixed point, not a freely chosen element).

Where It Diverges

First, the real distinction between esse and essentia is more than a factorization in a fixed algebra; for Aquinas it is a genuine metaphysical composition with its own causal structure. The formalism captures the factorization but not the dynamic by which essence "limits" esse — a process Aquinas treats as a genuine constituent of every finite being.

Second, divine simplicity forbids any composition in God whatsoever, including the algebraic identification \(e = (\text{empty essence}, \infty)\). The pair-decomposition is itself a composition, and applying it to God is precisely what Aquinas's doctrine forbids. The formalism normalizes God into the same factorization scheme as creatures, against the doctrine.

Third, analogy in the strict Thomistic sense is not merely a metric but a semantic relation that depends on actual ontological participation, not just structural similarity. \(\rho\) records similarity; analogy proper requires that the similarity be grounded in causal derivation. The formalism can correlate but cannot enforce this grounding.

Fourth, the doctrine of grace as elevating nature beyond its proper end has no algebraic encoding. The formalism's operations are closed under the algebra; grace, by definition, is the introduction of a supernatural end that nature could not reach by its own resources.

Fifth, the sacramental and ecclesial dimensions of Aquinas's theology — the way grace is communicated through visible signs, the structure of the Church as Body of Christ — require an embodied, historical, communal carrier that the bare algebra does not contain. Treating the sacraments as merely symbolic carriers of \(\omega\) underdescribes their causal efficacy in the Thomistic account.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Aquinas' \(\mathcal{H}\) is the analogical order of nature, grace, reason, revelation, and beatific vision. Morphisms translate creaturely predicates toward God by analogy rather than univocity, preserving causal dependence while altering semantic mode. λ matters for potency and act, ι for intellect's orientation to being, and χ for the coordinated but distinct horizons of philosophy and sacred doctrine.

This is T13: the Summa-like synthesis glues local truths from Aristotelian science, metaphysics, and revelation into a single analogically ordered account.

William of Ockham

1287–1347 · Nominalist

The Position

Ockham denies that universals have any extra-mental existence. Only individuals are real. The "common nature" that scholastic realists posited as somehow shared between many particulars is, on Ockham's view, a confused projection of mental terms onto the world. There are many men, but no Mankind; many trees, but no Tree-in-itself.

Universals are signs. A concept is a natural sign of the individuals it stands for; a spoken word is a conventional sign of the concept; a written word is a conventional sign of the spoken word. The relation of signification is wholly semantic, not ontological: the concept "human" picks out each individual human, but does not pick out an additional intelligible entity called Humanity.

Ockham's famous razor (entia non sunt multiplicanda praeter necessitatem) is the methodological corollary. If individuals plus their natural similarities suffice to explain the appearances, no additional universals need be posited. Realist commitments to common natures, formalities, and the rest of the late scholastic furniture are unnecessary multiplications.

The logic that supports this metaphysics is the theory of suppositio: the doctrine of how terms stand for things in different propositional contexts. The same term "man" can supposit personally (for individual men), simply (for the concept), or materially (for the spoken or written word). Logical analysis is the disambiguation of suppositio across contexts, and once it is done, ontological extravagance dissolves.

Theologically, Ockham combines this minimalist ontology with a maximalist account of divine power. God's omnipotence is constrained only by non-contradiction; the order we observe in nature reflects God's free choice (potentia ordinata) but could have been otherwise (potentia absoluta). This makes the world radically contingent, and any necessary connections between distinct existences are denied — anticipating Hume by four centuries.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of individual mental acts — concepts, judgments, propositions formed by a particular mind at a particular time. Each element of \({\mathcal I}\) is fully individual; there are no abstract entities, no shared natures, no universals as members. The identity \(e\) is the empty mental act: the act of considering nothing in particular.

Composition \(\circ\) is propositional synthesis: \(a \circ b\) is the mental act of joining two prior mental acts into a single proposition (subject + predicate, or antecedent + consequent). Composition is well-defined within a single mind at a single time but does not propagate across minds: there is no inter-mental \(\circ\) operation, only conventional sign-relations that approximate it.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is co-occurrence in mental acts: \(\rho\) is high when two terms are habitually combined in propositions, low when they are rarely combined. Crucially, \(\rho\) does not measure any external metaphysical similarity; it measures only the empirical pattern of mental association.

Curvature \(\sigma\) is identically zero.
