This is the algebraic statement of Ockham's Razor: there are no real universals over and above individual mental acts, hence no holonomy, no nontrivial loops in the algebra. Every chain of compositions that returns to its start is contractible to the trivial path. Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the mental act of denying \(a\), an internal operation with no commitment to external negative facts.

Reframing \(\eta\) is suppositio: the same term can stand for individuals, for concepts, or for words depending on context. Transmission \(\omega\) is inference: the propagation of mental commitments through valid syllogistic structure. The grading \(\deg\) is structural complexity: simple terms have \(\deg = 0\), atomic propositions have \(\deg = 1\), compound propositions higher.

\[ \sigma(a) = 0 \;\;\forall a \in {\mathcal I}, \qquad \eta_{\text{ctx}}(t) \in \{\text{personal}, \text{simple}, \text{material}\} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that \(\sigma \equiv 0\). This is the precise algebraic content of the Razor: any apparent curvature in the universe of discourse is an artifact of confused suppositio, removable by careful logical analysis. There are no real universals and therefore no real loops: the algebra is flat.

The second claim is that the apparatus of universals can be recovered as a quotient of \({\mathcal I}\) under \(\rho\)-thresholding without any commitment to mind-independent universals. Mental acts that resonate above a threshold form an equivalence class that functions like a universal in inference but is ontologically nothing more than the class itself. This is a T8 claim: the appearance of structural sameness is real, but the underlying entities are still individual.

The third claim is that \(\eta\) (suppositio) is the only legitimate route to disambiguating apparent universal reference. When realist philosophers think they are tracking a common nature, they are in fact equivocating across the simple and personal supposition of one term. Reframing dissolves the apparent ontological commitment. This is a T6 claim, but with a deflationary twist: reframing reveals that there was nothing structural to preserve.

The fourth claim concerns transmission. \(\omega\) (inference) does propagate commitments along chains, but only between mental acts in the same mind or between conventionally connected mental acts in different minds. There is no impersonal "logical space" in which inference happens; inference is always an act of an individual mind. This is a T7 claim restricted to individual carriers.

The fifth claim is the radical contingency of the lawlike order. There are no necessary connections between distinct existences; God could rearrange the order of nature without contradiction. This is a T5 claim about asymmetry — the world's lawfulness has a direction (the one God in fact ordained) but no algebraic necessity, and the formalism can encode this only by treating laws as freely chosen distributions rather than as theorems.

What the Formalism Adds

First, Ockham's Razor becomes a topological assertion: zero curvature. This makes the parsimonious commitment exactly precise; one can ask whether a given philosophical picture introduces nontrivial curvature, and if so, the Razor demands its removal. Parsimony stops being a vague aesthetic preference and becomes a checkable structural property.

Second, the theory of suppositio becomes a context-dependent typing system on terms. Modern accounts of indexicality and reference shifting can be read as descendants of suppositio; the formalism makes the kinship visible by treating \(\eta\) as a context-sensitive interpretation function in the style Ockham anticipates.

Third, the deflation of universals into \(\rho\)-equivalence classes shows precisely why nominalism is not eliminative. Universals as predicates do real work; they are just classes of individuals, not additional entities. The formalism vindicates Ockham's claim to explain everything realism explains, with strictly fewer entities.

Fourth, the radical contingency of natural order is cleanly separable from the necessity of mathematical and logical truths. The algebraic identities (\(\circ\) is associative, \(\eta\) is a retraction, etc.) are necessary; the particular distribution of beings and laws within the algebra is contingent. This separation matches Ockham's distinction between absolute and ordained power.

Fifth, the formalism makes Ockham an early structuralist. Universals do not exist independently, but the structural relations among individual mental acts — \(\rho\)-clusters, \(\eta\)-classes, \(\omega\)-chains — are real and explanatorily potent. This is the modern doctrine "structure without entities," anticipated in the fourteenth century.

Where It Diverges

First, Ockham's logic is much richer than a flat monoid. He developed sophisticated treatments of syncategorematic terms ("all," "some," "if," "and"), modal operators, and consequence relations that no associative binary operation can encode. The algebra captures the predicative skeleton at the cost of the inferential machinery.

Second, the doctrine of the divine ordained power introduces a counterfactual dimension the formalism does not naturally contain. Ockham wants to say that the laws of nature could have been otherwise, even though they actually are not. The algebra encodes only what is; representing the space of alternative ordinations requires an additional possible-worlds structure.

Third, mental acts in Ockham have an intentional structure — they are about something — that the algebra under-describes. Treating concepts as individual mental acts captures their token nature but not their semantic relation to extra-mental objects, which Ockham still recognizes as basic to cognition.

Fourth, Ockham's theological voluntarism — the priority of will over intellect in God — has no clean algebraic mirror. The formalism's operators are fixed by their structural properties; there is no operation corresponding to "freely choose which operations to perform."

Fifth, the historical reception of Ockham (and the development of the via moderna) emphasized fideist and skeptical implications that the algebra cannot reproduce. Once \(\sigma \equiv 0\) and necessary connections vanish, a great deal of inherited natural theology collapses; the formalism records the collapse but does not exhibit the spiritual seriousness with which Ockham combined nominalism with sincere Christian commitment.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Ockham's \(\mathcal{H}\) is a sparse nominalist field of mental terms, spoken signs, individual things, and theological commitments. Morphisms from language to reality preserve only singular reference and signification, pruning any horizon that posits real universals beyond need. β is weakened to supposition in context, δ separates terms by use rather than shared essences, and μ appears in the probabilistic economy of admissible explanations.

This is T14: universalist inference fails to pull back from scholastic language into the individualist horizon of real beings, exposing universals as artifacts of sign-use.

Medieval

Ibn Sīnā (Avicenna)

980–1037 · Islamic Neoplatonic Aristotelian

The Position

Avicenna distinguishes essence from existence: an essence considered in itself is neutral with respect to its instantiation, and existence is something added to essence by the act of a cause, ultimately the Necessary Existent whose essence is identical with its existence. Universals are common natures considered in the mind, which exist in things only as individuated by matter and accidents. Knowledge proceeds by the agent intellect's illumination, which actualises intelligibles in the receptive human intellect.

The Instantiation

The essence/existence distinction becomes the distinction between an idea \(I\) considered as a determinate type and the act of instantiation \(\rho(I)\) that places it in the order of being. Essences \(I\) live in the algebra; existence is the application of \(\rho\) plus a contextual existence operator that we may notate as \(\mathrm{ex}(I,c)\), reflecting that whether \(I\) is realised in context \(c\) is not determined by \(I\) alone.

The Necessary Existent is modelled as a unique element \(N\) such that \(\mathrm{ex}(N,c)=\top\) for every context \(c\) and such that for every other \(I\) one has \(\mathrm{ex}(I,c)=\top \Rightarrow N \prec_{\mathrm{cause}} I\). All other existents are possible-in-themselves, necessary-through-another, captured by \(\mathrm{ex}(I,c)=\top \Leftrightarrow \exists J:\ J\circ I\) holds and \(J\) is itself caused.

The agent intellect is a global operator \(\mathcal{A}\) that takes a sensible representation \(s\) and yields an intelligible \(\mathcal{A}(s)=I\) such that \(I\) is universal: \(\rho(I)\) covers many particulars, and \(\sigma(I,J)\) tracks essential rather than accidental relations. The receptive intellect is a register that becomes \(I\)-typed on receiving \(\mathcal{A}(s)\).

Common natures are equivalence classes of \(I\) under the relation "shares essential predicates", and the threefold consideration (in itself, in the mind, in things) is captured by three projections of \(I\): \(I\) bare, \(\eta(I,\text{intellect})\), and \(\rho(I)\) in matter.

The hierarchy of separate intelligences is a chain \(N \to \mathcal{A}_1 \to \cdots \to \mathcal{A}_{10}\) where each \(\mathcal{A}_k\) emanates from the prior by \(\circ\) with the contemplation of its source, the last being the agent intellect that supplies forms to the sublunar world.

\[ \mathrm{ex}(I,c)=\top \;\Longleftrightarrow\; \exists J\ (J \circ I)(c)\ \wedge\ N \prec_{\mathrm{cause}}^{*} J,\qquad \mathcal{A}(s)=I \]

Central Claim as Theorem

T-Avicenna-1 (essence/existence). For every \(I \neq N\), \(\mathrm{ex}(I,\cdot)\) is contingent: there exist contexts where \(\mathrm{ex}(I,c)=\bot\) and contexts where \(\mathrm{ex}(I,c)=\top\). Only \(N\) is constant across all contexts.

T-Avicenna-2 (necessity through another). If \(\mathrm{ex}(I,c)=\top\), then there is a chain \(N \to \cdots \to I\) of \(\circ\)-applications terminating at \(N\), and the chain is finite (no infinite causal regress).

T-Avicenna-3 (intelligibility). For every sensible \(s\) presented to a properly disposed intellect, \(\mathcal{A}(s)\) is defined, i.e. abstraction is universally available where the receptive conditions hold.

T-Avicenna-4 (universals in re, post rem, ante rem). The same essence \(I\) admits three modes: \(I\) ante rem (in \(N\)'s causal plan), \(I\) in re (\(\rho(I)\) individuated), \(I\) post rem (\(\eta(I,\text{intellect})\) abstracted), and the three are linked by canonical maps.

T-Avicenna-5 (emanation). The chain \(N \to \mathcal{A}_1 \to \cdots\) is determined by repeated self-contemplation: \(\mathcal{A}_{k+1}=\mathcal{A}_k \circ \mathcal{A}_k\)-restricted, so the entire ladder is generated from \(N\) and \(\circ\).

What the Formalism Adds

It separates "what \(I\) is" from "whether \(I\) is" rigorously, replacing potentially confused medieval debates with two distinct objects: the essence \(I\) and the existence predicate \(\mathrm{ex}\).

The agent intellect becomes a function with a domain and a range, allowing one to ask precisely which sensibles fall under it and what fails when the receptive intellect is malformed.

The threefold consideration of universals becomes three rigorously defined projections rather than a metaphysical mystery, clarifying what is shared across the modes.

Emanation is rendered as iterative composition, so the question "how many separate intellects?" becomes a question about fixed points and termination of \(\circ\) under self-application.

Necessity-through-another is given a graph-theoretic form: an existent is necessary iff it is reachable from \(N\) along causal edges, finitely.

Where It Diverges

Avicenna's existence is not a predicate added to essence in the Fregean sense; the algebra's \(\mathrm{ex}\) risks treating it as such, flattening the act-of-being into a Boolean.

The doctrine of essences as natures considered in themselves, neutral to universality and particularity, resists capture: equivalence classes are still in some context, while Avicennian essence-in-itself is strictly contextless.

Emanation is for Avicenna a metaphysical procession with theological weight; the algebraic ladder is structurally analogous but lacks the descending generosity of being that animates Neoplatonic emanation.

The agent intellect's status as a separate substance — not just a function but an angelic intelligence — is not captured by treating \(\mathcal{A}\) as an operator on the algebra.

The "floating man" thought experiment, in which a person stripped of all sensation still affirms its own existence, points to a self-knowledge that the formalism's contextual \(\mathrm{ex}\) does not naturally accommodate.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Avicenna's \(\mathcal{H}\) orders essence, existence, intellect, imagination, and emanative cosmology. Morphisms translate an essence considered in itself into its instantiation under existence, or from sensible images toward intelligible forms received from the Active Intellect. λ captures possible essence awaiting existential realisation, ι structures intellection, and χ distinguishes the necessary horizon of God from the contingent horizons of creatures.

This is T15: necessary existence is the trans-horizonal stability that no contingent essence has by itself, grounding every lower support of being.

Moses Maimonides

1138–1204 · Jewish Aristotelian, negative theology

The Position

Maimonides argues that positive predicates applied to God are either equivocal or to be taken as negations of privations: to say "God is wise" is to say "God is not ignorant", and even attributes of action (creator, merciful) describe the effects in the world rather than properties intrinsic to the divine essence. The Guide of the Perplexed reconciles Torah with Aristotelian science by re-reading anthropomorphic scripture as accommodation to imaginative intellects, while preserving the createdness of the world against the eternalist Aristotelian proof.

The Instantiation

Let \(G\) be the divine idea. The negative-theology constraint is \(\sigma(G,P)=\bot\) for every positive predicate \(P\) drawn from creaturely contexts: no \(P\) composes with \(G\) to yield a meaningful determination. Predications of \(G\) are admitted only as \(\sigma(G,\neg P)=\top\), denials of privations.

Attributes of action are re-routed: \(\sigma(G,\text{merciful})\) is undefined, but \(\sigma(\rho(G),\text{merciful-effect})\) is well-defined, where \(\rho(G)\) is the world considered as God's act. Theological language refers to \(\rho(G)\), not to \(G\) directly.

Scriptural anthropomorphism is a rewrite system \(R\) on the algebra: surface expressions involving \(G\) and bodily predicates are reduced by \(R\) to their philosophical analogues, with the rewrite indexed by the audience's intellectual capacity (\(\eta(\cdot,\text{vulgar})\) vs.\ \(\eta(\cdot,\text{philosopher})\)).

Creation is the assertion that the universe \(U\) satisfies \(U=\rho(G)\) in a context \(c_0\) that has no temporal predecessor; the eternity-of-the-world thesis would require that \(U\) be present in every \(c\), which Maimonides denies on demonstrative-skeptical grounds.

The intellectual love of God is the limit of human \(\eta(\cdot,\text{intellect})\): the deeper the inferential connection between one's ideas and the order of \(\rho(G)\), the more one approaches the asymptotic state of contemplating \(G\) by way of its effects.

\[ \sigma(G,P)=\bot\ \text{for positive } P;\qquad \sigma(G,\neg P)=\top;\qquad U=\rho(G)\text{ in }c_0 \]

Central Claim as Theorem

T-Maimonides-1 (negative theology). The set of positive predicates compatible with \(G\) is empty; only negations and action-descriptions of \(\rho(G)\) are admissible.

T-Maimonides-2 (equivocation). Any term that applies both to \(G\) and to creatures is equivocal: there is no univocal \(\sigma\)-relation linking the two uses.

T-Maimonides-3 (rewrite of scripture). For every anthropomorphic surface expression \(s\), there is a canonical rewrite \(R(s)\) consistent with negative theology, and the philosophical reading is the fixed point of repeated rewriting.

T-Maimonides-4 (createdness). Demonstrations from physics fail to settle whether \(U\) is eternal or created; absent demonstration, scriptural authority plus philosophical preference for an originating cause selects createdness.

T-Maimonides-5 (perfection by intellection). The maximal human \(\eta\)-state with respect to \(\rho(G)\) is the highest perfection available; no positive contemplation of \(G\)'s essence is possible in this life.

What the Formalism Adds

Negative theology becomes a precise constraint on the predicate algebra rather than a piety: it specifies which formulas are well-formed when \(G\) appears.

The action/essence distinction is operationalised as a redirection of \(\sigma\)-terms from \(G\) to \(\rho(G)\), making clear which propositions are theology proper and which are cosmology.

Esoteric writing becomes audience-indexed \(\eta\): the same \(I\) means different things at different intellectual levels, and the rewrite system formalises the levels.

The agnosticism on eternity vs.\ creation appears as an undecidability result for a class of theorems within the cosmological sub-algebra.

Intellectual love of God becomes a quantifiable approach to a limit, not an emotional state.

Where It Diverges

Maimonidean negative theology is meant to humble the intellect before divine transcendence; the algebraic constraint is purely formal and does not produce humility.

The esoteric/exoteric pedagogy presupposes a community of readers and a tradition of transmission; \(R\) and audience-indexing capture the structure but not the social-historical reality of Jewish learning.

The status of prophecy as the conjunction of perfected intellect, imagination, and moral character is only partially captured by maximal \(\eta\); the imaginative faculty's specific role is missed.

The legal-ritual dimension of Maimonides' thought (the Mishneh Torah) is a parallel project the algebra does not touch: the formalism captures the philosophical Maimonides and not the halakhist.

The reconciliation of reason and revelation has a normative urgency for a community in crisis; the algebra registers the reconciliation as a structural fact and not as a rescue.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Maimonides' \(\mathcal{H}\) is the layered space of Scripture, law, philosophical demonstration, prophetic imagination, and negative theology. Morphisms are disciplined translations from anthropomorphic surface readings to purified intellectual apprehension, constrained by communal practice and esoteric pedagogy. χ separates the vulgar, legal, and philosophical horizons; ι directs intellect toward God while negation prevents positive capture; and R appears in the Guide's reflexive concealment of its own method.

This is T14: divine predicates do not pull back functorially from ordinary language into metaphysics, so true theology is the controlled failure of positive \(\sigma\).

John Duns Scotus

c. 1266–1308 · Subtle Doctor, Franciscan

The Position

Duns Scotus introduces the univocity of being (the concept of being applies in the same sense to God and creatures, though with infinite intensive difference), the formal distinction (less than a real distinction but more than a merely conceptual one), and haecceitas (the "thisness" that individuates a substance beyond its common nature). He prioritises the will over the intellect and grounds moral law in divine command, while preserving substantial intellectual content in moral reasoning.

The Instantiation

Univocity: the concept "being" \(B\) is a single \(I\) such that \(\sigma(B,\text{God})=\sigma(B,\text{creature})=\top\) under the same \(\sigma\)-relation, with the difference recorded by an intensive parameter \(\deg(B,\text{God})=\infty\), \(\deg(B,\text{creature})<\infty\).

Formal distinction: between two aspects \(A,B\) of one entity \(E\), we have \(A \neq B\) in the algebra (so they are not identical concepts) but \(\rho(A)=\rho(B)\) on \(E\) (they are not separately instantiated). The relation \(\bowtie_F\) records this intermediate status.

Haecceitas: each individual \(x\) has a unique idea \(h_x\) such that \(\rho(h_x)=\{x\}\) and \(h_x\) is irreducible to any conjunction of common natures. Individuation is by adding \(h_x\) to the common nature \(C\), giving \(C \circ h_x\) as the complete being.

Voluntarism: the divine will operator \(W_G\) is prior to the divine intellect operator \(K_G\) in determining contingent moral facts: \(W_G(M)=\top\) makes \(M\) obligatory, and \(K_G\) recognises but does not constitute \(M\)'s obligatoriness.

Contingency: a contingent state of affairs \(s\) is one for which \(W_G(s) \in \{\top,\bot\}\) is not fixed by \(K_G\) alone; necessity is the residue where \(W_G\) and \(K_G\) coincide.

\[ \sigma(B,G)=\sigma(B,c)=\top,\ \deg(B,G)=\infty;\quad \rho(C \circ h_x)=\{x\};\quad W_G \prec K_G \text{ on contingents} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

T-Scotus-1 (univocity). There is a concept \(B\) such that \(\sigma(B,\cdot)\) is defined and identical in form across every category of being, with intensive grading by \(\deg\).

T-Scotus-2 (formal distinction). The relation \(\bowtie_F\) is well-defined, irreflexive on identity but reflexive on instantiation, and serves to distinguish (e.g.) the divine attributes without dividing the divine simplicity.

T-Scotus-3 (haecceity). For every individual \(x\), there is a unique \(h_x\) such that \(\rho(h_x)=\{x\}\), and the assignment \(x \mapsto h_x\) is functorial on the category of individuals.

T-Scotus-4 (voluntarism on contingents). For contingent \(s\), the truth-value is determined by \(W_G(s)\); \(K_G\) supplies recognition but not constitution.

T-Scotus-5 (synchronic contingency). A contingent state at time \(t\) is contingent at \(t\) itself, not merely temporally; the algebra's \(W_G\) is not bound to a sequential dependence on prior states.

What the Formalism Adds

It supplies a precise three-tier distinction system (real, formal, conceptual) through the interplay of \(\neq\), \(\bowtie_F\), and \(\rho\)-coincidence.

Univocity is rendered as sameness of \(\sigma\)-form combined with intensive grading, separating semantic and metaphysical questions usually conflated.

Haecceitas is operationalised: the individuator is exhibited as a specific kind of idea with a singleton extension, available for further analysis.

The will/intellect order is captured by an operator-priority relation, replacing vague talk of "primacy" with a specific algebraic claim about which operator fixes which class of facts.

Synchronic contingency receives a clean formulation: contingency is a property of the instantaneous configuration, not derived from temporal alternativeness.

Where It Diverges

The Scotist formal distinction is famously delicate; intensive grading and \(\bowtie_F\) capture the structure but not the metaphysical motivation about preserving simplicity-with-distinction.

Haecceitas is for Scotus a positive entity, not just an indexical \(h_x\); the algebra's individuator is functionally specified and lacks the positive-yet-non-qualitative character Scotus insists on.

Voluntarism risks appearing arbitrary in formalisation; Scotus' actual view is more nuanced (the will is rational and oriented to the good), and the operator-priority does not display this rationality.

The Subtle Doctor's notoriously fine-grained argumentation depends on a scholastic dialectical context the algebra abstracts away, losing the conversation with Aquinas, Henry of Ghent, and the Condemnation of 1277.

The Marian and Franciscan theological commitments shape Scotus' metaphysics; the formal apparatus extracts the metaphysics and silences the theology.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Scotus' \(\mathcal{H}\) links metaphysical, theological, logical, and individuating horizons under the univocity of being. Morphisms preserve the thin concept of being across God and creatures while adding modal and haecceitic determinations in local contexts. β is not merely naming but thisness as a constant individuating section, δ differentiates formalities without real separation, and μ enters through the modal space of synchronic alternatives.

This is T15: univocal being is trans-horizonal stability across infinite and finite domains, while haecceity stabilises the individual through every descriptive shift.

Early Modern

René Descartes

1596–1650 · Rationalist

The Position

Descartes inaugurates modern philosophy by demanding that knowledge rest on a foundation immune to doubt. The Meditations stages a methodic doubt that progressively suspends sensory beliefs (which can deceive), mathematical beliefs (which an Evil Demon could falsify), and indeed any belief whose truth is not guaranteed by the very act of thinking it.

The bedrock that survives is the cogito: cogito, ergo sum. So long as I am thinking — even thinking that I am being deceived — I exist as a thinking thing. The cogito is not an inference but a self-presenting truth, indubitable in the very act of its consideration. From it Descartes attempts to reconstruct the rest of knowledge.

The reconstruction proceeds by clear and distinct ideas. Whatever is perceived clearly and distinctly by the intellect alone is true; the trustworthiness of clarity-and-distinctness is itself underwritten by the proof of a non-deceiving God. The Cartesian circle — using clear ideas to prove God and God to validate clear ideas — has occupied commentators for centuries.

Substance dualism follows. The mind is a thinking, unextended substance; the body is an extended, unthinking substance; the two are really distinct and interact through (notoriously) the pineal gland. The mathematical physics of extension can then proceed without psychological residue, while the soul retains a separate ontological standing.

The scientific corollary is the mathematization of nature. Extension is the essence of body; geometry is therefore the language of the physical world; and a long chain of careful deductions, each step clear and distinct, suffices in principle to derive the whole of natural philosophy from a few first principles. Cartesian science is geometric in form even where it is empirical in content.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of clear and distinct ideas, together with their less-than-clear progenitors. Each element of \({\mathcal I}\) is tagged by its degree of clarity-and-distinctness, a real number in \([0,1]\). The identity \(e\) is the cogito itself: the unique idea that is fully clear, fully distinct, and self-presenting under any operation.

Composition \(\circ\) is deductive inference: \(a \circ b\) is the consequence drawn from premises \(a\) and \(b\). Composition preserves clarity when both premises are clear; it can degrade clarity when either premise is obscure. The associative structure of \(\circ\) corresponds to the chains of reasons (longue chaîne de raisons) that the Discourse celebrates as the geometric method.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is mutual entailment strength: how strongly the two ideas support one another in deduction. Curvature \(\sigma\) is the curvature of doubt — the extent to which an idea can be made to waver under skeptical pressure. \(\sigma\) is high for sensory beliefs (much doubt absorbs them) and low for mathematical truths (which return to themselves under doubt), and zero only at the cogito.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the confused, obscure counterpart of \(a\): the same content presented in a way that resists clear apprehension. Reframing \(\eta\) is methodic doubt: an operator that strips an idea down to its clear and distinct core, leaving only what can be reasserted after the demon scenario has been considered.

Transmission \(\omega\) is the chain of reasons: the iterated application of \(\circ\) that carries one from first principles through intermediate theorems to remote conclusions. The grading \(\deg\) is clarity-and-distinctness itself, with \(\deg(e) = 1\) at the cogito and \(\deg = 0\) at fully obscure prejudice.

\[ \eta(a) = \operatorname{cl}(a), \qquad \eta(\text{cogito}) = \text{cogito}, \qquad \deg(\eta(a)) \geq \deg(a) \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that the cogito is the unique fixed point of \(\eta\). Methodic doubt, applied to anything, eventually strips away whatever can be doubted and leaves only what must be presupposed by the doubting itself. This is a T6 claim: reframing converges, and the convergent point is the act of thinking that survives every reframing. Its uniqueness is what underwrites Descartes's foundationalism.

The second claim is that the method of long deduction is \(\omega\) treated as a telescoping chain. Each step in a Cartesian deduction must be clear; the entire chain inherits clarity from the preservation properties of \(\circ\) under \(\omega\). This is a T7 claim about transmission: clarity transmits along well-formed deductive chains and degrades along ill-formed ones.

The third claim is that clarity-and-distinctness is the genuine grading on \({\mathcal I}\). It is not merely a subjective marker; it is the algebraic feature that determines which ideas can serve as foundations and which require derivation. This is a T9 claim: there is a real hierarchy of representations, and Cartesian science is the project of tracing every accepted belief back up to maximal-\(\deg\) sources.

The fourth claim concerns the proofs of God's existence. The ontological argument and the trademark argument both attempt to show that the idea of God is the unique element with maximal \(\deg\) and maximal objective reality, requiring a cause of equal formal reality. This is a T2-and-T9 claim: the resonance of the idea of God with its own content forces a unique extra-mental correlate, and so identifies a second fixed point of \(\eta\) outside the thinker.

The fifth claim is the substance dualism: mind and body are decoupled subalgebras with no overlap in essential attributes. Formally, \({\mathcal I}\) decomposes as \({\mathcal I}_{\text{mind}} \sqcup {\mathcal I}_{\text{body}}\), with \(\circ\) closed within each part. This is a T8 claim of structural non-equivalence: no \(\eta\) carries thought content into extension content, so the two regions are intrinsically distinct.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the systematic doubt becomes a precise retraction map. Where Descartes presents the doubt as a meditative discipline, the formalism shows it as an operator with definite algebraic properties: idempotent, monotone in \(\deg\), and with the cogito as its unique fixed point. This dignifies the meditative procedure as a structural fact about the algebra.

Second, the long chains of reason become \(\omega\)-orbits with monotone \(\deg\). The Cartesian insistence that deduction must proceed without loss of clarity is the algebraic requirement that \(\omega\) be \(\deg\)-non-decreasing on its trajectories. The geometric method is then the constructive search for orbits of this kind.

Third, the Cartesian circle can be diagnosed precisely. The circle arises if and only if the clarity criterion and the divine guarantee depend on each other within a single fixed-point computation. The formalism makes the dependence structure explicit and shows where the Meditations relies on a prior, non-circular argument for clarity (in the cogito itself) before recruiting God's veracity for the rest.

Fourth, the substance dualism becomes a clean algebraic decomposition rather than a mysterious metaphysical postulate. Two subalgebras with disjoint essence-types compose only within themselves. The mind-body interaction problem becomes the question of which (if any) cross-subalgebra operations are admissible.

Fifth, the role of the will in Descartes's account of error becomes the assertion that the will applies \(\circ\) prematurely — composing ideas before their \(\deg\) warrants composition. Error is then not a deficiency of intellect but a misuse of the composition operator, an account that is sharper than Descartes's own but visibly continuous with it.

Where It Diverges

First, the mind-body union — Descartes's late attempt to insist that the soul is "intermingled" with the body, not just associated with it — is not captured by \(\circ\). The real distinction between substances forbids ordinary composition, but the union demands more than mere correlation. The algebra has no operation that is neither composition nor mere co-occurrence.

Second, the role of God in Descartes is far richer than a second fixed point. God grounds the eternal truths, sustains the world by continuous creation, and guarantees the veracity of clear ideas. The algebra captures the fixed-point status but not the sustaining and guaranteeing roles, both of which require an external causal relation to the algebra as a whole.

Third, the Cartesian insistence on innate ideas — ideas the mind possesses prior to all experience — sits uneasily with the formalism's neutrality about how \({\mathcal I}\) is populated. Treating the cogito and the idea of God as innate generators is consistent with the algebra but is not forced by it; one could equally posit empirical origins.

Fourth, the meditative and first-personal character of the Meditations is opaque to the algebra. Descartes addresses the reader as a fellow meditator who must perform the doubt for herself. The algebra encodes only the structural fact of the cogito's fixed-point status, not the experiential gesture of arriving at it.

Fifth, Cartesian physics — the program of deriving extended nature geometrically — failed empirically, and the algebra has no resources for explaining why. The same operator \(\omega\) that delivers the cogito and the existence of God also delivers the vortex theory of planetary motion, and there is no internal criterion in the formalism for distinguishing the successes from the failures.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Descartes' \(\mathcal{H}\) is the sequence of skeptical, cogitative, divine-guarantee, and mathematical-physical horizons. Morphisms are methodic doubts and subsequent restorations: they strip inherited beliefs to the cogito, then re-extend reliable cognition under clear and distinct perception. R is central in the self-certifying cogito, ι marks the mind's ideas as directed contents, and β is sought for clear mathematical essences immune to dream or demon shifts.

This is T15: the cogito is the minimal section whose stability survives every horizon-translation generated by doubt.

John Locke

1632–1704 · Empiricist

The Position

Locke's Essay Concerning Human Understanding opens with a polemical denial of innate ideas. The mind, at birth, is a blank slate (tabula rasa), and all the materials of knowledge come from experience. Experience itself has two springs: sensation, by which external objects supply ideas of color, sound, shape, motion; and reflection, by which the mind observes its own operations of perceiving, doubting, willing, believing.

Ideas come in two grades. Simple ideas are atoms received passively from sensation or reflection — the mind cannot manufacture them, and any deficit in the supply (e.g., congenital blindness) leaves a corresponding gap in possible thought. Complex ideas are built by the mind out of simples, by combining, comparing, or abstracting; here the mind is active.

Among complex ideas, Locke distinguishes ideas of substances, modes, and relations, and develops a famous account of "real" versus "nominal" essence. The real essence of gold is its underlying microstructure, knowable in principle but not by us; the nominal essence is the cluster of observable properties — yellow, malleable, heavy, soluble in aqua regia — by which we sort things into the kind "gold." Sortal terms refer through nominal essences.

Personal identity, on Locke's account, is not the persistence of substance but of consciousness. A person is the same person over time so long as memory connects later states to earlier ones — a position that anticipates psychological continuity theories down to Parfit. The forensic notion of accountability for past acts presupposes this consciousness-based identity.

Politically and epistemically, the same picture supports a moderate liberalism. Knowledge is limited to what experience and reflection provide; opinion fills the gap; toleration is required because no one's opinions enjoy guaranteed authority. The empiricist epistemology and the liberal politics share a single root: humility about what individual minds can claim to know.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the disjoint union of simple ideas (atomic generators) and complex ideas (composites). Each element is tagged by its source (sensation, reflection, or both) and by its complexity. The identity \(e\) is the empty mental state — the slate before any sensation has impressed itself.

Composition \(\circ\) is mental composition: \(a \circ b\) is the complex idea formed by combining \(a\) and \(b\) under one of Locke's three operations (combining, comparing, abstracting). The operation is naturally associative when the combined ideas are compatible types and breaks when one tries to compose a relation with a substance in the same way one composes two simples.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is co-occurrence in experience: ideas that frequently arrive together, or are made to follow each other in reflection, accumulate high \(\rho\). Curvature \(\sigma\) records the curvature of association — the extent to which mental sequences "loop" rather than progressing linearly. Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the idea of the absence or deprivation of \(a\): blindness as the negation of sight, silence of sound.

Reframing \(\eta\) is abstraction in Locke's sense: stripping away the particularity of an idea to form a nominal essence. \(\eta(\text{this gold sample}) = \text{the kind GOLD as defined by observable properties}\). Locke's nominalism about kinds means that \(\eta\) yields a class of properties, not an essence in the metaphysical sense.

Transmission \(\omega\) is habit and association: the repeated co-occurrence of ideas that sets up automatic mental transitions. The grading \(\deg\) is complexity: simple ideas have \(\deg = 0\); complex ideas built from \(n\) simples have \(\deg = n\); ideas of substance, modes, and relations rise through this scale.

\[ a \text{ simple} \;\Rightarrow\; \deg(a) = 0, \qquad \deg(a \circ b) = \deg(a) + \deg(b) + 1, \qquad e = \text{tabula rasa} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that simple ideas are the generators of the free monoid \({\mathcal I}\). Every complex idea decomposes uniquely (up to associativity) into a multiset of simples. This is a T1 claim about composition and identity, with the additional empiricist content that the generators are not pre-installed but supplied by experience over the course of a life.

The second claim is the unique decomposability of complex ideas — Locke's Aufhebung in algebraic form. Given any complex idea, one can in principle trace it back to its simple constituents and the operations by which they were composed. This is a T3 claim: every composite has a canonical decomposition into preserved, negated, and synthesized parts, vindicating the analytical method of the Essay.

The third claim concerns transmission: \(\omega\) (habit) fixes associations over time. Repeated experience strengthens \(\rho\)-bonds between specific ideas until they become automatic, so that the perception of one cues expectation of the other. This is a T7 claim: transmission chains of habit produce stable cognitive dispositions, the empirical analogue of innate connections.

The fourth claim, on personal identity, is that the self is a consciousness-class under \(\rho\). The same person at two different times is the same iff there is a high-\(\rho\) connection (memory) between the two consciousness states. This is a T2-shaped claim about resonance: identity over time is not substance-persistence but resonance-continuity.

The fifth claim, on nominal vs. real essence, is that \(\eta\) (abstraction) generates kinds whose membership is determined by \(\rho\) to a list of properties rather than by underlying microstructure. This is a T6 claim: reframing changes presentation, and Locke's claim is that the human reframe is necessarily nominal — we have no access to real essences.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the tabula rasa becomes the algebraic claim that the initial state of \({\mathcal I}\) has no pre-installed generators. All elements of \({\mathcal I}\) at any time are reachable from \(e\) by composition with empirically supplied simples. The empiricism is then a thesis about the bootstrapping of the algebra, not about any particular content.

Second, T3 supplies a unique decomposition theorem that vindicates Lockean analysis. When Locke proposes to analyze any complex idea down to its simples, the formalism guarantees the analysis terminates and is essentially unique. This grounds the introspective method on a rigorous reduction theorem.

Third, the consciousness-based theory of personal identity becomes a precise quotient construction: persons are equivalence classes under the transitive closure of \(\rho\)-memory connections. This makes possible an immediate engagement with later puzzles about fission, fusion, and partial overlap that Locke himself only gestured at.

Fourth, the nominal/real essence distinction maps onto \(\eta\) (a definable map on \({\mathcal I}\)) versus an ideal real-essence map that is not in the algebra. The limits of Lockean knowledge become the algebraic limits of \(\eta\): we know what we can present, not what underlies presentation.

Fifth, Locke's anticipatory associationism is recovered as the assertion that \(\omega\) is a Markov-like operator on \({\mathcal I}\), with transition weights given by past \(\rho\). This places Locke directly in the line that runs through Hume, Hartley, and modern statistical learning theory.

Where It Diverges

First, the nominal/real essence distinction does not map cleanly to a single \(\deg\). Real essence is not a higher-degree complex; it is a kind of element the algebra cannot contain at all on Locke's epistemology. The formalism handles this only by treating real essence as a placeholder beyond the algebra, which is consistent but extra-structural.

Second, Locke's notorious admission that "substance" is "something I know not what" — a substratum supporting properties but unknowable in itself — resists algebraic treatment. The substratum cannot be represented as a member of \({\mathcal I}\) without contradicting Locke's epistemic strictures, but its absence makes the metaphysics of complex ideas of substance uneasy.

Third, the doctrine of primary and secondary qualities — that some perceived qualities resemble their causes in objects while others do not — requires a representational structure outside the algebra. The algebra records ideas; whether they resemble extra-mental causes is a question the formalism cannot answer from inside.

Fourth, the contractarian and natural-rights political theory built on Locke's epistemology depends on a normative content the algebra does not supply. That ideas of right and obligation are complex ideas formed from sensation and reflection does not yet tell us why they bind; that step requires resources outside the bare combinatorics.

Fifth, Locke's tolerance for divine revelation as a source of knowledge — provided it does not contradict reason — opens a channel into \({\mathcal I}\) that the formalism cannot characterize without additional theological structure. Empirical and revelatory inputs both appear as "given" in the algebra, but they have very different epistemic standings on Locke's own view.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Locke's \(\mathcal{H}\) consists of experiential horizons built from sensation, reflection, simple ideas, complex ideas, and public language. Morphisms compose or abstract from received simple data, but they may lose the real essences that underwrite substances outside the mind. ι marks ideas as signs of qualities, β is fragile because names bind to nominal essences, and μ fits Locke's graded probability where demonstrative knowledge runs out.

This is T14: our nominal classifications do not pull back reliably to real constitutions, so many substance-inferences carry an unavoidable horizon obstruction.

David Hume

1711–1776 · Empiricist/Skeptic

The Position

Hume radicalizes the Lockean program. All the contents of the mind are perceptions, divided into impressions (vivid, immediate) and ideas (faint copies of impressions). The mind possesses no idea that is not derived from a prior impression; the famous test is to demand the impression that any putative idea copies, and to dismiss what fails the demand.

Perceptions are connected by three principles of association: resemblance, contiguity in space and time, and cause and effect. These principles are not deliverances of reason but features of the imagination's spontaneous activity. They function as a "gentle force" that organizes the stream of perception into something like an experienced world.

The most consequential application is to causation. Hume argues that we never observe a necessary connection between cause and effect; we observe constant conjunction, and our minds, by habit, project an expectation of the second on the appearance of the first. The "necessity" of causal connection is a feature of the observer, not of the world.

The same skeptical analysis dissolves the self. When Hume looks inward, he finds only a bundle of perceptions, each itself a fleeting impression, with no enduring substantial self underlying them. Personal identity is a fiction the imagination weaves out of resemblance and contiguity among successive perceptions.

The result is a comprehensive naturalism: causation, induction, the self, modal necessity, moral judgments — all are products of psychological mechanisms operating on perceptions. Reason is "the slave of the passions"; what we call rational belief is in fact custom and feeling, justified pragmatically by their indispensability to common life rather than rationally by their grounding in mind-independent fact.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the disjoint union of impressions and ideas, with impressions tagged by their sensory or reflective source and ideas tagged by the impression they copy. Each element of \({\mathcal I}\) has a vivacity score in \([0,1]\): impressions score high, ideas score lower, and faint memories lower still. The identity \(e\) is the empty perceptual state — but Hume's bundle theory denies that any persisting self instantiates this state.

Composition \(\circ\) is association: \(a \circ b\) is the perception that arises when \(a\) cues \(b\) in the imagination. Because association is a contingent psychological law rather than a logical operation, \(\circ\) is well-defined only modulo an empirical history; the same two impressions may compose differently in different minds with different habits.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is associative strength, decomposable into three weighted components: resemblance, contiguity, and (apparent) causation. Curvature \(\sigma\) is zero for genuinely rational connections — there are essentially none, on Hume's view — and strictly positive for associative habits, which loop perceptions back to expectations.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is best read as the gap analogue of \(a\): the missing perception in the family of which \(a\) is a member. The famous "missing shade of blue" is exactly such a gap — a possible idea not directly copied from any impression. Reframing \(\eta\) is skeptical reduction: the operation of replacing an idea with the impression it copies, refusing to allow ideas with no impressional source.

Transmission \(\omega\) is repeated experience forming habit: \(\omega\) propagates expectations along chains of constantly conjoined perceptions, strengthening \(\rho\) over time. The grading \(\deg\) is vivacity itself, with impressions at the top and faint ideas at the bottom; mental life is largely the redistribution of vivacity across the network of perceptions.

\[ \rho(a,b) = w_{\text{res}} \cdot \text{res}(a,b) + w_{\text{cont}} \cdot \text{cont}(a,b) + w_{\text{caus}} \cdot \text{caus}(a,b), \qquad \eta(a) = \text{impression}(a) \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that there is no rational composition operator connecting cause to effect. The only \(\circ\) that operates between cause-impression and effect-expectation is associative, not deductive. Necessity is a projection of \(\omega\) onto the world. This is a T2 claim: causation is a feature of resonance, not of the underlying ontology.

The second claim is the bundle theory: there is no identity element \(e\) for personal identity. The self is a \(\rho\)-cluster of perceptions, not a substance underlying them. This is a T1 failure for personal identity: the algebra has no member that, composed with any perception, returns that perception unchanged as "mine."

The third claim concerns the "missing shade of blue": there exists an idea-shaped gap in the generator set, fillable by the imagination on the basis of \(\rho\)-extrapolation even though no corresponding impression has been received. This complicates the strict copy principle and makes \({\mathcal I}\) closed under a limited form of interpolation.

The fourth claim is that \(\omega\), iterated by experience, converges on stable habits without ever delivering rational justification. The trajectory of belief-formation is a transmission chain, but its convergence does not vindicate its conclusions. This is a T7 claim with a skeptical edge: stability is not validity.

The fifth claim is that the entire apparatus of moral and aesthetic judgment is downstream of \(\rho\) — sympathy is a kind of resonance that propagates feeling between minds. Moral evaluation is therefore not a perception of mind-independent value but a structural feature of the network of resonant feelings. This is a T2 claim transposed into the practical domain.

What the Formalism Adds

First, Hume's three principles of association become a weighted resonance metric whose weights are empirically tunable. Modern cognitive psychology and machine learning have produced algorithms that look strikingly similar to weighted association; the formalism makes Hume their natural ancestor.

Second, the "missing shade of blue" becomes a precise gap in the generator set: an element interpolatable by \({\mathcal I}\)'s structure but not directly supplied by experience. This formalizes Hume's reluctant concession against the strict copy principle and shows it is a structural concession, not an ad hoc one.

Third, the bundle theory of self acquires a clean algebraic statement: failure of T1 for personal identity. Where rival theories assert an extra "self" element, the bundle theory denies it, and the algebra immediately exhibits the consequences (no rigid persistence, no clear individuation across time).

Fourth, the projection theory of necessity is rendered as a categorical confusion: mistaking a feature of \(\omega\) (the propagation of expectations) for a feature of \(\circ\) (the composition of perceptions). The algebraic frame makes the confusion visible by separating the two operators, where ordinary language collapses them under "must."

Fifth, sympathy as the moral analogue of association maps onto an inter-personal version of \(\rho\), making Humean ethics structurally parallel to Humean epistemology. Both are theories of how \(\rho\)-clusters form, and both terminate in stable patterns whose stability is psychological rather than logical.

Where It Diverges

First, the formalism assumes a well-defined carrier set \({\mathcal I}\). Hume's deepest skepticism — about the persistence of perceptions when unobserved, about the reality of the external world — undermines exactly this assumption. The algebra requires a stable inventory of elements that Hume's analysis intermittently denies.

Second, the bundle theory makes the very identification of "the same" perception across moments problematic. The algebra needs to talk about specific elements; the bundle theory denies that there are stable referents to talk about. Encoding Hume's view rigorously forces the formalism into either tokens-only or fictions-as-elements, neither of which fully matches Hume's prose.

Third, the affective dimension of Hume's account — the felt vivacity of impressions, the pleasure of association, the sentiment of approbation — is captured only as a single scalar (\(\deg\)). The phenomenology of feeling is much richer than this scalar, and Hume's psychology depends on distinctions the algebra elides.

Fourth, the radical empiricism's denial of necessary connections seems to prohibit the very algebraic identities the formalism asserts (e.g., associativity of \(\circ\)). A consistent Humean would have to treat these identities as themselves empirical regularities, generating a regress that the formalism does not resolve.

Fifth, Hume's pragmatic acquiescence in common life — his recognition that the skeptic must stop philosophizing and play backgammon — has no algebraic counterpart. The algebra cannot represent the lived suspension of theoretical doubt that Hume identifies as the actual habit by which philosophical skepticism is rendered livable.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Hume's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the stream of impressions, ideas, habits, and social sentiments, ordered by vivacity rather than metaphysical depth. Morphisms copy impressions into ideas and associate ideas by resemblance, contiguity, and cause, but no morphism reaches a necessary connection behind the sequence. τ governs succession, μ governs belief as enlivened expectation, and ψ captures the sentimental valence behind morals.

This is T14: causal necessity is exactly what fails to pull back from repeated conjunctions into any rationally inspectable \(\eta\), leaving habit as the obstruction's practical substitute.

Gottfried Leibniz

1646–1716 · Rationalist/Monadologist

The Position

Leibniz's late metaphysics is built on the monad: a simple, indivisible substance that is the ultimate building block of reality. Monads are not material atoms; they are immaterial points of perception, each one a perspective on the entire universe. Composite bodies are well-founded phenomena (phaenomena bene fundata) underwritten by aggregates of monads.

Monads have no windows. They cannot causally interact with one another, because what could pass between simple substances? Each monad's states unfold according to its own internal law, the law of its individual essence, which God selected at creation. The appearance of interaction — the moving billiard ball, the heard voice — is a coordinated change in monadic perceptions, not a transmission of force.

This coordination is pre-established harmony. God, in choosing to actualize this universe, selected a system of monads whose internal unfoldings would mesh perfectly, like a vast orchestra in which each player plays a fully scored part without ever listening to the others. The harmony is the divine guarantee of inter-subjective coherence.

The selection is not arbitrary. God, by the Principle of Sufficient Reason, must have a reason for actualizing one possible world rather than another. The chosen world is the best of all possible worlds — best in the sense of containing the greatest variety of phenomena producible from the simplest set of laws. Theodicy is the defense of this optimism against the appearance of evil.

Logically, Leibniz pursues a universal characteristic — a symbolic system that would render all genuine reasoning calculable. Combined with his discovery of the calculus and his pioneering work on what would later be called formal logic, the project anticipates by centuries the mathematization of inference. Truth, for Leibniz, is the containment of predicate in subject, and a sufficiently developed calculus could in principle decide all questions.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I} = \prod_i {\mathcal I}_i\), the product of monad-internal algebras indexed by all the monads of the actual world. Each \({\mathcal I}_i\) is a complete copy of the universe as perceived from monad \(i\), with its own composition \(\circ_i\), its own identity \(e_i\), and its own grading. The global identity \(e\) is the tuple of all monadic identities, corresponding to the divine view in which every monad is at rest.

Composition \(\circ\) acts component-wise: \((a_i)_i \circ (b_i)_i = (a_i \circ_i b_i)_i\). There is no cross-monadic mixing in \(\circ\); the windowlessness is encoded as the strict locality of composition. Each monad's algebra evolves independently, even though all monads' algebras evolve in coordinated ways.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is the harmony score between two global states: how well the perceptions of all monads cohere across the index. \(\rho\) is maximal exactly at states that lie on the divinely chosen trajectory; near-maximal at perturbations of it; and low for incoherent profiles.

Curvature \(\sigma\) is the curvature of perception, capturing the petites perceptions — the infinitesimal, sub-conscious variations that fill out each monad's perceptual life. Negation \(\nu_i(a)\) is the confused complement: what monad \(i\) only dimly perceives in contrast to its clear and distinct perceptions.

Reframing \(\eta\) is apperception, the monad's reflective awareness of its own perceptions; \(\eta\) carries an unreflective state to the corresponding self-conscious state. Transmission \(\omega\) is the divinely pre-established harmony: \(\omega\) is the global identity on inter-monadic relations (no causation), but it is non-trivial as the joint dynamics of the entire product, fixed by God once for all. The grading \(\deg\) is the degree of distinctness of a monad's perception, with bare monads at the bottom, animal souls in the middle, and rational spirits at the top.

\[ {\mathcal I} = \prod_{i \in \text{Monads}} {\mathcal I}_i, \qquad \omega \text{ between monads} = \mathrm{id}, \qquad \rho \text{ globally maximized at the actual world} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that each monad is an autonomous copy of the full algebra. Every \({\mathcal I}_i\) is, by Leibniz's "each monad mirrors the universe," isomorphic as a structure to the global algebra restricted to a perspective. This is a T8 claim about structural equivalence: the universe is repeated, with perspectival variations, inside each of its members.

The second claim is the pre-established harmony: \(\rho\) achieves a unique global maximum at the actual world. This is a T2 claim of unusual ambition — among all possible monadic configurations, the actual one maximizes resonance, and God's choice is the optimization that produced it. Theodicy becomes a claim about a global extremum.

The third claim is the windowlessness: \(\omega\) restricted to inter-monadic transmission is the identity. This is a T7 claim with the surprising content that the natural transmission operator does no work between substances; all inter-substantial coordination is pushed up to the global level of God's pre-established arrangement.

The fourth claim is the principle of identity of indiscernibles. If \(a\) and \(b\) are members of \({\mathcal I}\) with the same predicates, they are the same element. Algebraically, \({\mathcal I}\) is anti-symmetric under coincidence of properties. This is a T1 claim about identity, sharpened into the rejection of bare numerical distinctness.

The fifth claim is that truth is predicate containment. Every true proposition has its predicate already contained in the complete concept of its subject; \(\rho\) at the limit becomes containment, and a sufficiently powerful calculus can in principle decide every question. This is a T2-and-T1 claim: resonance saturates into identity for analytic truths, and analyticity exhausts all truth in the long run.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the product structure makes Leibniz's "each monad mirrors the universe" precisely formalizable. Each \({\mathcal I}_i\) is isomorphic as a structure to the global algebra, with the projection map \({\mathcal I} \to {\mathcal I}_i\) playing the role of the perspectival lens. This converts a metaphor into an algebraic isomorphism with sharply specifiable failure modes.

Second, the harmony measure \(\rho\) gives an explicit criterion for God's choice of world. One can in principle compare alternative monadic configurations by their global \(\rho\); Leibnizian theodicy then becomes a constrained optimization problem rather than an article of faith. Whether the actual world wins is empirical only modulo the choice of the harmony function.

Third, the doctrine of petites perceptions becomes the curvature \(\sigma\) at small scales. Sub-conscious perception is not the absence of perception but the smoothly varying background that integrates into apperception only when a threshold is crossed. The formalism predicts the existence of such thresholds and lets one ask where they lie.

Fourth, the identity of indiscernibles becomes a uniqueness condition on the algebra: no two distinct elements have the same property profile. This converts a metaphysical principle into a constraint on the structure of \({\mathcal I}\) that has consequences for the cardinality and topology of the algebra.

Fifth, the project of a universal characteristic is recast as the search for a calculus on \({\mathcal I}\) that decides \(\rho\) by symbolic manipulation. Modern proof-search and SAT solvers are direct descendants; the formalism makes Leibniz the explicit ancestor of mechanized reasoning, with the algebraic identities corresponding to the rules of the characteristic.

Where It Diverges

First, the absence of inter-monad causation means \(\circ\) is trivial across the product. The formalism cannot model the appearance of interaction — the apparently transmitted force of a collision — without supplementary apparatus that explains why the harmony looks like causation. Leibniz himself supplied an elaborate phenomenological story; the algebra does not.

Second, monads admit infinitely many degrees of distinctness, and the perceptions of any single monad are infinitely articulated. The formalism's grading \(\deg\) is at best a coarse approximation to this continuum; treating it as discrete strips out the differential richness Leibniz insists upon.

Third, God's pre-established harmony presupposes a free creative choice from a space of possible worlds. The formalism encodes the actual world as one product but does not represent the other possible products that God considered and rejected. Leibnizian modality requires more than the actual algebra; it requires the space of admissible algebras.

Fourth, the principle of sufficient reason — that every truth has a reason — cannot be enforced by the algebra alone. The algebra exhibits structure but does not guarantee that every structural fact has a non-trivial explanation; the principle is a meta-constraint that must be imposed from outside.

Fifth, the felt reality of monadic perception, the qualitative interiority that distinguishes a monad's view from a mere data structure, is lost in the product encoding. The formalism captures the structure of perspectives without capturing what it is like to be one.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Leibniz's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the ordered family of monadic perspectives on one pre-established world, together with the divine horizon of possible worlds. Morphisms between monads are not causal exchanges but harmony-preserving correspondences, each translating the whole universe from a different degree of clarity. π is decisive for monadic point of view, μ ranks possible worlds by compossibility and optimality, and β stabilises individual concepts across modal comparison.

This is T15: the best world is the maximal trans-horizonal stability of compossible predicates under God's survey of possible supports.

Baruch Spinoza

1632–1677 · Rationalist/Monist

The Position

Spinoza's Ethics argues, more geometrico, that there is one and only one substance, which can equivalently be called God or Nature (Deus sive Natura). Substance is that which exists in itself and is conceived through itself; only an absolutely infinite being satisfies this criterion, and there cannot be two such beings, since they would limit each other.

This single substance has infinitely many attributes, of which the human mind perceives two: thought and extension. Each attribute expresses the substance's essence completely under one aspect; thought and extension are not two parts of substance but two complete expressions of one substance, conceived from different angles.

Modes are particular ways the one substance is modified. Finite minds and finite bodies are modes — local, dependent, parasitic on substance. Every finite mode is determined by an infinite chain of prior modes, themselves modes of substance; freedom of indifference is illusory, and everything that happens follows with strict necessity from the nature of God.

Knowledge has three kinds. First-kind knowledge (imagination) is fragmentary and confused. Second-kind knowledge (reason) grasps the common notions, the structural features shared by all bodies and minds. Third-kind knowledge (intuitive science) sees particular things sub specie aeternitatis — under the aspect of eternity — as the necessary expressions of the divine essence.

The Ethics culminates in the doctrine of blessedness: the intellectual love of God. This is not a sentimental attachment but the mind's recognition of itself and of all things as expressions of the one substance, an active joy that follows necessarily from third-kind knowledge. Salvation is intellectual; freedom is the recognition of necessity.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of modes of the one substance. Each mode is tagged by attribute (thought, extension, …) and by its place in the deductive chain that follows from the divine essence. The identity \(e\) is the substance itself: not a mode among modes, but the unique element such that every mode is conceived through it and depends on it.

Composition \(\circ\) is modal determination: \(a \circ b\) is the mode that follows from the joint determination by modes \(a\) and \(b\) under the laws of the substance's nature. Composition is associative because the deductive structure of the Ethics is associative; there is one rigid chain of determination, branching but always consistent.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is the degree of shared attribute and shared determination: two modes resonate when they instantiate the same attribute and trace back to a common ancestor in the chain. Curvature \(\sigma\) is the curvature between adequate and inadequate ideas — between ideas that include their own grounds and ideas that do not.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the opposite mode in the same attribute: finite negation as Spinoza understands it, "all determination is negation." Reframing \(\eta\) is the view sub specie aeternitatis: an operator that carries an inadequate, time-bound idea to the eternal pattern of which it is a temporal instance.

Transmission \(\omega\) is the causal-logical order of Nature: the deductive unfolding of all modes from the substance. Crucially, since nothing is outside Nature, \(\omega\) acts only within \({\mathcal I}\); there are no external inputs. The grading \(\deg\) is degree of power (potentia) or conatus — the striving to persevere in being — with substance at the top and the most dependent modes at the bottom.

\[ \omega(a) \in {\mathcal I} \;\;\forall a \;\;(\text{immanence}), \qquad \eta = \text{view sub specie aeternitatis}, \qquad \nu(a) \text{ negates within an attribute} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that the one substance is the identity \(e\) of the entire algebra. Every mode is composed with \(e\) trivially, because every mode is conceived through substance. This is a T1 claim of maximal scope: there is exactly one identity, and it is metaphysically ultimate.

The second claim is that \(\omega\) is the identity at the global level of substance: there is nothing outside \({\mathcal I}\) for transmission to come from or go to. This is a T7 claim about closed transmission: the universe is causally and logically self-contained, with no transcendent input.

The third claim is that the view sub specie aeternitatis is a retraction onto adequate ideas. \(\eta\) carries any idea to its place in the eternal logical order, and the fixed points of \(\eta\) are exactly the adequate ideas. This is a T6 claim: reframing converges on the eternal, and salvation is the iterated application of \(\eta\) to one's mental life.

The fourth claim is the parallelism of attributes: thought and extension are isomorphic subalgebras of \({\mathcal I}\), each a complete expression of substance. This is a T8 claim of structural equivalence: under \(\eta\), the two attribute-subalgebras present the same structural facts under different surface forms.

The fifth claim is the conatus principle: every mode strives to persevere in being. Algebraically, every \(a\) maximizes \(\deg\) within the constraints of its position in the deductive chain. This is a T9 claim: the grading is not merely descriptive but is the directional force animating each mode, with God's infinite power as the global maximum.

What the Formalism Adds

First, parallelism becomes the assertion that the thought-subalgebra and the extension-subalgebra are isomorphic under \(\eta\). The notorious problem of how mental and physical can correspond without interacting becomes the structural fact that the two attributes share a common algebraic skeleton.

Second, conatus becomes a measurable quantity \(\deg\), giving Spinoza's psychological and political theory a quantitative framework. The dynamics of affect, the formation of political bodies, the struggle of each mode to persist — all become questions about the distribution and flow of \(\deg\) across the algebra.

Third, "all determination is negation" becomes the algebraic identity that finite modes are produced precisely by the limitation \(\nu\) of the infinite attribute. The formalism makes Spinoza's compressed slogan visibly equivalent to the existence of a non-trivial \(\nu\) operator that carves up the attributes into determinate modes.

Fourth, the third kind of knowledge — intuitive science — is given a precise algebraic location: it is the ability to see any particular mode \(a\) as the result of \(\omega\) applied to \(e\), in one synoptic view. This dignifies intuition as a structural fact, not an ineffable mystical experience.

Fifth, the doctrine of necessitarianism — that everything that happens follows necessarily from God's nature — becomes the assertion that \(\omega\), restricted to \({\mathcal I}\), is deterministic. Modal categories collapse: nothing is contingent because nothing has any source other than the eternal unfolding of substance. The formalism makes the determinism precise enough to be discussed.

Where It Diverges

First, Spinoza's infinite attributes — only thought and extension are accessible to finite minds, but there are infinitely many — are not finitely representable in \({\mathcal I}\). The formalism necessarily truncates to the two attributes we can think, losing the infinite richness Spinoza insists is constitutive of substance.

Second, the necessitarianism would imply \(\sigma = 0\) globally: nothing could have been otherwise, so there is no real curvature in the space of possibilities. This flattens the formalism's most distinctive feature; an algebra with everywhere-zero curvature is structurally close to a triviality, and Spinoza's geometry of affects requires more than this.

Third, the felt character of conatus — the inwardly experienced striving of each mode — is captured only as a scalar gradient. Spinoza's psychology of affects is much more articulated, distinguishing kinds of joy and sadness with their own dynamics that a single \(\deg\)-flow cannot represent.

Fourth, the third-kind intuitive knowledge is supposed to be qualitatively different from second-kind reasoning, not just more thorough. The algebra distinguishes them only by extent of \(\eta\)-iteration, eliding the phenomenological discontinuity Spinoza marks between deduction and beatific intuition.

Fifth, the political theory of the Tractatus Politicus — the formation of multitudes, the dynamics of state power, the conditions for democratic stability — depends on aggregations and emergent collective bodies that the bare modal algebra does not richly represent. Treating the state as a complex mode loses the political specificity Spinoza is at pains to develop.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Spinoza's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the immanent order of attributes, modes, and levels of knowledge: imagination, reason, and intuitive science. Morphisms translate the same substance under different attributes or move inadequate partial ideas toward adequate common notions. φ is conatus as the positive gradient of power, ι is the idea's expression of its object in God, and χ is flattened because there is only one substance-horizon differentiated by attributes.

This is T15: blessedness is the trans-horizonal stability of adequate ideas under the eternal necessity of substance.

George Berkeley

1685–1753 · Idealist/Immaterialist

The Position

Berkeley's central thesis, esse est percipi (to be is to be perceived), abolishes material substance. The reason is direct: the only things we ever encounter are ideas — colors, shapes, sounds, tactile sensations — and ideas exist only in minds. To posit a mind-independent material substance behind ideas is to posit something whose existence we could never have any evidence of, since all our evidence is itself ideas.

The young Berkeley arrives at this conclusion by an Ockham-like cut. Locke had insisted that primary qualities (extension, motion, shape) inhered in mind-independent matter, while secondary qualities (color, taste) existed only in the mind. Berkeley argues that the same considerations that show secondary qualities to be mind-dependent show primary qualities to be mind-dependent: extension is no less perceived than color.

What about the persistence of objects when no finite mind perceives them? Berkeley's answer is theological: God is the permanent perceiver. Objects continue to exist because God always perceives them. The order and regularity of nature is the order of ideas in the mind of God, communicated to finite minds in a regular language we have learned to interpret.

Crucially, Berkeley denies abstract general ideas. Locke had supposed that the mind, by abstraction, could form an idea of triangle that is neither right nor obtuse nor scalene nor isosceles. Berkeley insists this is impossible: every idea is fully determinate. What we call "general ideas" are particular ideas being used as signs for many other particulars resembling them.

The result is a remarkable combination of immaterialism and common sense. Berkeley is not denying tables and chairs; he is denying material substrata behind them. Tables and chairs are stable bundles of ideas, perceived by us when we look and by God always. Science remains intact as the description of the regular language of nature; theology gains immediate support; materialism — the alleged enemy of religion — is dissolved.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I} = \bigcup_M {\mathcal I}_M\), the union of mental idea-sets indexed by minds (finite spirits and God). Every element of \({\mathcal I}\) belongs to some mind; there is no idea that floats free of all minds. The identity \(e\) is the empty mental state, but in Berkeley's system it is realized only as God's resting attention on no particular finite object.

Composition \(\circ\) is co-perception: \(a \circ b\) is the complex idea that arises when \(a\) and \(b\) are perceived together by the same mind. Composition is fully internal to each \({\mathcal I}_M\); there is no inter-mind composition operator, although there can be mind-to-mind communication via signs.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is co-occurrence in perception: ideas that habitually accompany one another in the natural language of God's perception accumulate high \(\rho\). Curvature \(\sigma\) is identically zero: there is no material substrate beneath ideas, no holonomy from looping through a mind-independent matter, only the smooth play of perception.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the idea that negates \(a\) — for a perceived presence, the perceived absence. Reframing \(\eta\) is abstraction in Locke's sense, which Berkeley denies: there is no operator \(\eta\) on \({\mathcal I}\) that produces a fully indeterminate "general idea." The closest Berkeley admits is the use of a particular as a sign for many resembling particulars.

Transmission \(\omega\) is God's sustaining perception: God continuously perceives every idea in every \({\mathcal I}_M\), and God's perception is what holds the natural order in being. Within finite minds, \(\omega\) is the regular sequencing of ideas that constitutes the language of nature. The grading \(\deg\) is vividness or immediacy of sensation, with currently perceived sense-ideas at the top and faint memories at the bottom.

\[ \forall a \in {\mathcal I},\; \exists \text{ mind } M : a \in {\mathcal I}_M, \qquad \sigma \equiv 0, \qquad \omega = \text{God's perception sustaining all ideas} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that \({\mathcal I}\) is exactly the union of mental idea-sets; every idea belongs to some mind. This is a T1 claim restricted to mental monoids: the identity, composition, and grading all live within minds, and the very existence of \({\mathcal I}\) presupposes minds to host it. Materialism is the false claim that \({\mathcal I}\) extends beyond \(\bigcup_M {\mathcal I}_M\).

The second claim is that God is the unique fixed-point perceiver. Without God, the persistence of finite ideas across moments of finite inattention would have no carrier. With God, every \(a \in {\mathcal I}\) is sustained by \(\omega_G\), the divine perception. This is a T7 claim: there is a universal transmitter whose orbit covers the entire algebra.

The third claim is that abstraction \(\eta\) is not well-defined on \({\mathcal I}\) for fully general ideas. Where Locke posited \(\eta(\text{this triangle}) = \text{triangle in general}\), Berkeley denies that any such image exists; \(\eta\) collapses to the use of a particular as a sign. This is a T6 failure: reframing does not produce a new abstract element, only a re-labeling of an existing concrete one.

The fourth claim is that \(\sigma \equiv 0\): there is no material substrate, hence no curvature beyond what perception itself supplies. This is a T5 claim about the absence of asymmetry in the metaphysical depth: there are no hidden loops produced by mind-independent matter doing things behind our backs.

The fifth claim is the natural-language thesis: the regularities of experience constitute a language by which God communicates with finite minds. \(\omega\) acts on \({\mathcal I}\) as a generative grammar, and the laws of nature are the grammar's productions. This is a T7 claim with a semantic twist: transmission is meaningful, not merely mechanical.

What the Formalism Adds
