Bad faith is the systematic flight from this freedom — the pretense that one is a being-in-itself with a fixed essence, the role-playing of the waiter who is "being a waiter" too thoroughly to be free. Authenticity, by contrast, is the lucid acceptance of one's freedom and responsibility.

The look (le regard) of the other transforms one's situation. To be seen by another is to be reduced, momentarily, to an object in the other's world; the conflict of looks structures intersubjectivity. "Hell is other people" is not a complaint but an ontological observation.

Sartre's later turn to Marxism in Critique of Dialectical Reason attempts to integrate existentialist freedom with the structures of practico-inert seriality, group fusion, and historical agency. Throughout, his philosophical writing is interwoven with literary and political work that gives the abstractions concrete force.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of intentional projects of a for-itself: items as possibilities consciousness projects toward, situated within facticities that consciousness must take up. Each element is tagged by its facticity (the given) and its transcendence (the projected). The identity \(e\) is the bare consciousness as nihilating relation, prior to any specific project.

Composition \(\circ\) is project-formation: the integration of possibilities into the unified life-project that consciousness sustains. \(\circ\) is constrained by facticity but not determined by it; the same situation can ground multiple projects.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is project-coherence: the degree to which two possibilities can be integrated into one for-itself's life. Curvature \(\sigma\) is the curvature of nothingness — the irreducible gap between the for-itself and the in-itself, the negation that consciousness is.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is bad faith with respect to \(a\): the systematic pretense that one is determined where one is in fact free, or free where one is in fact constrained. \(\nu\) is internal to the for-itself, a self-misrelation, not an external opposite.

Reframing \(\eta\) is the look: the operator by which the for-itself is constituted as object in another's world, transforming its self-understanding. Transmission \(\omega\) is the temporal projection of the for-itself: continually fleeing the past and toward the future, never coinciding with itself. The grading \(\deg\) is degree of authenticity: bad-faith projects at the bottom, lucidly assumed projects at the top.

\[ \text{for-itself: } a \text{ is what it is not and is not what it is}, \qquad \nu(a) = \text{bad faith regarding } a \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that consciousness is a nihilating relation: the for-itself is not identical with itself but is the negation of its in-itself. \(\sigma \neq 0\) is constitutive; without curvature, there is no for-itself. This is a T5 claim with strong ontological content.

The second claim is that existence precedes essence: the elements of \({\mathcal I}\) for human beings are not given by a prior essence but are constituted through the projects \(\circ\) actually composes. This is a T1 claim with strong anti-essentialist consequences.

The third claim is that bad faith is \(\nu\) of an internal kind: the systematic self-misrelation by which the for-itself flees its own freedom. \(\nu\) operates on the relation, not on the relatum. This is a T1+\(\nu\) claim with deep psychological consequences.

The fourth claim is that the look is \(\eta\): a reframing operator that constitutes the for-itself as object in another's world. The other is not just another item in my world but a structural transformation of my world. This is a T6 claim with intersubjective content.

The fifth claim is that authenticity is the assumption of freedom: the for-itself that lucidly recognizes \(\sigma\) and accepts the responsibility \(\circ\) imposes. This is a normative addition to the structural facts: among the algebraically available projects, some are authentic and some are not.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the for-itself/in-itself distinction becomes the algebraic distinction between elements with non-trivial \(\sigma\) and elements with \(\sigma = 0\). Things have no curvature; consciousnesses are curvature.

Second, bad faith as \(\nu\) of the relation makes the Sartrean diagnosis structural: the bad-faith subject is not in error about a fact but in misrelation to its own freedom. The structural location of bad faith is in the relating, not in any relatum.

Third, the look as \(\eta\) gives intersubjective transformation an algebraic location: another's gaze does not just add information but reconfigures one's own algebra by introducing the in-itself dimension to one's previously for-itself self-understanding.

Fourth, the temporal structure of consciousness — fleeing the past, projecting toward the future — becomes the structural fact that \(\omega\) for the for-itself never has fixed points. There is no settled identity for consciousness to converge to.

Fifth, the freedom-facticity dialectic becomes the algebraic claim that \(\circ\) is constrained but not determined: the same facticity admits multiple projects, and the choice among them is not fixed by the facticity. The algebra exhibits the room within which existential freedom operates.

Where It Diverges

First, the lived feel of freedom and responsibility — the felt weight of choice, the anguish at the void of justification — is essential to Sartrean existentialism but resistant to algebraic representation. The formalism describes structural facts; it does not transmit the affective force.

Second, the literary and dramatic mode of Sartre's most influential work (Nausea, No Exit, The Flies) carries philosophical content that the structural exposition cannot replicate. The novels and plays are part of the philosophy.

Third, the relation between Sartre's existentialism and his later Marxism remains contested, and the algebra cannot adjudicate. Whether the practico-inert and group fusion really integrate with the for-itself or whether Sartre's later work breaks with the earlier is a substantive question outside the algebra.

Fourth, the political engagement that defined Sartre's public life — his stances on Algeria, on Vietnam, on Cuba, on the Soviet Union — depends on substantive commitments the algebra does not generate.

Fifth, Sartre's own later partial repudiation of Being and Nothingness as too individualist and too Cartesian indicates a self-revision the algebra's static treatment misses. The mature Sartre's relation to the existentialist Sartre is itself a philosophical event.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Sartre's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the field of situations in which pour-soi projects itself against facticity, others, and the inert en-soi. Morphisms are choices that reconfigure the meaning of factical constraints without abolishing them; bad faith is a degenerate restriction that treats a project as a thing. π is the situated agent, ι is consciousness as directed nothingness, τ is projective temporality, and ψ marks shame under the look of the Other.

This is T14: no essence pulls back from social or biological facts into the agent's horizon; meaning is non-invariant under free projection.

Critical Theory

Theodor W. Adorno

1903–1969 · Frankfurt School, negative dialectics

The Position

Adorno's Negative Dialectics resists the Hegelian closure of the dialectic in synthesis: identity-thinking — the reduction of the particular to its concept — is the philosophical face of administered domination. The non-identical, that aspect of the object that exceeds its concept, must be honoured. Truth content lives in fragments, in art (especially difficult modernist art), and in the constellation of concepts around an object, never in a single positive proposition. The Dialectic of Enlightenment (with Horkheimer) shows that instrumental reason turns against itself, producing the regression it sought to overcome.

The Instantiation

For each object \(x\) there is a concept \(C_x\) that subsumes it; the non-identical residue is \(N_x = x \setminus \rho(C_x)\) considered as that which the concept does not exhaust. Identity-thinking is the operation of suppressing \(N_x\): \(x \mapsto C_x\), discarding remainder.

Negative dialectics is the operator \(\nabla\) that, given a concept \(C\) and an object \(x\), produces not a synthesis but a constellation \(\{C_1,\ldots,C_k\}\) of concepts whose joint configuration approximates \(x\) without reducing it: \(\nabla(C,x)=\{C_i\}\) with \(\bigcap \rho(C_i) \supseteq \{x\}\) and no \(C_i\) alone covers \(x\).

Truth content of an artwork \(A\) is the way \(A\) sediments contradictions: \(\sigma(A,P) \wedge \sigma(A,\neg P)\) holds for socially significant \(P\), and the artwork's value is the precision with which it holds the contradiction without premature resolution.

Instrumental reason is the operator \(\iota\) that maximises means-end efficiency: \(\iota(C,\text{end})=C'\) where \(C'\) reduces \(C\) to its functional core. The Dialectic of Enlightenment theorem is that iterating \(\iota\) globally produces regression: \(\iota^n\) eventually yields myth-like rigidity.

The culture industry is \(\iota\) applied to cultural production: \(\iota(\text{art})=\text{commodity}\), where \(\sigma\) is reduced to predictable consumer satisfaction and \(\eta\) to standardised genre placement.

\[ N_x = x \setminus \rho(C_x);\quad \nabla(C,x)=\{C_i\}_i\text{ s.t. }\bigcap_i \rho(C_i) \supseteq \{x\};\quad \iota^n \to \text{regression} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

T-Adorno-1 (non-identity). For any non-trivial object \(x\) and concept \(C_x\), \(N_x \neq \emptyset\): the concept never exhausts the object.

T-Adorno-2 (constellation over synthesis). \(\nabla(C,x)\) yields a non-empty configuration of concepts whose joint coverage of \(x\) is strictly greater than any \(C_i\) alone, and no synthesis \(C^* = \bigvee C_i\) recovers the same coverage.

T-Adorno-3 (truth in contradiction). The truth content of an artwork is measured by the load of unresolved contradictions it sustains: \(\mu(A) = |\{P : \sigma(A,P) \wedge \sigma(A,\neg P)\}|\), with greater \(\mu\) indicating greater truth content.

T-Adorno-4 (dialectic of enlightenment). \(\lim_{n\to\infty} \iota^n(\text{reason})\) converges to a fixed point indistinguishable from mythic compulsion.

T-Adorno-5 (critique). The only adequate philosophical operation in administered society is critique: a reflexive \(\nabla\) applied to the categories of administration themselves.

What the Formalism Adds

The non-identical is given a specific algebraic placeholder \(N_x\), distinguishing the philosophical thesis from a mere injunction to humility.

Constellation-thinking is rendered as a definite multi-concept structure rather than a vague metaphor, allowing one to ask what makes a constellation good.

The dialectic-of-enlightenment thesis becomes a convergence claim about iteration of an operator, exposing it to formal scrutiny.

Truth content of art receives a measure, however rough, that connects modernist difficulty to a structural property of \(\sigma\).

Identity-thinking is identified as a specific suppression operation, making explicit what Adorno polemicises against.

Where It Diverges

Adorno's prose enacts what it describes: the constellation method is performed in his sentences, and the algebra cannot perform — only describe.

The aesthetic theory's specificity to Schoenberg, Beckett, Kafka is not captured by a generic \(\mu\) measure; the formalism flattens what Adorno particularises.

The pessimism about late capitalism is a substantive social diagnosis; the algebraic regression theorem is structural and silent on the historical situation.

Adorno resists systematic philosophy on principle; instantiating him in an algebra is a betrayal he would name as such.

The Marxian commitments — the labour theory of value, the analysis of exchange — are background conditions for negative dialectics that the algebra abstracts away.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Adorno's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the damaged field of late-capitalist culture, identity-thinking, artworks, and negative dialectical critique. Morphisms translate concepts onto objects and show the remainder they fail to capture; genuine thought preserves non-identity rather than forcing closure. δ is crucial as differentiation against the concept, χ marks administered social totality, and ψ registers suffering as the affective truth of domination.

This is T14: identity-concepts fail to pull back onto the object's non-identical content, and philosophy must dwell in that obstruction rather than erase it.

Walter Benjamin

1892–1940 · Critical theorist, messianic materialist

The Position

Benjamin's thought combines Marxism, Jewish messianism, and a theory of media: the work of art in the age of mechanical reproduction loses its aura (the unique here-and-now of the original) and gains political accessibility. History is not a continuum to be narrated but a constellation of now-times (Jetztzeit) that flash up in moments of danger. The dialectical image arrests history in a constellation that makes the past legible to the present.

The Instantiation

Aura is the function \(\alpha(A,c) = (\text{uniqueness}(A) \cdot \text{distance}(A,c))\) defined on artworks \(A\) in contexts \(c\); \(\alpha\) is high for the cult original and decays to zero under reproduction operators \(R\): \(\alpha(R(A),c) \approx 0\).

Reproducibility is an operator \(R: A \mapsto \{A_1,A_2,\ldots\}\) producing a multiplicity of instances; under \(R\), \(\rho(A)\) explodes from a singleton to a mass extension.

Now-time is a special context \(c^*\) at which a past idea \(I_{\text{past}}\) becomes legible: \(\eta(I_{\text{past}},c^*)\) is defined and discloses \(I_{\text{past}}\) in its truth, which it did not possess in its own time.

The dialectical image is a pair \((I_{\text{past}}, I_{\text{present}})\) such that \(\sigma(I_{\text{past}}, I_{\text{present}})\) is a flash-relation, instantaneous and unrepeatable, and \(\nu(I_{\text{past}}, I_{\text{present}})\) is non-trivial: each transforms the other in the encounter.

Messianic time is a global cessation operator \(M\) that arrests the homogeneous flow of history: under \(M\), the chain \(c_1 \to c_2 \to \cdots\) is interrupted and a constellation of all past \(c_k\) becomes simultaneously present to a redemptive gaze.

\[ \alpha(R(A),c) \approx 0;\quad \eta(I_{\text{past}},c^*)\ \text{defined};\quad M:\text{seq}(c_k) \to \text{constellation}(c_k) \]

Central Claim as Theorem

T-Benjamin-1 (decay of aura). For any reproducible \(A\), \(\alpha(R^n(A),c) \to 0\) as \(n \to \infty\); aura is destroyed by mass reproduction.

T-Benjamin-2 (politicisation). As \(\alpha\) decays, the political availability of \(A\) (its capacity to enter \(\sigma\)-relations with collective practices) increases.

T-Benjamin-3 (dialectical image). At privileged \(c^*\), \(\sigma(I_{\text{past}},I_{\text{present}})\) is non-trivial and discloses both terms; outside such \(c^*\), the relation is not visible.

T-Benjamin-4 (now-time). Historical understanding is not the reconstruction of the past but the seizure of \(c^*\) at moments of danger, when \(\eta\) suddenly opens.

T-Benjamin-5 (messianic interruption). \(M\) is not derivable from any operation within the historical chain; it enters from outside as a weak messianic power granted to each generation.

What the Formalism Adds

Aura is given a measurable structure with components, exposing what reproduction destroys and what survives.

The dialectical image is exhibited as a definite relational pair, replacing a notoriously elusive concept with a structure one can examine.

Now-time and messianic interruption are distinguished as different operators, clarifying Benjamin's complex temporality.

The thesis on art and politics becomes a comparative claim about \(\alpha\) and political availability, susceptible to empirical examination.

The constellation model of history is shared with Adorno but here applied to time itself, and the formalism makes the parallel explicit.

Where It Diverges

Benjamin's writing is fragmentary, aphoristic, allegorical; reducing his theses to operators flattens the very form that for him is part of the truth.

The theological dimension — the Jewish messianic — is more than a placeholder operator \(M\); it carries a specific tradition the algebra does not invoke.

The Arcades Project's method of citation and montage is a working procedure the formalism cannot reproduce: Benjamin assembles, he does not theorise.

The political stakes of the aura essay (anti-fascist art-politics in the 1930s) are urgent and historical; the algebra treats them as timeless.

The notion of weak messianic power presupposes a covenantal relation the algebra renders only as an external operator.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Benjamin's \(\mathcal{H}\) is constellated from historical moments, artworks, commodities, ruins, and messianic interruptions. Morphisms are citations and dialectical images that make a past fragment flash up in a present danger rather than linearly transmitting it. τ is now-time rather than homogeneous chronology, χ marks epochs of reproducibility and commodity phantasmagoria, and β appears in names as non-arbitrary linguistic revelation.

This is T13 with messianic tension: historical understanding glues fragments into a constellation whose unity is not progress but a charged present image.

Jürgen Habermas

1929– · Communicative action, discourse ethics

The Position

Habermas reconstructs critical theory on a communicative basis: rationality is not the monological deployment of instrumental reason but the intersubjective achievement of understanding under conditions of free, equal, and sincere discourse. Validity claims (truth, normative rightness, sincerity) are raised in every speech act and can be redeemed only through argumentation. The ideal speech situation is a counterfactual presupposition of any genuine discourse, and it grounds discourse ethics: norms are valid iff they could be agreed to by all affected in unconstrained dialogue.

The Instantiation

A speech act \(\phi\) is a triple \((p,n,s)\) of validity claims: propositional truth \(p\), normative rightness \(n\), sincerity \(s\). For \(\phi\) to be communicatively rational, all three must be redeemable in argumentation.

Argumentation is a multi-agent extension of \(\eta\): \(\eta_{\text{disc}}(\phi,\Gamma)\) where \(\Gamma\) is a discourse community and the operator yields the consensus reached under unconstrained exchange of reasons.

The ideal speech situation is a structural constraint on \(\Gamma\): every participant has equal opportunity to speak, no force operates other than the force of the better argument, and all relevant validity claims are open to challenge.

Discourse ethics is the principle: a norm \(n\) is valid iff \(\eta_{\text{disc}}(n,\Gamma_{\text{ideal}})=\top\), where \(\Gamma_{\text{ideal}}\) is the unconstrained discourse community of all affected.

Strategic action contrasts with communicative action: in strategic mode, the speaker treats the hearer as an object to be influenced, suspending validity-claim redemption; the algebra distinguishes the two by whether \(\eta_{\text{disc}}\) is genuinely operative.

\[ \phi=(p,n,s);\quad \eta_{\text{disc}}(\phi,\Gamma_{\text{ideal}})=\top \;\Leftrightarrow\; \phi\text{ is rationally valid} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

T-Habermas-1 (universal pragmatics). Every serious speech act necessarily raises the three validity claims; denial of this is performatively self-refuting.

T-Habermas-2 (discourse principle). A norm \(n\) is morally valid iff it would be agreed to by all affected in ideal discourse.

T-Habermas-3 (ideal-speech-situation as presupposition). Engagement in argumentation already presupposes the ideal-speech conditions counterfactually; abandoning them dissolves the argumentation itself.

T-Habermas-4 (system vs. lifeworld). Modern societies decompose into system (steered by money and power, instrumental rationality) and lifeworld (steered by communication, communicative rationality); colonisation of lifeworld by system pathologises social life.

T-Habermas-5 (rational reconstruction). Critical theory proceeds by rationally reconstructing the implicit competences of communicating subjects, not by deriving norms from a prior metaphysics.

What the Formalism Adds

It exhibits validity claims as a structured triple, making explicit what is at stake in any communicative interaction.

Discourse ethics is rendered as a definite condition involving a counterfactual operator and an ideal community, susceptible to formal analysis of its presuppositions.

System and lifeworld become distinct algebraic regimes (means-end vs. communicative \(\sigma\)), and colonisation becomes a definable encroachment.

The contrast with strategic action is captured as a difference in which operator is genuinely active, replacing intuitive contrast with structural distinction.

Universal pragmatics becomes a claim about the necessary structure of any speech-act algebra, exposed to formal-pragmatic test.

Where It Diverges

The ideal speech situation is criticised as too thin or too thick; the algebra inherits both criticisms by needing to specify \(\Gamma_{\text{ideal}}\) precisely.

Habermas' procedural ethics has been faulted for being substantively empty; the algebra's reduction to a discourse condition reproduces this thinness.

Power asymmetries inside actual discourse (gender, race, class) are not eliminable by stipulating ideal conditions; critics from Foucault onward press this point and the algebra does not refute them.

The reconstructive method depends on a developmental psychology (Kohlberg, Piaget) the algebra does not encode and which has been substantively contested.

Religious and metaphysical sources of moral motivation that Habermas later acknowledges as needed for political solidarity are not accessible to the formal apparatus.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Habermas' \(\mathcal{H}\) is the communicative space of lifeworlds, system media, speech situations, and public spheres. Morphisms are validity-claim translations among speaker, hearer, and shared world, ideally preserving truth, rightness, and sincerity while resisting colonisation by money and power. π is intersubjective rather than solitary, χ is the lifeworld background, and μ measures discursive acceptability under unconstrained argument.

This is T13: rational consensus is sheaf-gluing of horizon-local validity claims under the cover of all affected participants in discourse.

Phenomenology

Edmund Husserl

1859–1938 · Phenomenologist

The Position

Husserl founds phenomenology as the rigorous descriptive science of consciousness. Its central insight is intentionality: every act of consciousness is consciousness of something. Perception is perception-of, judgment is judgment-of, memory is memory-of. There are no contentless mental acts, and there is no content not given to some act.

To study consciousness scientifically, Husserl introduces the phenomenological reduction or epoché: a methodical bracketing of the natural attitude in which we take the world's existence for granted. We do not deny that the world exists; we suspend the existence-thesis to attend to how things are given to consciousness, regardless of whether they exist as given.

Each intentional act has a noetic side (the act of perceiving, remembering, judging) and a noematic side (the perceived-as-perceived, the remembered-as-remembered). The noema is not the object simpliciter but the object as it presents itself in the act, with its modes of givenness. Phenomenology describes these noematic structures.

A central distinction is between empty and fulfilled intentions. I can think of a friend who is absent (an empty intention); when she enters the room, the empty intention is fulfilled by the corresponding intuition. Knowledge, in Husserl's account, is the fulfillment of empty intentions by adequate intuition; evidence is the lived experience of such fulfillment.

Inner time-consciousness is the deep structure that makes intentional life possible. Every present moment is structured as primal impression, retention (the just-past held in the now), and protention (the about-to-come anticipated in the now). This triple structure is the form of all temporal experience and ultimately the source of the unity of consciousness.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of noematic contents — senses (Sinne) presented to consciousness in their full noematic articulation. Each element is tagged by act-type (perception, memory, imagination, judgment) and by mode of givenness (full, partial, empty, signitive). The identity \(e\) is the empty noema of any-content-whatever — a formal placeholder for the open horizon of consciousness.

Composition \(\circ\) is intentional synthesis: the unification of multiple noemata under a single act of consciousness. Husserl's analyses of perceptual synthesis (the unity of an object across its profiles) and predicative synthesis (the unity of subject-and-predicate in judgment) are particular instances of \(\circ\) acting on appropriate inputs.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is noematic overlap — the fulfillment measure between two noemata. When \(a\) is an empty intention and \(b\) is the corresponding intuition, \(\rho(a,b) = 1\) marks complete fulfillment; partial overlap registers partial fulfillment. Curvature \(\sigma\) is the curvature of intentional fulfillment versus disappointment: where expectations meet incoming intuition, \(\sigma\) is small; where they fail, \(\sigma\) spikes.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the empty intention lacking fulfillment for \(a\): the intentional state in which \(a\) is meant but not given. Reframing \(\eta\) is the epoché: the operator that brackets the existential index of any noema, presenting only its noematic content in suspension from the natural attitude.

Transmission \(\omega\) is time-consciousness, structured as the triple \(\langle\text{retention}, \text{primal impression}, \text{protention}\rangle\). \(\omega\) propagates noemata along the temporal stream, holding the just-past in retention, projecting the about-to-come in protention. The grading \(\deg\) is clarity of givenness, with fully fulfilled intuitions at the top and merely signitive intentions at the bottom.

\[ \omega(a) = \langle \text{ret}(a),\, a,\, \text{prot}(a) \rangle, \qquad \rho(a,b) \text{ maximal} \iff a \text{ fulfilled by } b \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that intentionality is \(\circ\) with the noema as right-identity relative to the act. Every act is composed with its noema in a structurally specific way; without this composition there is no intentional content. This is a T1 claim: the very possibility of consciousness presupposes the algebra's basic operation.

The second claim is that time-consciousness is \(\omega\) realized as a triple operator. Every conscious moment carries its retention and protention; the triple structure is what makes the unity of temporal experience possible. This is a T7 claim with a specific decomposition: \(\omega\) is not a scalar transmission but a structured triple.

The third claim is that the epoché is the \(\eta\) that brackets the existential index. \(\eta\) does not deny existence; it suspends the question of existence to expose pure noematic structure. This is a T6 claim about reframing: presentation is changed (existence-claim removed), but structural content is preserved.

The fourth claim is that evidence is a maximum of \(\rho\). Apodictic knowledge — knowledge given with the impossibility of being otherwise — is the limit case in which \(\rho(a, \text{intuition}_a) = 1\) for some act \(a\). This is a T2 claim about resonance maxima as the algebraic content of evidential certainty.

The fifth claim concerns the horizon structure of consciousness: every noema comes with a set of co-meant possibilities (the back of the cube I see only the front of, the friend's other moods given in her present face). Algebraically, every \(a\) carries a set of \(\rho\)-near elements not currently fulfilled but available for fulfillment. This is a T2-and-T7 structural claim about the implicit dimension of every explicit content.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the fulfillment relation is exhibited as \(\rho\). When \(\rho(a,b)\) is maximal, the empty intention \(a\) is fulfilled by the intuition \(b\). This makes evidence a precise structural property — a maximum of resonance — rather than a phenomenological primitive resistant to further analysis.

Second, the noetic-noematic correlation becomes the assertion that the act-side and content-side jointly satisfy the monoid axioms. The notoriously difficult question of how noema relates to noesis becomes the structural fact that they are inputs to the same composition.

Third, time-consciousness as a triple operator gives Husserl's Inner Time-Consciousness lectures a precise algebraic encoding. The retention-protention structure is not a mysterious new dimension of consciousness but a definite functorial structure on \({\mathcal I}\) over a temporal index.

Fourth, the epoché's careful distinction between bracketing and denying becomes the algebraic distinction between applying \(\eta\) (which preserves \(\circ\)-structure) and removing elements from \({\mathcal I}\) (which would destroy structure). The methodological discipline of phenomenology has algebraic backing.

Fifth, the genetic phenomenology of the late Husserl — the constitution of higher-level intentionalities from sedimented lower ones — is rendered as a layered structure on \({\mathcal I}\), with \(\omega\) propagating sedimentation upward. This recasts genetic analysis as a study of how the algebra is built up over time from primal data.

Where It Diverges

First, the first-person character of phenomenology resists algebraization. The algebra treats elements as third-person structural items; Husserl insists that phenomenological description begins from the lived first-person standpoint. The formalism elides the indexical "this consciousness here" that grounds the entire enterprise.

Second, the transcendental ego — the pure I that performs the epoché and unifies the stream of consciousness — has no clean algebraic counterpart. Treating it as the identity \(e\) over-formalizes; treating it as just another element under-respects its constitutive role.

Third, intersubjectivity, which Husserl treats with great care in the Fifth Cartesian Meditation, requires multiple algebras with constitutive cross-mappings (appresentation, pairing). The bare formalism handles only one consciousness; the inter-monadic apparatus needed to constitute the other is not internally available.

Fourth, the lifeworld (Lebenswelt) of the late Husserl is a pre-theoretical horizon of taken-for-granted significance that grounds all scientific practice. The algebra, being an explicit structure, lives within the very theoretical attitude the lifeworld stands beneath.

Fifth, the essentialism of eidetic intuition — the claim that we directly intuit essences (the essence of red, the essence of perception) — requires a faculty distinct from the algebra's structural similarity \(\rho\). Eidetic insight is supposed to deliver necessity, not just resonance.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Husserl's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the lattice of noetic-noematic horizons belonging to a constituting consciousness: profiles, anticipations, memories, kinaesthetic possibilities, and intersubjective confirmations. Morphisms are epoché-style bracketings and intentional modifications that restrict the natural attitude to what is given as such. ι is explicit intentionality, π is the transcendental ego's perspectival field, τ is inner time-consciousness, and R appears in reflection on constitution itself.

This is the paradigmatic T13: constitution of an object is sheaf-gluing of profile-perceptions across a covering family of perspectival horizons.

Martin Heidegger

1889–1976 · Phenomenologist/Existentialist

The Position

Heidegger's Being and Time reopens the question of Being (Seinsfrage) which, he argues, the metaphysical tradition has both forgotten and concealed. Being is not a being among others — not a thing, not a property, not a substance — but the disclosure (Lichtung, the clearing) within which beings come to presence as the beings they are.

To approach Being, Heidegger begins with Dasein: the being for whom Being is an issue, the being whose own Being is at stake in its understanding. Dasein is human existence, characterized by always already being-in-the-world, thrown into a situation it did not choose, projecting itself toward possibilities, and falling into the chatter and curiosity of everyday averageness.

The structures of Dasein — care (Sorge), being-toward-death, anxiety, conscience, guilt, resoluteness — are existentialia, not properties of an object. Authenticity (Eigentlichkeit) consists in owning one's finitude, hearing the call of conscience, and projecting oneself with resolute openness; inauthenticity is absorption in the das Man, the impersonal "they" that levels possibilities to the average.

The later Heidegger turns from existential analytic to history of Being. Different epochs are different sendings of Being — different ways in which beings are disclosed. The modern epoch is the Gestell (enframing), the technological revealing that reduces all beings to standing reserve for ordering. The danger is total enframing; the saving power is the possibility of another revealing.

Throughout, Heidegger insists on the ontological difference: Being is not a being. To collapse the difference — to treat Being as if it were one super-being or the totality of beings — is the fundamental forgetting that the entire tradition has perpetrated. Thinking proper is the meditation that holds open the difference.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of meanings disclosed within a world — items as they show themselves under a particular sending of Being. Each element is tagged by its readiness-to-hand or presence-at-hand status, by its position in a referential totality, and by its disclosed-versus-concealed character. The identity \(e\) is the clearing itself, the open in which any disclosure can occur — but \(e\) is not properly an element of \({\mathcal I}\) so much as its precondition.

Composition \(\circ\) is hermeneutic synthesis: the interpretation that understands one entity in terms of another within a world of significance. Composition presupposes the referential totality of the world; one cannot compose meanings outside of a context of involvement. The "as"-structure of all interpretation makes \(\circ\) hermeneutic in essence.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is ontological proximity: how near two meanings stand in the clearing — whether they share equipmental context (hammer and nail), existential meaning (anxiety and death), or epochal disclosure (technology and standing reserve). Curvature \(\sigma\) is the ontological difference itself — the irreducible curvature between Being and beings, never closable without forgetting.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the concealment (Verborgenheit) of \(a\): the hidden side of every disclosure, the not-yet-revealed that accompanies every revealing. Reframing \(\eta\) is Destruktion: the dismantling of sedimented tradition to retrieve original meaning, an operator that strips away accreted interpretations to expose the more primordial disclosure.

Transmission \(\omega\) is thrownness-projection (Geworfenheit-Entwurf): Dasein is thrown into a situation and projects itself toward possibilities; the world transmits itself as the horizon of significance Dasein finds itself within. The grading \(\deg\) is degree of disclosure: ownmost (authentic) at the top, average everydayness in the middle, fallenness into chatter at the bottom.

\[ \sigma \neq 0 \;\;(\text{ontological difference}), \qquad \omega(\text{Dasein}) \ni \text{Dasein} \;\;(\text{self-referentiality}) \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that the ontological difference is \(\sigma \neq 0\). Being cannot be collapsed into any being, including the totality of beings, because the structural curvature between disclosing and disclosed is irreducible. This is a T5 claim: the algebra has an essential asymmetry that no further synthesis can dissolve.

The second claim is that Destruktion is the reframing operator \(\eta\) that reverses sedimentation. Where the metaphysical tradition has accumulated layered interpretations of Being that conceal the original question, \(\eta\) strips the layers to recover what was first disclosed. This is a T6 claim: reframing changes presentation while exposing the original structure.

The third claim is that Dasein is the unique self-referential element: \(\omega(\text{Dasein}) \ni \text{Dasein}\). Dasein is the being whose being is at issue for it, the being included in its own thrownness. This is a T7 claim of unusual reflexivity — the only element of the algebra whose transmission orbit contains itself.

The fourth claim is being-in-the-world: there is no idea outside a context of significance, no decontextualized element of \({\mathcal I}\). Composition \(\circ\) is everywhere world-relative. This is a T1 claim that monoid identity is contextual: \(e\) is not a freestanding element but the open condition of a particular world.

The fifth claim is the epochal sending of Being. Different historical epochs realize different distributions of \(\rho\), \(\sigma\), and \(\omega\) over \({\mathcal I}\); the Greek world, the medieval, the modern, the technological each present beings differently. This is a T7 claim with epochal periodicity: \(\omega\) is itself history-dependent, and the algebra is differently parameterized in each epoch.

What the Formalism Adds

First, being-in-the-world becomes the algebraic claim that there is no idea outside a context of significance — no decontextualized element of \({\mathcal I}\). The Cartesian assumption of context-free representations is dissolved by the structural fact that \(\circ\) presupposes a referential totality.

Second, the clearing is the pre-algebraic condition for \({\mathcal I}\) itself. Identifying \(e\) with the clearing rather than with any disclosed item locates the difference between Being and beings precisely where Heidegger insists it lies: at the boundary of the algebra.

Third, the structure of care (Sorge) — being-ahead-of-itself, being-already-in, being-alongside — maps onto a triple structure on \({\mathcal I}\) at every Dasein-element, parallel to Husserl's protention-retention-primal-impression but ontologically (not merely temporally) loaded.

Fourth, the analysis of equipment (Zeug) — the hammer is what it is only within a totality of references to nails, boards, work, dwelling — becomes the algebraic claim that every readiness-to-hand element belongs to a connected component of \(\rho\) with all the items of its referential context.

Fifth, Gestell (enframing) becomes the diagnostic that the modern epoch reduces all elements of \({\mathcal I}\) to a single role: standing reserve under \(\omega\). The diversity of disclosure modes collapses to one dimension, and the algebra becomes pathologically thin. The formalism makes the danger Heidegger names visible as a structural impoverishment.

Where It Diverges

First, Heidegger insists that Being cannot be mathematized; the formalism performs exactly the kind of "calculative thinking" he opposes. Its value is at most diagnostic — exposing structural facts that meditative thinking might then take up — and never substitutive for the meditative engagement Heidegger demands.

Second, the difference between meditative and calculative thinking is the very hinge of Heidegger's late critique of metaphysics, and the algebra falls cleanly on the calculative side. Using the formalism to interpret Heidegger thus enacts the forgetting of Being whose overcoming Heidegger calls for.

Third, the historical sending of Being involves a depth of singularity that no parameterized algebra can capture. Each epoch is irreducibly itself, not an instance of a more general type; treating epochs as parameter values for one algebra normalizes the very singularity Heidegger's history of Being is meant to honor.

Fourth, the language of Heidegger's late thought — Ereignis, Geviert, Es gibt — operates poetically rather than structurally, attempting to evoke what cannot be said in propositional form. The algebra is propositional through and through; it cannot host the linguistic play that is Heidegger's last methodological resort.

Fifth, Heidegger's controversial political engagements and his refusal of liberal-democratic universalism flow from his ontology in ways the formalism cannot reproduce. The structural facts the algebra captures are politically neutral; the substantive politics Heidegger draws from his ontology requires concrete historical commitments outside the algebraic content.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Heidegger's \(\mathcal{H}\) is Dasein's worldhood: equipmental contexts, moods, being-with, thrown projection, and the temporal horizon of Being. Morphisms are disclosedness-shifts—breakdown, anxiety, interpretation, and authentic retrieval—that change how entities show up. τ is ecstatic temporality, π is Dasein as being-in-the-world, φ is care, and χ names historical understandings of Being.

This is T14: present-at-hand ontology fails to pull back across the ready-to-hand and existential horizons, revealing Being as horizon-relative disclosure.

Maurice Merleau-Ponty

1908–1961 · Phenomenologist

The Position

Merleau-Ponty argues that perception is not the inner mental representation of an outer world but a bodily engagement with the perceived. The perceiving body is not an object I have but the subject I am — the lived body (corps propre), the zero-point from which all spatial orientation departs and to which it returns.

The body schema is the pre-reflective, dynamic awareness of one's own body in its possibilities of action. It is not built up from kinesthetic sensations but is presupposed by them; the body knows how to reach for the cup before any deliberation. Skill, habit, and motor intentionality all live at this pre-reflective level.

Perception is fundamentally figure-ground structured. There is no perception of an isolated point; every perceived item appears against a horizon, with implicit context and indeterminate edges. The Gestalt structure is constitutive of perceptual life, and any attempt to decompose it into atomic sensations falsifies it.

The flesh (la chair), in Merleau-Ponty's late ontology, is the common element of perceiver and perceived — neither matter nor mind but the chiasmic intertwining by which the touching hand can also be touched. This reversibility is the ontological clue that subject and object are not two separable substances but two sides of one fabric.

Throughout, Merleau-Ponty insists that intellectualism and empiricism alike miss the phenomenon by attempting to reconstruct perception from prior abstractions. The Phenomenology of Perception aims to return to the things themselves as encountered by the engaged, embodied subject, prior to any theoretical re-construction.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of bodily-motor meanings: items disclosed to the lived body in its capacities for action. Each element is tagged by motor intentionality (graspable, reachable, traversable) and by sedimentation (novel skill, partially mastered, automatic habit). The identity \(e\) is the body schema itself: the implicit, ever-present awareness of one's own bodily possibilities relative to which any motor act is performed.

Composition \(\circ\) is motor synthesis: the combination of skills and gestures into more complex coordinated actions. Riding a bicycle is the composition of balance, pedaling, steering, and looking; each component lives within \({\mathcal I}\), and \(\circ\) integrates them into a single executable habit.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is gestural resonance: how strongly two motor meanings recruit similar bodily resources or anticipate similar action arcs. Curvature \(\sigma\) is the curvature of figure-ground — the irreducible structure by which any perceived item appears against a context that is not perceived in the same mode.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the motor failure or breakdown of \(a\): the inability to perform what one had been able to perform, the famous case of Schneider whose body schema is impaired by brain injury. \(\nu\) reveals the structures of \({\mathcal I}\) by their failure.

Reframing \(\eta\) is thematic reflection: the act of making implicit motor skill explicit, attending to what the body usually does unattended. \(\eta\) carries pre-reflective skill into reflective awareness, often disturbing the skill in the process. Transmission \(\omega\) is habit formation — the sedimentation of motor skills through repetition. The grading \(\deg\) is degree of automaticity, with novel struggles at the bottom and fully sedimented habits at the top.

\[ \omega^n(a) \to \text{habit}(a), \qquad e = \text{body schema}, \qquad \nu(a) = \text{motor failure of } a \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that habit is the fixed point of \(\omega\). Iterated practice converges on a stable motor pattern that is both effective and felt as effortless. This is a T7 claim with experiential content: transmission terminates in fixed-point skills, and skill acquisition is exactly the trajectory of \(\omega\) toward such fixed points.

The second claim is that the body schema is the identity \(e\) relative to motor synthesis. Every composition of motor acts is performed against the background of the body's pre-reflective sense of itself; without the body schema, no motor act could be initiated or coordinated. This is a T1 claim about the identity element being lived rather than conceptual.

The third claim is that \(\sigma \neq 0\) captures the irreducibility of figure-ground structure. Perception always carves the perceptual field into a definite figure on an indeterminate ground; this carving is structural, not optional. The algebra cannot represent perception in a flat-curvature regime. This is a T5 claim grounded in Gestalt psychology.

The fourth claim concerns motor intentionality: meanings are grasped through bodily engagement before any thematic representation. \(\rho\) at the motor level operates prior to and independently of \(\rho\) at the conceptual level. This is a T2 claim about a non-cognitive layer of resonance, which the formalism allows by partitioning \(\rho\) by act-type.

The fifth claim is the chiasm of the late ontology: the touching is also touched, the seer is also seen. Algebraically, certain elements of \({\mathcal I}\) carry a duality structure under which they appear in both the agent role and the patient role of the same act. This is a T8 claim about structural equivalence, with the further content that the equivalence is realized within a single locus.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the pre-reflective body schema becomes the monoid identity for motor synthesis. The phenomenological insight that the body is presupposed by every motor act gains a precise algebraic statement: \(e\) is non-trivially present in every \(\circ\) composition.

Second, the breakdown of the body schema (\(\nu\)) gives a structural account of pathological cases — phantom limb, Anton's syndrome, Schneider's apraxia. Each becomes a specific failure mode of the algebra, where \(\nu\) selectively damages parts of \({\mathcal I}\) while leaving the rest intact.

Third, the convergence of \(\omega^n\) predicts the phenomenology of skill acquisition. The early stages, characterized by halting attention and explicit rehearsal, correspond to high \(\sigma\); the late stages, characterized by automatic flow, correspond to fixed-point convergence under \(\omega\). The transition is structural, not merely psychological.

Fourth, the figure-ground irreducibility is rendered as the impossibility of decomposing \(\circ\)-products into context-free atoms. Every motor act is figure-against-ground; any algebra that pretended to flatten this structure would distort the phenomenon. The formalism vindicates Merleau-Ponty's polemic against atomistic empiricism.

Fifth, the chiasmic structure of the late ontology is made structurally tractable as a duality on subsets of \({\mathcal I}\). The hand that touches and is touched corresponds to elements that occupy both the source and target of a specific operator simultaneously, and the formalism can investigate the consequences of such self-dual loci.

Where It Diverges

First, the lived body is not a set of elements but an ongoing self-organizing process; the formalism's static carrier set misses the dynamic constitution. \({\mathcal I}\) presents a snapshot; the lived body is the temporal unfolding that no snapshot captures.

Second, the felt quality of bodily experience — the thickness of perception, the density of one's own flesh, the proprioceptive intimacy with one's own movement — is captured only as scalar markers (\(\deg\)) on structural items. The phenomenological richness Merleau-Ponty insists upon is largely lost.

Third, the chiasm is more than a duality; it is an ontological intertwining that resists the subject-object distinction the algebra implicitly preserves. Treating reversibility as a self-dual locus retains the duality the chiasm dissolves.

Fourth, the intersubjective dimension — the perception of another lived body as another perceiver — requires multiple algebras with constitutive cross-mappings. The bare formalism handles only one body's algebra at a time, and the intercorporeality essential to Merleau-Ponty's social phenomenology requires extra structure.

Fifth, the political and aesthetic resonances of the late Merleau-Ponty — his readings of Cézanne, his engagement with Marxism, his account of speech as the institution of meaning — depend on substantive cultural content the algebra cannot internally generate.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Merleau-Ponty's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the embodied perceptual field: bodily schema, motor possibilities, visible profiles, intercorporeal encounters, and the flesh of the world. Morphisms are sensorimotor translations that carry an object from one bodily orientation to another without reducing perception to judgment. π is embodied perspective, ι is motor intentionality, τ is lived temporal thickness, and ψ marks affective attunement in perception.

This is T13: perceptual objectivity is the gluing of embodied profiles across possible movements and shared bodily horizons.

Hans-Georg Gadamer

1900–2002 · Hermeneuticist

The Position

Gadamer's Truth and Method argues that understanding is always situated within tradition. We approach any text or other to be interpreted with prejudgments (Vorurteile) — fore-meanings inherited from our linguistic and cultural formation — and these are not obstacles to be eliminated but the very condition that makes understanding possible.

The hermeneutic circle is therefore not a vicious circularity to be escaped but the structure of understanding itself. We project a meaning for the whole on the basis of a prior expectation; the parts confirm or revise that projection; the revised whole reorganizes our reading of the parts. Understanding is the productive movement within this circle.

Interpretation is best understood as a fusion of horizons. The interpreter's horizon, formed by her place in tradition, encounters the horizon of the text, formed by its place in another tradition; understanding is the new horizon that emerges when the two are brought into dialogue. Neither horizon is overcome; each is enlarged by encounter with the other.

Tradition (Wirkungsgeschichte, the history of effects) is not what stands behind us but what we belong to. We are always already inside a tradition of interpretation, and the texts we read have already shaped the language and concepts with which we read them. Historical consciousness is the awareness of this belonging, not a vantage above it.

The model of understanding is conversation, not method. Gadamer resists the application of natural-scientific method to the human sciences; he insists that understanding is more like a dialogue in which the matter at issue (die Sache) makes its own claim on us than like an experimental procedure with controllable parameters. Application (Anwendung) is not a step that follows interpretation but an essential moment of it.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of meanings within a tradition: items as they have been articulated and re-articulated in the history of effects of texts and practices. Each element is tagged by its horizon of origin and its present interpretive context. The identity \(e\) is the open horizon — the not-yet-determinate space within which any interpretation can be projected and revised.

Composition \(\circ\) is horizon fusion: the merging of interpretive contexts that occurs when an interpreter and a text-meaning encounter each other. The fusion produces a new horizon that contains both inputs as moments and is itself open to further fusion.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is hermeneutic proximity: the extent to which two meanings share a tradition, share a vocabulary, share a presupposed background. Curvature \(\sigma\) is the curvature of prejudice — the fore-structure of understanding that makes interpretation possible while also tilting it. Gadamer's positive rehabilitation of prejudice is the claim that some \(\sigma \neq 0\) is enabling.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the alien horizon that challenges \(a\): the interpretation that calls \(a\)'s presuppositions into question, the foreign tradition that puts the familiar one in relief. Reframing \(\eta\) is application (Anwendung): the act of bringing meaning to bear on the present situation, which is internal to interpretation rather than a subsequent step.

Transmission \(\omega\) is Wirkungsgeschichte: the history of effects by which a text travels through generations, accumulating interpretations that shape subsequent readings. The grading \(\deg\) is depth of sedimentation in tradition, with surface readings at the bottom and meanings deeply integrated into a tradition's self-understanding at the top.

\[ a \circ_H b = \text{fusion}(a,b), \qquad \rho(a,b) = |\text{horizon}(a) \cap \text{horizon}(b)|, \qquad \sigma \neq 0 \text{ (productive prejudice)} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that horizon fusion is \(\circ\). Every interpretation is a composition of two horizons, and every act of understanding produces a new compound horizon. This is a T1 claim about composition, with the further content that the operation is constitutively dialogic.

The second claim is that tradition is \(\omega\)-transmission through history. \(\omega\) carries meanings forward through generations, accumulating Wirkungsgeschichte; the present interpretive situation is the cumulative result of \(\omega\)'s long action. This is a T7 claim about transmission chains with specifically historical carriers.

The third claim is that prejudice is the productive curvature \(\sigma \neq 0\) — irreducible and enabling. Without fore-structure, no interpretation could begin; with it, interpretation is biased and revisable. This is a T5 claim with a positive valence: asymmetry is constitutive, not corrupting, and the Enlightenment prejudice against prejudice is itself a prejudice.

The fourth claim is that the hermeneutic circle is the structural fact that \(\circ\) is not free. The whole and the parts of any meaning constrain each other; one cannot freely compose a part-interpretation without revising the whole-interpretation it presupposes. This is a T1 claim with a non-freeness condition.

The fifth claim is that application is internal to interpretation: \(\eta\) is an essential moment of \(\circ\), not a subsequent operation. The interpreter's situation always informs how the text speaks. This is a T6 claim that reframing is not optional postprocessing but constitutive of meaning-formation.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the hermeneutic circle becomes the algebraic claim that \(\circ\) is not free — context-dependent, with whole and part mutually constraining. The "circularity" that troubles methodologists is exhibited as the structural non-freeness of meaning-composition, with the bonus that productive non-freeness can be analyzed without dismissing the operation as ill-defined.

Second, the rehabilitation of prejudice becomes the assertion that \(\sigma \neq 0\) is enabling. Gadamer's polemic against the Enlightenment's "prejudice against prejudice" gains structural form: an algebra with everywhere-zero curvature would have no fore-structure, and therefore no projective capacity, and therefore no understanding.

Third, application \(\eta\) is given algebraic location as a reframing operator that maps to the present context. The notorious Gadamerian claim that we always understand "differently if at all" is the structural fact that \(\eta\) is non-trivially context-dependent.

Fourth, fusion of horizons becomes a definite composition rule: the result of \(a \circ b\) is neither \(a\) nor \(b\) nor their union but a new horizon of which both are now moments. This dignifies Gadamer's metaphor with a precise compositional content.

Fifth, Wirkungsgeschichte as \(\omega\)-transmission predicts the long-run distribution of interpretive weight: meanings that have accumulated more transmission are more deeply sedimented in present horizons, and certain canonical readings become quasi-fixed-points of the operator. The formalism explains why some interpretations are recovered easily while others require Destruktion-like effort.

Where It Diverges

First, the playfulness of the text — the text's own claim on the reader, the Sache that makes its own demand — is not a one-way \(\omega\) but a two-way event in Gadamer's account. The formalism's directed transmission misses this, treating the text as a passive carrier of meaning rather than an interlocutor.

Second, the dialogical character of understanding presupposes more than a single algebra. Authentic dialogue requires two horizons that can each genuinely surprise the other, and the formalism captures only one algebra's perspective at a time. Inter-horizon dialogue requires resources beyond the bare formalism.

Third, the model of conversation as the paradigm of understanding resists method-like formalization on principle. Gadamer's resistance to method is part of his content; offering an algebraic structure can therefore be read as a category mistake about what kind of theory hermeneutics ought to be.

Fourth, the linguisticality of all understanding ("Being that can be understood is language") embeds \({\mathcal I}\) in language in a way the bare formalism does not honor. The formalism takes meanings as primitives; Gadamer takes them as inseparable from their linguistic articulation.

Fifth, the normative dimension of the hermeneutic ethic — openness, humility, willingness to be addressed — is a stance toward the algebra rather than a feature of it. The formalism describes structures; it cannot enjoin the disposition that makes engaging with them fruitful.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Gadamer's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the historically effected field of text, interpreter, tradition, question, and application. Morphisms are fusions in which the interpreter's prejudgments and the text's claims form a pushout rather than a neutral reconstruction. χ is tradition as connected horizon, τ is Wirkungsgeschichte, and ι is the question-directedness that lets a text address the present.

This is paradigmatic T13: fusion of horizons is the colimit \(h_{\text{interpreter}}\sqcup h_{\text{text}}\), and understanding is the unique gluing of local readings on that cover.

Paul Ricoeur

1913–2005 · Hermeneuticist/Phenomenologist

The Position

Ricoeur's project occupies the long detour from immediate self-knowledge through the symbols, narratives, and institutions in which the self is articulated. We do not know ourselves directly; we know ourselves through the cultural mediations that interpret human existence — myth, metaphor, narrative, ideology, sacred text — and only by engaging these can we recover what self-understanding we attain.

His early Symbolism of Evil argues that "the symbol gives rise to thought": symbolic discourse, with its surplus of meaning, is the soil in which philosophical reflection takes root. Reductive readings that strip symbols to their literal residue lose the very surplus that makes them productive. A second naïveté must follow the first: we read symbols critically and yet remain open to what they say.

The hermeneutic of suspicion (Marx, Freud, Nietzsche) is balanced against a hermeneutic of restoration. Critique exposes false consciousness; restoration recovers the meaning that critique had to bracket. Philosophy moves between the two, never collapsing into either.

Narrative is, for the mature Ricoeur, the privileged mode of temporal self-understanding. The triple mimesis of Time and Narrative — the prefigured world of action, the configured world of plot, the refigured world of the reader's own life — exhibits how human beings make time intelligible by emplotment. Narrative identity is who one is when one's life is read as a story.

The late Ricoeur's Oneself as Another (Soi-même comme un autre) argues that the self is constituted in relation to alterity at every level: the body that is mine and yet other, the second person who addresses me, the other in conscience that calls me to account. There is no immediate self-presence; selfhood is always already mediated by what it is not.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of narrative meanings and symbols — items articulated through plot, metaphor, sacred text, or other cultural mediation. Each element is tagged by its narrative role (event, action, character, theme) and by its symbolic register. The identity \(e\) is the bare subject-pole that can be addressed and that can address itself, a placeholder that gains content only through narrative articulation.

Composition \(\circ\) is emplotment (mise en intrigue): the configuration of disparate events into a coherent narrative whole. Aristotle's mythos returns here as Ricoeur's central operation; \(\circ\) is constrained by the demand that the parts cohere in a unity of action with beginning, middle, and end.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is narrative coherence: how strongly two meanings serve as moments of a common emplotment. Curvature \(\sigma\) is the curvature of the hermeneutic arc — the productive tension between structural explanation (which treats meaning as a closed system) and lived understanding (which engages meaning as a possibility for one's own existence).

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the counter-narrative or distanciation of \(a\): the alternative emplotment, the suspicious reading, the moment in which the meaning is held at arm's length to be examined. Reframing \(\eta\) is appropriation: the act by which an interpreted meaning becomes one's own, taken up into the projects of self-understanding.

Transmission \(\omega\) is narrative transmission across time: the way stories travel, are retold, and shape the self-understandings of those who inherit them. The grading \(\deg\) is narrative integration: episode \( < \) emplotted sequence \( < \) life-narrative \( < \) inter-personal narrative web.

\[ \omega(n_1 \circ n_2) = \omega(n_1) \circ \omega(n_2) \;\;(\text{narrative transmission as homomorphism}), \qquad e = \text{addressable subject-pole} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that emplotment is the central composition \(\circ\). To make sense of human time, one must compose events into plots; \(\circ\) is the algebraic content of the Aristotelian mythos. This is a T1 claim with the additional constraint that not all compositions are admissible: a plot must satisfy concordance-discordance constraints.

The second claim is that the hermeneutic arc is the curvature \(\sigma\) between structural explanation and lived understanding. Pure structuralism flattens \(\sigma\) (no understanding); pure existential engagement abandons it (no explanation). Ricoeur's middle path keeps \(\sigma\) productive. This is a T5 claim about asymmetry as the condition of interpretive depth.

The third claim is that narrative identity is the fixed point of the \(\omega\)-transmission of one's own story. To be a self is to inherit a stream of narratives, to retell them, and to converge — never finally — on a recognizable life-shape. This is a T7 claim with the further content that the fixed point is not static but a moving target.

The fourth claim is that critique and restoration are dual operators on \({\mathcal I}\): \(\nu\) (suspicious distanciation) and \(\eta\) (appropriative re-engagement) form a pair, and authentic interpretation iterates between them. This is a T6 claim about reframing in two directions, with the algebraic content that neither alone is sufficient.

The fifth claim, from Oneself as Another, is that the self is constituted by alterity at every level. Algebraically, \(e\) is not a free-standing identity element but is itself a composite that includes \(\nu(e)\) as a constitutive moment. This is a T1-with-\(\nu\) claim of unusual reflexivity: identity is internally mediated by otherness.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the concordance-discordance of narrative becomes the algebraic claim that \(\circ\) is not fully associative — the order of emplotment matters, and rearranging episodes can disrupt the unity of the plot. This makes narrative time non-commutative in a precise structural sense.

Second, the metaphorical surplus of meaning ("the symbol gives rise to thought") becomes the assertion that \(\rho\)-distances in the symbolic register are not exhausted by literal-paraphrase distances. Symbols connect items the literal language separates, and the algebra makes the surplus measurable.

Third, the duality of suspicion and restoration becomes a precise pair of operators that can be iterated. The formalism shows that interpretation is not a one-shot procedure but a structured oscillation, with stable interpretations as the joint fixed points of repeated \(\nu\)-then-\(\eta\) cycles.

Fourth, the threefold mimesis of Time and Narrative maps onto a layered structure: prefigured world (the pre-narrative \({\mathcal I}\)), configured world (after \(\circ\)-emplotment), refigured world (after \(\eta\)-appropriation by the reader). The structural picture clarifies how a text's meaning passes through three transformations between author and reader.

Fifth, narrative identity as a moving fixed point clarifies why selfhood is both stable and revisable. The orbit under \(\omega\) converges on a coherent self-shape without ever finalizing it; the self is what the trajectory looks like, not where it lands.

Where It Diverges

First, the fragility of human identity — the deep alterity that haunts the self in Oneself as Another — involves an irreducible otherness that \(\nu\) captures only schematically. The other is not just a counter-narrative; it is a face that addresses me, a conscience that calls me, a body that is mine and yet not mine. The algebra elides these registers.

Second, the ethical and political dimension of Ricoeur's late work — the just institution, the capable human being, memory and forgiveness — depends on substantive normative content the algebra cannot generate. Narrative identity is supposed to support an ethics; the formalism describes the structure but cannot enjoin the responsibility.

Third, the engagement with the social sciences (psychoanalysis, structural anthropology, history) that animates much of Ricoeur's middle period requires inter-disciplinary translation rules the bare formalism does not contain.

Fourth, the religious dimension of Ricoeur's work — his reading of biblical hermeneutics, his thought on evil and forgiveness — operates with theological resources outside the algebra.

Fifth, the patient, mediating tone of Ricoeur's prose — his refusal to settle on either pole of an opposition, his insistence on the long detour — is itself a substantive philosophical commitment that the algebraic schematism necessarily flattens. Ricoeur's method is itself an ethic of interpretation that the formalism merely describes.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Ricoeur's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the hermeneutic arc from lived action, symbol, text, distanciation, explanation, and reappropriated self-understanding. Morphisms move from prefiguration through configuration to refiguration, especially in narrative time. τ is narrative temporality, R is the self interpreted through signs, ι is reference opened by the text's world, and χ marks symbolic traditions.

This is T13: interpretation glues explanatory, textual, and existential horizons so that the world of the text refigures the reader's own horizon.

Post-Structuralism

Ferdinand de Saussure

1857–1913 · Structural Linguist

The Position

Saussure's posthumous Cours de linguistique générale founds modern linguistics by treating language as a system to be studied in its own right rather than as a window onto thought or a fossil of historical change. Where nineteenth-century philology had sought the laws of phonetic evolution, Saussure asks what makes a language a language at any given moment.

The fundamental unit is the linguistic sign, a two-sided psychological entity: a signifier (a sound-image) coupled with a signified (a concept). The relation between the two is arbitrary — there is nothing in the sound-image of "tree" that selects the concept tree — and yet, once instituted within a community, the relation is constitutive of meaning.

Language as system (langue) is to be distinguished from language as use (parole). Langue is the social institution: the abstract system of relations shared by a speech community. Parole is the individual act of speech. Linguistics, for Saussure, takes langue as its proper object because only langue exhibits systematic structure.

The synchronic study of langue is to be sharply separated from the diachronic study of language change. The two axes have different objects and different methods. Saussure's polemical insistence on synchrony was a corrective to a discipline that had reduced language to its history; it was not the denial that language has a history.

The cardinal slogan is that language is a system of differences without positive terms. The value of any sign is determined entirely by its differential position relative to other signs in the system. Meaning is purely relational; nothing in a sign carries content of its own apart from how it stands to its neighbors.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of signs, each a (signifier, signified) pair within the synchronic system langue. Each element is tagged by phonemic, morphemic, lexical, or syntactic level. The identity \(e\) is the empty position — the slot in the system that admits any sign of the appropriate category, the differential placeholder against which substitution can be tested.

Composition \(\circ\) is syntagmatic combination: the linear concatenation of signs into morphemes, words, phrases, sentences. \(\circ\) is constrained by the syntactic rules of the langue; not every combination is admissible, and the admissibility conditions are themselves part of the system.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is paradigmatic opposition — purely differential. \(\rho(a,a) = 0\) by definition: a sign has no value relative to itself. \(\rho\) is high precisely between signs that occupy nearby paradigmatic positions and that thereby contrast in the system.

Curvature \(\sigma\) is the curvature of the arbitrary relation between signifier and signified: there is no continuous deformation of the signifier into the signified, and the relation must be maintained by social convention. Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the differential opposite of \(a\) in the system — the sign that occupies the contrasting paradigmatic slot.

Reframing \(\eta\) is the commutation test: the operator that substitutes one sign for another to test whether a difference of meaning results, thereby establishing the relevant oppositions of the system. Transmission \(\omega\) is diachronic change: the slow evolution of the langue across generations. The grading \(\deg\) is syntagmatic rank — phoneme below morpheme below word below phrase below sentence.

\[ \rho(a,a) = 0, \qquad \rho(a,b) > 0 \iff a \neq b \text{ contrast in some paradigm} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that meaning is purely relational: \(\rho(a,a) = 0\). The value of any sign is exhausted by its differential relations to other signs; there is no surplus self-identical content. This is a T2 claim of unusual strength — resonance is not an enrichment of identity but a replacement for it.

The second claim is that the sign is arbitrary: \(\sigma \neq 0\) for the signifier-signified relation. There is no natural bridge between sound-image and concept; the connection is sustained by the system itself. This is a T5 claim with social ontological backing: the asymmetry between signifier and signified is irreducible.

The third claim is that langue is a monoid under syntagmatic composition. \(\circ\) operates on the differential values of signs and produces new differential values; the entire system is closed under this operation. This is a T1 claim with the further restriction that \(\circ\) is rule-governed at every level.

The fourth claim is the priority of synchrony: at any given time, the system is to be analyzed without reference to its history. Algebraically, the algebra at time \(t\) is fully determined by its synchronic data; \(\omega\) operates between time-slices but does not enter the synchronic value of any sign. This is a T7 claim with strong locality.

The fifth claim is the social ontology of langue: the system is the property of a community, not of any individual. Algebraically, \({\mathcal I}\) is shared across speakers; parole is each speaker's individual realization. This is a structural claim with sociological force: the algebra is communal even when its instances are individual.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the differential definition of meaning becomes an off-diagonal resonance metric — value only between distinct elements. The Saussurean slogan that there are no positive terms gains structural backing: \(\rho\) is concentrated entirely off the diagonal.

Second, the langue/parole distinction maps onto the difference between the algebra's structural relations and the actual elements that appear in any speech act. The structure is what is shared; the realizations are what individuals contribute.

Third, the syntagmatic and paradigmatic axes correspond to two different operations on \({\mathcal I}\): \(\circ\) (combination) and \(\rho\) (substitutability). The two famous Saussurean axes are distinguished as composition versus resonance.

Fourth, the commutation test as \(\eta\) becomes the operational procedure by which the algebra's structural relations are actually discovered. Saussure's methodology is given an algebraic correlate: the structuralist linguist's substitution-and-test is reframing under \(\eta\).

Fifth, the constraint that \(\circ\) is rule-governed clarifies the difference between systemic and unsystematic combinations. Not every concatenation is a syntagm; the algebra has admissibility conditions, and the discovery of those conditions is the linguist's task.

Where It Diverges

First, Saussure's system is fundamentally synchronic; the algebra's \(\omega\) introduces diachrony as an additional structure rather than holding it strictly outside synchronic analysis. The methodological purity of synchrony is harder to maintain when \(\omega\) is part of the same algebra.

Second, the langue/parole distinction is not crisply captured by the bare formalism. Treating \({\mathcal I}\) as the langue and individual usage as a labeling on instantiations does not honor the ontological difference between system and act.

Third, the social-institutional character of langue — its existence as the property of a community — has no internal algebraic counterpart. The algebra is itself a single object; that the same algebra is shared by many speakers is a fact outside the algebra.

Fourth, the semiological project Saussure sketches — extending sign-theory to all systems of signs (etiquette, fashion, ritual) — requires a way of identifying which structures count as semiological and which do not. The algebra applies indifferently to anything with the right shape, losing the substantive criterion.

Fifth, the speaker's psychological reality of the sign as a "psychological imprint" sits uncomfortably with the algebra's structural neutrality. The signifier is not a pure structural slot but a felt sound-image; phenomenology returns where structuralism wanted to leave it behind.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Saussure's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the synchronic language-system, with parole-events and diachronic shifts as related but methodologically distinct horizons. Morphisms translate sign-values only within a system of differences; a sign cannot carry its value unchanged outside the structure that opposes it to others. δ is decisive as negative differential value, χ is langue as connected schema, and τ is bracketed when synchronic analysis is primary.

This is T14: signification fails to pull back across language-systems because \(\sigma_h\) is constituted by local differences, not positive substance.

Jacques Derrida

1930–2004 · Deconstructionist

The Position

Derrida's deconstruction targets what he calls the metaphysics of presence: the long Western assumption that meaning, being, truth, and the self are immediately present to themselves in some founding moment. Speech is taken as more present than writing, intuition more present than mediation, signified more present than signifier; deconstruction denies that any such founding presence is ever achievable.

The core neologism is différance, with an "a" — the simultaneous deferral and differentiation that constitutes meaning. Every sign means what it means by virtue of differential relations to other signs; and every sign defers full presence to other signs that themselves defer to others. There is no end to this chain at any present moment.

Every text harbors aporias — points at which it undoes its own central claims. Deconstructive reading does not impose contradictions from outside but draws out the contradictions the text already houses. The hierarchies the text relies upon (speech over writing, presence over absence, original over supplement) are exposed as unstable inversions of what they suppress.

Iterability is the structural condition that any sign must be repeatable in absence of its original speaker, hearer, and context. This iterability is what makes signs functional and what guarantees that no context fully fixes meaning. The "supplement" — what is added to make up for an alleged lack — turns out to constitute what it is added to.

Derrida's later writings turn to ethical and political registers — hospitality, justice, the gift, mourning, friendship, religion — bringing the same operations to bear on inherited concepts.
