Second, the picture theory presupposes a notion of logical form shared between proposition and fact that the algebra does not internally contain. The algebra's elements are propositional; facts are extra-algebraic, and the isomorphism that gives meaning lives at the interface.

Third, the elementary propositions on which the entire structure rests were never actually exhibited by Wittgenstein; their existence is a postulate. The algebra inherits this postulate: it presupposes a basis of atomic elements whose existence is assumed rather than demonstrated.

Fourth, the ethical and aesthetic — declared mystical — are arguably not just outside the algebra but operative in any actual life, including the lives of those who use the algebra. The formalism's silence on these is a structural fact, not a refutation of their importance.

Fifth, the self-undermining final move resists structural reconstruction. To honor it, the formalism would have to declare itself nonsensical at exactly the moment it is fully articulated — a performative gesture an algebra cannot enact.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

The early Wittgenstein's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the austere picture-theoretic space of facts, elementary propositions, logical form, and the limit of language. Morphisms project propositions onto possible states of affairs when they share pictorial form; ethical, aesthetic, and metaphysical utterances lack such admissible projection. β binds names to objects, χ is logical space, and R appears when the book's own propositions must be thrown away.

This is T14: what can be shown does not pull back into sayable propositions, so metaphysical sense fails at the boundary of \(\mathcal{H}\).

Ludwig Wittgenstein (Investigations)

1889–1951 · Ordinary Language

The Position

The Philosophical Investigations (1953) repudiates the picture theory of the Tractatus. Meaning is not a relation of word to object or proposition to fact but a feature of use within a practice. To understand a word is to know how it is used; to ask for its meaning apart from its use is to be misled by a grammatical picture.

Language games are the units of analysis: small, complete forms of language-with-action that exhibit how words function. The slab-game of the builders, the giving-and-following of orders, the reporting of pain — each is a language game with its own internal grammar. Natural languages are families of language games woven together.

Family resemblance replaces essential definition. There is no single feature that all "games" share; instead, games are linked by overlapping, criss-crossing similarities, like the resemblances among members of a family. Most concepts function this way; the search for necessary and sufficient conditions is misdirected.

The private language argument denies that there could be a language whose words refer to private sensations knowable only to the speaker. Without public criteria, the speaker could not distinguish using a word correctly from merely thinking she was using it correctly — and a distinction we cannot make is no distinction at all.

Philosophy is therapeutic: its task is to dissolve, not solve, the philosophical problems that arise when language goes on holiday. By describing how words actually function in their natural homes, philosophy releases us from the captivity of misleading pictures. There are no philosophical theses; if there were, everyone would agree to them.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of language games: practices in which words and actions are interwoven into complete forms of life. Each element is tagged by the game it belongs to, the role it plays in that game, and the criteria of correct use that govern it. The identity \(e\) is the empty practice-position — the slot a contribution to a language game fills.

Composition \(\circ\) is language-game combination: the way smaller games are interwoven into larger forms of life. \(\circ\) is constrained by the forms of life themselves; not every combination is a recognizable practice, and the constraints are themselves part of the form of life.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is family resemblance: an overlap measure of features shared between practices, with no requirement that any single feature be common to all. Curvature \(\sigma = 0\) for within-game use: each game is internally clear, and apparent depth or paradox is the residue of mixing games or holidaying outside any game.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the language game that exposes the idle running of \(a\): the contrast case that brings out where \(a\) was pretending to function but was not actually doing work. Reframing \(\eta\) is grammatical investigation: the description of how a word actually functions in its native game.

Transmission \(\omega\) is teaching and learning: how language games are transmitted by training, by example, by initiation into a form of life. The grading \(\deg\) is largely refused: anti-essentialism resists hierarchical ordering, and what counts as a more-fundamental game varies from purpose to purpose.

\[ \rho(a,b) = |\{f : f \text{ is a feature shared by } a \text{ and } b\}| \;\;(\text{family resemblance, no essence required}) \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that meaning is use: there is no element of \({\mathcal I}\) apart from its role in \(\circ\). To pull a word out of its language game and ask for its meaning in isolation is to encounter no meaning at all. This is a T1 claim with extreme holism: identity of an element is exhausted by its compositional role.

The second claim is that family resemblance is \(\rho\) as an overlap count, not an essence. \(\rho\) does not require any single property; it tracks the shifting overlaps that connect practices into families. This is a T2 claim with anti-essentialist content: similarity does not presuppose shared essence.

The third claim is that philosophy is \(\eta\): the reminding of how words actually work, the description that releases us from misleading pictures. There are no philosophical discoveries, only philosophical clarifications. This is a T6 claim with therapeutic intent.

The fourth claim is the private language argument: \({\mathcal I}\) requires a public \(\circ\) to be well-defined. A would-be private language has no public criteria of correctness, and so its purported elements are not stably identifiable. This is a structural claim with deep consequences for philosophy of mind.

The fifth claim is that forms of life are the limit of justification. When asked why we go on as we do — why we extend a series this way rather than that, why we apply a word this way rather than that — eventually the spade is turned: this is simply what we do. This is a structural claim about where the algebra terminates: \(\omega\) bottoms out in agreement in form of life, not in further reasons.

What the Formalism Adds

First, family resemblance becomes a precise \(\rho\)-metric without essential properties. The intuition that some concepts hang together without sharing an essence gains an algebraic correlate: \(\rho\) is overlap-based rather than membership-in-a-class-based.

Second, the private language argument becomes the assertion that \({\mathcal I}\) requires a public \(\circ\) to be well-defined — a social condition on the monoid. The argument's structural form is that without a public composition, there are no determinate elements to be composed.

Third, the rejection of essences is the structural claim that \(\circ\) does not factor through any privileged set of features. There is no canonical decomposition of practices into essential and accidental components; the algebra resists the essence/accident distinction.

Fourth, the therapeutic conception of philosophy becomes the algebraic claim that \(\eta\) does not produce new elements but reframes existing ones. Philosophy adds nothing to \({\mathcal I}\); it rearranges what is already there to make its workings visible.

Fifth, the bottoming-out of \(\omega\) in form of life clarifies that the algebra is not self-grounding. Its elements and operations rest on practices that the algebra describes but does not justify, and the demand for further justification is itself a misuse of the algebra.

Where It Diverges

First, the anti-theoretical stance of the Investigations directly opposes building a theory; the formalism produces exactly the kind of theory Wittgenstein II opposes. To present an algebra of language games risks performing the very mistake the book diagnoses.

Second, the piecemeal, conversational, dialectical style of the Investigations is part of its content. The algebra's systematic exposition flattens what Wittgenstein deliberately presents as a series of remarks intended to provoke rather than to demonstrate.

Third, the absence of theses in Wittgenstein's later philosophy is in tension with the theorem-shaped claims the algebra invites. To claim that "meaning is use" as a theorem risks turning a reminder into a doctrine.

Fourth, the irreducibility of natural language to a single formal system is a Wittgensteinian commitment that the algebra honors only by being open about its own limits. Any actual algebra is a model of certain features and a distortion of others.

Fifth, the role of training, initiation, and the lived sense of the form of life as preconditions of meaning resists being captured by the algebra's structural relations. To engage with the algebra is already to be inside a form of life the algebra cannot fully describe.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

The later Wittgenstein's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the discrete-up-to-family-resemblance category of language-games and forms of life. Morphisms are partial overlaps in use, training, and criteria, not hidden common essences; meaning is intra-horizonal and public. χ is the form of life, π is the participant trained into rule-following, δ is contrastive use, and private-language fantasy is a failed singleton horizon.

This is paradigmatic T14: cross-game inference fails, and the private-language argument denies that any ξ-section over an isolated private horizon is even definable.

Willard Van Orman Quine

1908–2000 · Naturalist/Holist

The Position

Quine's "Two Dogmas of Empiricism" (1951) attacks the analytic-synthetic distinction and the verificationist atomism on which the early logical positivists relied. There is no principled way to separate truths in virtue of meaning from truths in virtue of fact; analyticity, once examined, dissolves into a circle of equally suspect notions.

The positive thesis is confirmation holism: the unit of empirical significance is the whole of science, not individual statements. When a theory clashes with experience, no single statement is uniquely refuted; we may save any statement at the cost of revising others, and the overall web of belief is what is empirically constrained.

Ontology is relative to a theory. The objects to which a theory is committed are those over which its quantifiers must range to make its statements true; different theories carry different ontological commitments, and there is no theory-independent fact about what really exists. Translation is similarly indeterminate: distinct manuals can do equally well, with no fact of the matter about which is correct.

Epistemology is naturalized: the study of how we acquire knowledge becomes a chapter of empirical psychology, freed from the foundationalist project of grounding knowledge in indubitable starting points. Indispensability arguments support a moderate Platonism about mathematical entities: we should accept the existence of those abstracta that our best scientific theories quantify over.

Quine's vision is austere and naturalistic: a thoroughgoing scientific philosophy with no appeal to meanings, intentions, or modal essences. Where these notions cannot be regimented, they are to be regretfully discarded. Logic and mathematics are continuous with empirical science, distinguished only by their centrality and resistance to revision.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of sentences of a total scientific theory: items as standing claims integrated into the web of belief. Each element is tagged by its centrality (logical, mathematical, theoretical, observational) and by its empirical exposure. The identity \(e\) is the trivially-true sentence — what every theory accepts because it makes no claim.

Composition \(\circ\) is deductive closure plus integration: the addition of sentences to the web in such a way that the new web remains coherent. \(\circ\) is constrained by the requirement that the resulting set be consistent and adequate to the data the theory must accommodate.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is degree of mutual dependence in the web of belief: how strongly the truth of \(a\) is implicated by the truth of \(b\) and vice versa. Curvature \(\sigma\) is the curvature of underdetermination: multiple theories fit the same total data, and the choice among them is not fixed by the data alone.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is recalcitrant experience: an observation that conflicts with \(a\) and pressures revision. Reframing \(\eta\) is regimentation: the translation of ordinary or theoretical statements into the canonical first-order logical notation that exposes their structure for scientific assessment.

Transmission \(\omega\) is web-revision under empirical pressure: when recalcitrance hits, \(\omega\) propagates adjustments through the web, with peripheral statements typically revised before central ones. The grading \(\deg\) is centrality: logic and mathematics most central, then highly theoretical statements, then observation reports at the periphery.

\[ \sigma \neq 0 \;\;(\text{underdetermination}): \;\exists\, {\mathcal I}' \neq {\mathcal I},\; \rho({\mathcal I}', \text{data}) = \rho({\mathcal I}, \text{data}) \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is holism: every sentence's content depends on all others via \(\rho\). There are no isolated atoms of content; meaning is global. This is a T2 claim with maximal scope.

The second claim is that the web of belief is the connected algebra under \(\circ\). The total theory is one connected structure, and changes anywhere can in principle propagate everywhere. This is a T1 claim with strong global connectivity.

The third claim is underdetermination: \(\sigma \neq 0\) at the level of total theory. Multiple total theories are empirically equivalent, and the residual asymmetry between them — which one to adopt — is not closable by data. This is a T5 claim with epistemological force.

The fourth claim is the rejection of analyticity: there is no sub-algebra of analytic truths separable from the rest. \({\mathcal I}\) is not partitionable into the meaning-true and the fact-true; the partition has no principled location. This is a structural claim about the algebra's lack of an analytic/synthetic decomposition.

The fifth claim is naturalized epistemology: the study of how the algebra is acquired and revised is itself a chapter of the algebra. Epistemology is not prior to the rest of inquiry but continuous with it; the algebra includes its own theory of its own acquisition. This is a structural reflexivity claim with anti-foundationalist consequences.

What the Formalism Adds

First, the centrality \(\deg\) gives precise content to Quine's claim that logic and mathematics are more central. Higher \(\deg\) means more resistant to revision; the inertia of the central is structurally measured.

Second, ontological relativity is the assertion that \({\mathcal I}\) and \({\mathcal I}'\) can be proxy-related while being non-isomorphic with respect to the objects their quantifiers range over. The algebra exhibits the relativity as a structural fact: theories with the same observational consequences may differ in their algebraic shape.

Third, the indeterminacy of translation becomes the absence of a unique \(\eta\) mapping one theory's regimentation onto another's. Multiple regimentations preserve empirical content equally well; \(\eta\) is many-valued in a controlled sense.

Fourth, the Duhem-Quine thesis on confirmation becomes the structural fact that \(\nu\) does not localize: a recalcitrant observation propagates pressure through the web rather than refuting any one element. The algebra makes the holistic character of confirmation operational.

Fifth, the indispensability argument for mathematical objects becomes the structural claim that the sub-algebra of mathematical statements cannot be excised without disrupting the rest of the web. The mathematical entities are committed-to because the structural integration requires them.

Where It Diverges

First, Quine's behaviorism about meaning — meaning as stimulus meaning, accessible only through observable response to stimuli — is not captured by the algebraic \(\rho\), which is theory-internal. The algebra describes the web; the behaviorist account of how we get into the web requires extra-algebraic resources.

Second, Quine's sweeping eliminativism about modality, intentionality, and abstract meanings is more than the algebra requires. The structural facts can be honored without the metaphysical denial; nothing in the algebra forces Quine's revisionary austerity.

Third, the indeterminacy of translation, taken at its strongest, threatens the very identity of the algebra. If there are no facts of the matter about reference, then \({\mathcal I}\) is not uniquely individuated, and the algebra wobbles. The formalism handles this by parameterizing, but Quine's claim is more radical than parameterization captures.

Fourth, the seamless continuity of philosophy with science that Quine demands sits in tension with the algebra's status as a meta-level structural account. The algebra is not part of the empirical web; describing the web requires standing somewhere outside it.

Fifth, Quine's preference for desert landscapes — austere ontology, sparse posits, suspicion of the intensional — is a methodological taste the algebra does not enforce. Other workings of the formalism (richer in modality, in intentionality, in higher types) are equally consistent with the structural content; Quine's specific philosophical sensibility is an extra commitment.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Quine's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the web of belief under sensory stimulation, translation manuals, ontological commitments, and regimentation into canonical notation. Morphisms revise the web holistically or translate another language by matching stimulus meanings, but there is no unique fact selecting one manual. χ is the conceptual scheme only insofar as it is a connected web, μ is empirical confidence distributed over the web, and β is relativised to background theory.

This is T14: translation and ontology fail to pull back uniquely across manuals, with indeterminacy appearing as a first cohomological obstruction in interpretation.

Jerry Fodor

1935–2017 · Computational/Representationalist

The Position

Fodor argues for a representational and computational theory of mind. Cognitive processes are formal operations performed over syntactically structured mental representations, and these representations form a language of thought (Mentalese) with the productivity, systematicity, and compositionality that natural languages display.

The central argument from systematicity is that anyone who can think aRb can also think bRa: thoughts come in systematically related families, and the best explanation of this fact is that thoughts have constituent structure — they are built up from atomic concepts by combinatorial operations.

Modularity of mind, in The Modularity of Mind (1983), holds that perception and language processing are accomplished by encapsulated, domain-specific, mandatory modules. Higher cognition (central systems) is, by contrast, isotropic and Quinean — and therefore, Fodor pessimistically concludes, less amenable to scientific theorizing.

Content, in Fodor's later work, is determined by causal-informational relations to the world rather than by inferential role within a system. The thought "horse" is about horses because tokenings of HORSE in Mentalese are caused, under the right conditions, by horses. This asymmetric dependency theory of content is meant to vindicate a robust externalist semantics without lapsing into holism.

Fodor's polemics — against pragmatism, against connectionism, against Wittgensteinian use-theories, against radical concept atomism's discontents — are part of the position. He defends an unfashionable picture of mind as classical, architecturally structured, and richly innate, against what he saw as the misguided drift of much contemporary cognitive science.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of mental representations in Mentalese: items as syntactically structured tokens in a language of thought. Each element is tagged by its constituent structure, its semantic content, and its computational role. The identity \(e\) is the trivial representation — the empty syntactic frame waiting for content.

Composition \(\circ\) is syntactic composition: the construction of complex representations from atomic concepts via phrase-structure-like combinatorial operations. \(\circ\) preserves structural relations needed to support productivity and systematicity.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is inferential role: how much \(a\) licenses the same inferences as \(b\) within the cognitive economy. Curvature \(\sigma\) is the curvature of content: non-zero, since narrow content (functional role) and wide content (causal-informational) come apart.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the mental representation incompatible with \(a\): a definite computational item that figures in the cognitive system's inferences and conflicts. Reframing \(\eta\) is computational process: the formal symbol manipulation that transforms one representation into another according to syntactic rules sensitive only to form.

Transmission \(\omega\) is belief revision and inference: the temporal evolution of the cognitive system as it adds, modifies, and discards representations under the pressure of new input and inferential demands. The grading \(\deg\) is syntactic complexity: atomic concepts at the bottom, molecular concepts higher, propositional attitudes higher still.

\[ \eta(a \circ b) = \eta(a) \circ \eta(b) \;\;(\text{compositionality of mental representations}) \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is compositionality: \(\eta\) is a homomorphism. The semantic content of a complex representation is determined by the contents of its constituents and the way they are combined. This is a T6 and T1 claim jointly: reframing preserves \(\circ\)-structure.

The second claim is that the language of thought is a free monoid generated by atomic concepts. Productivity (infinitely many possible thoughts from finite means) and systematicity (related families of thoughts) are the structural consequences of free generation. This is a T1 plus T3 claim with strong combinatorial implications.

The third claim is that content is wide: \(\rho\) tracks causal-informational connections to the world, not just inferential role internal to the system. This is a T2 claim with externalist content: the semantic relation reaches outside the algebra.

The fourth claim is modularity: certain sub-algebras of \({\mathcal I}\) are encapsulated — operations within the module do not draw on representations outside it, and operations outside cannot intrude on the module's processing. This is a T1 claim with strong locality on certain sub-algebras.

The fifth claim is conceptual atomism: lexical concepts have no internal structure; what makes a concept the concept it is is its causal-informational locking onto its denotation, not its definitional decomposition. This is a structural claim against decomposition: many elements of \({\mathcal I}\) are atoms, not constructed by \(\circ\).

What the Formalism Adds

First, Fodor's productivity argument becomes the free-monoid property of \(\circ\): finite atoms plus structured composition yield an infinite set of constructible representations, and the systematicity of related thoughts is a structural consequence.

Second, the formalism makes explicit that compositionality requires \(\eta\) to be a strict homomorphism — failures of compositionality are failures of T6. Pragmatic effects, idiomaticity, and context-sensitivity that violate strict compositionality become structural deviations from the homomorphic ideal.

Third, modularity becomes the structural assertion that certain sub-algebras of \({\mathcal I}\) admit \(\circ\)-closure restricted to their own elements: representations within the module cannot interact with arbitrary other representations. The Fodorian modularity thesis is given structural form.

Fourth, the wide-content thesis becomes the assertion that \(\rho\) is anchored in extra-algebraic causal relations. The algebra cannot be self-contained; semantic facts require an interpretation map outside the algebra.

Fifth, the asymmetric dependence theory of content becomes a structural condition on which causal links count: \(a\)'s content is fixed by certain privileged causal connections that are themselves the source of all other actual causal connections to \(a\). The structural shape of asymmetric dependence is exhibited algebraically.

Where It Diverges

First, the symbol grounding problem — how Mentalese gets its content — requires connecting \({\mathcal I}\) to a causal theory outside the algebra; the formalism cannot host intentionality without such an extension. The algebra describes structure; content must be hooked into the world by some additional theory.

Second, the radical innateness of concepts that Fodor late in his career endorsed (most lexical concepts are innate) is a substantive empirical commitment the algebra does not require. The formalism is consistent with innateness, learning, or any mixture; Fodor's specific position is independent.

Third, the pessimism about central cognition — the claim that central inferential processes are too holistic for cognitive science to model — sits in tension with the algebra's optimism about systematic structure. The algebra captures what Fodor agrees can be captured; it is silent about the holistic remainder he leaves to philosophy.

Fourth, the polemical engagement with rivals (connectionism, embodied cognition, predictive processing, dynamicism) cannot be settled by the algebra. The structural facts the formalism captures are compatible with multiple architectures; Fodor's specific architectural commitments are extra.

Fifth, the lived intentionality of conscious thought — the felt aboutness of an experience, the sense of meaning something — is not captured by structural facts about Mentalese tokens. The algebra describes the syntax and the externalist semantics; the phenomenology of intentionality remains a hard problem.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Fodor's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the computational architecture of modules, central cognition, mental representations, and causal-informational links to the world. Morphisms preserve syntactic structure across computations while attempting to secure semantic content by nomic relations. ι is mental aboutness, β is the atomistic concept as stable symbol, χ marks modular domains, and T14 pressures arise where holism threatens compositionality.

This is T15 for the language of thought: productivity and systematicity require representational types stable across indefinitely many computational horizons.

Robert Brandom

1950– · Inferentialist/Pragmatist

The Position

Brandom's Making It Explicit (1994) develops a systematic inferentialism: meaning is inferential role, and to understand a concept is to know what inferences it licenses, what commitments entitle one to it, and what other commitments it precludes. Representational notions are derivative from inferential ones, not the other way around.

The pragmatist starting point is that linguistic practice is essentially the giving and asking for reasons. To assert is to undertake a commitment whose entitlement can be challenged, defended, and acknowledged by participants in a discursive community. Conceptual content is constituted by these social practices of normative score-keeping.

Logical vocabulary plays a special role: it makes inferential commitments explicit, allowing speakers to say what they would otherwise only do. The conditional says explicitly that an inference is endorsed; negation says that two commitments are incompatible. Logic is the organ of self-conscious rational practice.

Material inference — non-formal entailments such as "if x is red, then x is colored" — is taken as basic, with formal logical inference as a derived special case. This reverses the standard ordering, in which logical inference is foundational and material inference is to be reduced to logical inference plus meaning postulates.

The expressive role of philosophy is to make explicit the implicit structure of our discursive practices. Brandom's later A Spirit of Trust (2019) extends this expressive project to a reading of Hegel as the great theorist of recognition and reciprocal authority, completing a sweep from Frege through Sellars to a renewed appropriation of German idealism.

The Instantiation

Take \({\mathcal I}\) to be the set of deontic statuses: commitments and entitlements that participants in discursive practice can attribute, undertake, and assess. Each element is tagged by its content, by its current attribution, and by its inferential connections to other statuses. The identity \(e\) is the empty deontic position — the slot a candidate commitment fills before its content is determined.

Composition \(\circ\) is inferential commitment: if one is committed to \(a\), then one is committed (by the inferential rules of the practice) to \(a \circ b\) for the appropriate \(b\). \(\circ\) is governed by the material inferences the practice endorses.

The resonance \(\rho(a,b)\) is inferential proximity: how short the inferential chain between \(a\) and \(b\) is, and how robustly the practice underwrites that chain. Curvature \(\sigma\) is the curvature of incompatibility: some pairs of commitments are incompatible, and \(\sigma\) is non-zero where commitments cannot be jointly maintained without revision.

Negation \(\nu(a)\) is the commitment incompatible with \(a\): the maximal element such that one cannot be entitled both to \(a\) and to \(\nu(a)\). Reframing \(\eta\) is substitution inference: the operation by which co-referential singular terms are interchanged within attitude-neutral contexts, with corresponding adjustments to the inferential commitments.

Transmission \(\omega\) is social score-keeping: the distributed updating of attributions and undertakings as participants in the practice attribute, challenge, defend, and acknowledge commitments. The grading \(\deg\) is normative complexity: atomic claims at the bottom, compounds higher, metalinguistic and self-referential commitments higher still.

\[ a \vdash_\rho b \iff \rho(a,b) \leq \theta \;\;(\text{entitlement: inference within radius } \theta) \]

Central Claim as Theorem

The first theorem-shaped claim is that inferential role is \(\rho\). Conceptual content is exhausted by the position a commitment occupies in the network of inferential relations. This is a T2 claim with strong inferentialist content: meaning reduces to resonance.

The second claim is that incompatibility is \(\nu\) and \(\sigma \neq 0\). Some commitments cannot be jointly entitled; the algebra has irreducible curvature where incompatibility holds, and \(\nu\) picks out the maximally incompatible commitment for each element. This is a T5 claim with logical force.

The third claim is that social score-keeping is \(\omega\): the distributed transmission of deontic statuses across participants and over time. The norms governing assertion and inference are sustained by what speakers do, attribute, and acknowledge. This is a T7 claim with sociological grounding.

The fourth claim is that substitution salva veritate is \(\eta\): the operation that preserves inferential role under interchange of co-referential terms. The Fregean analysis of identity is preserved within an inferentialist framework. This is a T6 claim that recovers a Fregean structural fact in inferentialist terms.

The fifth claim is that representational notions are derivative from inferential ones. The relation of being-about, traditionally treated as primitive, is reconstructed from the inferential roles of singular terms and their substitutional behavior. This is a structural claim about which features of the algebra are basic and which are derived.

What the Formalism Adds

First, material inference (non-logical entailment) is encoded in \(\rho\) directly. The algebra makes the Brandomian priority of material over formal inference structurally vivid: \(\rho\) is the basic structure, and logical inference is its formal abstraction.

Second, the formalism functions as a model of inferentialism in which logical constants are not primitive but are definable from \(\rho\) and \(\nu\). The conditional is whatever operator makes inferential commitments explicit; negation is whatever operator marks incompatibility. The expressive role of logic is given an algebraic location.

Third, the social character of \(\omega\) becomes a precise structural fact: the algebra is shared across participants, and updates propagate through the score-keeping network. Each speaker's algebra is partly hostage to the algebras of others.

Fourth, the entitlement-commitment distinction maps to a layered structure on \({\mathcal I}\): every commitment carries a defensive shield of entitlements, and the inferential network can be analyzed at either layer with corresponding consequences.

Fifth, the Brandomian project of making implicit structure explicit becomes the algebraic project of exhibiting \(\circ\), \(\rho\), \(\sigma\), \(\nu\), \(\eta\), \(\omega\), and \(\deg\) as formal structures of practices that are normally exercised without being articulated. The formalism is itself an instance of the expressive project.

Where It Diverges

First, Brandom's normative irreducibility — the claim that social norms cannot be reduced to natural facts — resists the formalism's mathematical naturalism. The algebra describes structural relations; the normative force of those relations (their being binding rather than merely descriptive) is not internal to the structure.

Second, the recognitive depth of Brandom's reading of Hegel — the I-Thou structure of mutual recognition through which subjects come to be subjects — exceeds what \(\omega\) can carry. Recognition is not merely transmission of statuses; it is constitutive of the participants themselves.

Third, the historical and developmental dimensions of A Spirit of Trust — the long story of how modern subjectivity emerges through phases of recognitive failure and recovery — require a substantive philosophy of history that the algebra cannot internally generate.

Fourth, the inferentialist analysis of singular terms and substitution faces well-known difficulties with empty names, fictional discourse, and intensional contexts. The algebra inherits these difficulties: \(\eta\) is well-defined only where the inferential conditions for substitution are met, and the boundary cases require extra apparatus.

Fifth, the relation between Brandom's expressive project and the substantive content of the practices it describes is delicate. Making explicit what is implicit is supposed to be illuminating without altering; the algebra's articulation of practices may itself become a new practice with its own content, complicating the transparency Brandom seeks.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Brandom's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the normative space of reasons: scorekeeping practices, commitments, entitlements, material inferences, and recognitive communities. Morphisms update deontic score as assertions are made, challenged, inherited, or withdrawn. π is the recognitive participant, ι is inferential role rather than mental pointing, χ is the practice, and μ is entitlement status within the game of giving and asking for reasons.

This is T13: conceptual content is the sheaf-gluing of locally tracked commitments and entitlements into a public inferential role.

Wilfrid Sellars

1912–1989 · Pittsburgh-school analytic, anti-foundationalism

The Position

Sellars' Empiricism and the Philosophy of Mind attacks the Myth of the Given: the doctrine that there is a class of cognitive episodes (sensory experiences, sense-data) whose content is non-inferentially and indubitably available and which can serve as the foundation of empirical knowledge. To know is to be able to justify, and justification belongs to "the logical space of reasons", not to natural causation. The manifest image (the world of persons) and the scientific image (the world of micro-particulars) coexist and must be reconciled by a synoptic philosophy.

The Instantiation

The space of reasons is the inferential graph \(\eta\): an item \(I\) counts as known iff it is locatable in \(\eta\) with appropriate justifying neighbours. The Myth of the Given would posit a class of items \(G\) such that \(I \in G \Rightarrow I\) is justified without \(\eta\)-connections; Sellars denies any such \(G\).

The reliability theory of perception is captured by an additional layer: a perceptual report \(I\) is well-grounded iff there is a reliable causal chain from environment to utterance, but the report enters the space of reasons only when the speaker can in principle articulate the reliability.

Concept possession is participation in inferential practice: \(\sigma(I,J)\) being available to a speaker requires that the speaker recognise the move from \(I\) to \(J\) as licit; otherwise the speaker does not yet possess \(I\).

Manifest and scientific images are two sub-algebras \(\mathcal{I}_M\) and \(\mathcal{I}_S\) over partly overlapping domains, each with its own \(\sigma\)-relations, and the synoptic project is to construct a translation/replacement map between them that preserves the irreducible features of personhood.

Psychological discourse about inner episodes (Jones' myth) is itself a theoretical posit modelled on overt linguistic behaviour: inner thoughts \(T\) are introduced as theoretical entities whose \(\sigma\)-structure mirrors that of public speech acts.

\[ I \text{ is known} \;\Leftrightarrow\; I \in \eta\text{-justified};\qquad \text{No } G\subseteq\mathcal{I} : I\in G \Rightarrow I\text{ is given} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

T-Sellars-1 (no Given). There is no non-empty subclass \(G\) of \(\mathcal{I}\) whose members are justified independently of \(\eta\); every justification is inferential.

T-Sellars-2 (logical space of reasons). Knowledge is a status in \(\eta\), not in causation; the space of reasons is normative, not natural.

T-Sellars-3 (concept possession via inference). To possess \(I\) is to be a competent navigator of \(\sigma(I,\cdot)\) and \(\sigma(\cdot,I)\); concepts are inferential roles.

T-Sellars-4 (synoptic vision). Manifest and scientific images cannot be reduced one to the other; a stereoscopic synthesis is required and is the proper task of philosophy.

T-Sellars-5 (psychological nominalism). All awareness of sorts, resemblances, and facts is a linguistic affair; pre-linguistic awareness is causal but not cognitive.

What the Formalism Adds

The Myth of the Given is identified as the postulation of a specific (and forbidden) sub-algebra, making the polemical thesis precise.

Justification and causation are explicitly separated as different operators on the algebra, removing the slide between them that Sellars diagnoses.

Inferentialism about concepts is rendered as participation in \(\eta\), connecting Sellars to Brandom and to the wider algebraic picture.

The two-images problem becomes a sub-algebra-mapping problem, exposing what a successful synthesis would have to preserve.

The myth of Jones is captured as a theoretical posit with a determinate origin in linguistic behaviour, clarifying its semi-fictional status.

Where It Diverges

Sellars' Kantian background — the categories as inferential roles inherited from a transcendental analytic — is gestured at by \(\eta\) but not faithfully reconstructed.

The synoptic vision is supposed to be philosophically achieved, not merely posited; the algebra describes the goal but cannot perform it.

The treatment of sensory consciousness as ultimately requiring a "sensum" theory in late Sellars (the "grain argument") cuts against a purely inferentialist reading the algebra encodes.

The dense, allusive style of Sellars — the appeal to "stereoscopic vision", the mythic narratives — is part of his philosophical method and the algebra renders only the doctrine.

The historical embedding in Wittgensteinian, Carnapian, and pragmatist debates of mid-century America gives Sellars' positions their force; isolated as theorems they look thinner.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Sellars' \(\mathcal{H}\) is the layered space of the manifest image, scientific image, linguistic training, and normative inferential roles. Morphisms translate reports, observations, and theoretical posits into the space of reasons, rejecting any given item that bypasses conceptual uptake. χ distinguishes manifest and scientific images, π is the language-entry participant, and ι is norm-governed classification rather than bare sensory givenness.

This is T14: the Myth of the Given is the false claim that sensory content pulls back into knowledge without inferential horizon-translation.

Donald Davidson

1917–2003 · Truth-conditional semantics, anomalous monism

The Position

Davidson proposes that a theory of meaning for a natural language can take the form of a Tarski-style truth theory, with the meaning of a sentence given by its truth conditions. Radical interpretation — assigning meanings to a stranger's utterances from scratch — proceeds by the principle of charity, maximising the truth and rationality of the speaker. Anomalous monism holds that mental events are identical to physical events under some description, but mental descriptions resist strict psychophysical laws. Belief, desire, and meaning are ascribed together in a holistic interpretation; there is no first philosophy of any one of them.

The Instantiation

For each sentence \(s\) in language \(L\), the truth-theoretic meaning is a T-sentence "\(s\) is true iff \(p\)" where \(p\) is a metalanguage statement. The algebra hosts \(L\)-sentences as ideas \(I_s\) and the T-relation \(T(I_s,I_p)\) as a special \(\sigma\).

Radical interpretation is a procedure that, given observed assents and dissents in contexts \(c\), produces a triple (truth theory, belief assignment, meaning assignment) that maximises agreement: \(\arg\max \sum_c |\{\text{true beliefs of speaker in }c\}|\) subject to systematicity constraints on \(T\).

The principle of charity is a constraint on \(\eta\)-ascription: the interpreter must assign \(\eta\)-relations among the speaker's \(I\)s such that they form a largely rational and largely true network.

Anomalous monism: each mental event \(m\) coincides extensionally with a physical event \(p\) (\(\rho(m)=\rho(p)\)) but the mental description of \(m\) and the physical description of \(p\) belong to different sub-algebras with no bridging \(\sigma\)-laws.

Triangulation: meaning emerges only in the three-way relation between speaker, interpreter, and shared world; the algebra requires a triadic operator \(\tau(I_{\text{speaker}}, I_{\text{interpreter}}, w)\) to fix content.

\[ T(I_s,I_p);\quad \text{interpret}=\arg\max\text{ charitable fit};\quad \rho(m)=\rho(p),\ \nexists\text{ law } \sigma_{\text{psy-phys}} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

T-Davidson-1 (truth-conditional meaning). The meaning of any \(L\)-sentence is determined by its T-sentence under a finitely axiomatisable truth theory for \(L\).

T-Davidson-2 (charity). No interpretation that ascribes massive false belief to the speaker is admissible; charity is constitutive of interpretation, not merely heuristic.

T-Davidson-3 (holism). Belief, desire, and meaning are interdefinable; one cannot fix any one without simultaneously fixing the others.

T-Davidson-4 (anomalous monism). Mental token-identical with physical, mental type-irreducible to physical, no strict psychophysical laws.

T-Davidson-5 (triangulation). Determinate content requires the triadic structure; a solitary speaker has no objective meanings to be ascribed.

What the Formalism Adds

The T-sentence machinery becomes a specific kind of \(\sigma\)-relation, integrable with the rest of the algebra.

Charity is exhibited as a maximisation constraint, exposing what is being maximised and at what cost.

Anomalous monism is rendered as a coincidence of \(\rho\) without coincidence of \(\sigma\)-laws, sharpening a thesis often felt as paradoxical.

Triangulation receives an explicit triadic operator, distinguishing Davidsonian externalism from Cartesian solipsism in a structural way.

Holism is captured as joint determination by a system of constraints, vindicating Davidson's claim that no piecemeal account suffices.

Where It Diverges

The choice of T-sentences as meaning-givers is contested (Foster's problem, Soames' critique); the algebra inherits the dispute by adopting the schema.

Charity has been criticised as imposing an interpreter's standards on the speaker; the maximisation framing makes this worry sharper, not less.

Anomalous monism walks a fine line between physicalism and dualism; its rendering as \(\rho\)-coincidence without \(\sigma\)-laws can be read either way and Davidson's specific resolution is lost.

The pragmatic, context-sensitive elements of language use that Davidson treats with malapropism and "passing theory" arguments fit awkwardly with a finitely axiomatised \(T\).

The unity of Davidson's project — the way semantics, philosophy of mind, and metaphysics interlock — is more architectural than the disparate operators of the algebra suggest.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Davidson's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the triangulated diagram of speaker, interpreter, and shared world, with passing theories as local horizons of use. Morphisms are charitable translations that jointly adjust belief, meaning, and truth-conditions while preserving enough rationality for interpretation to begin. π is intersubjective triangulation, μ is charitable truth-maximisation, and χ is the holistic web rather than a separable language scheme.

This is T13: radical interpretation glues speaker-local assent, interpreter theory, and worldly causes into one coherent truth-theoretic assignment.

Saul Kripke

1940–2022 · Modal logic, rigid designation

The Position

Kripke's Naming and Necessity overturns the descriptivist theory of names: proper names are rigid designators, referring to the same individual in every possible world in which that individual exists. Reference is fixed by an initial baptism and propagated through a causal chain of communication, not by a cluster of associated descriptions. Necessary a posteriori truths (e.g.\ "water is H2O", "Hesperus is Phosphorus") and contingent a priori truths (e.g.\ "the standard metre is one metre long") demonstrate that necessity and apriority come apart. Later work extends to philosophy of mind (the modal argument against type-identity) and to Wittgenstein on rule-following (the sceptical paradox of meaning).

The Instantiation

Add to the algebra a set \(W\) of possible worlds and a designation function \(d_n\) for each name \(n\): \(d_n: W \to \mathcal{O}\) where \(\mathcal{O}\) is a domain of individuals. Rigidity is the constraint that \(d_n(w)=d_n(w')\) for every \(w,w'\) where \(d_n\) is defined.

Initial baptism is an event \(b_n=(n,o,c_0)\) introducing the name \(n\) for object \(o\) at context \(c_0\); the causal-historical chain is a sequence of \(\omega\)-transmissions of \(n\) preserving its referent.

Necessary a posteriori: an identity statement \(n_1 = n_2\) where both are rigid is necessary if true at all (since both designate the same \(o\) in every world), but its truth is established empirically; thus \(\sigma(\Box(n_1=n_2),\text{evidence})\) is non-trivial.

Contingent a priori: stipulating "let \(s\) be the length of this rod at \(t_0\)" makes "rod has length \(s\) at \(t_0\)" knowable a priori in the actual world while remaining false in worlds where the rod is shorter.

Kripkenstein: the rule-following paradox is a non-existence result for facts that determine \(\sigma(I,J)\) for novel cases — no finite set of past uses fixes a unique extrapolation; meaning, if it exists, is constituted by community assent (\(\eta_{\text{community}}\)).

\[ d_n(w)=d_n(w')\ \forall w,w';\quad \Box(n_1=n_2)\text{ if }d_{n_1}=d_{n_2};\quad \nexists f:\text{past uses}\to\sigma\text{-extension} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

T-Kripke-1 (rigid designation). Proper names are rigid; descriptions are typically not.

T-Kripke-2 (causal-historical reference). Reference of a name is determined by the causal chain from baptism to use, not by satisfaction of associated descriptions.

T-Kripke-3 (necessary a posteriori). Identity statements between rigid designators are necessary if true; their truth is often a posteriori.

T-Kripke-4 (contingent a priori). Reference-fixing stipulations yield a priori knowledge of contingent truths in the actual world.

T-Kripke-5 (rule-following sceptic). No fact about a finite past fixes the meaning of "+" for arguments not yet computed; the meaning, if real, is communal.

What the Formalism Adds

Possible-world semantics gives a precise framework for distinguishing rigid from non-rigid designators, recasting old debates in modal-logic terms.

Causal-historical chains are exhibited as sequences in \(\omega\), connecting reference to a tractable transmission relation.

The split between necessity and apriority becomes a structural fact about which dimensions of the algebra (modal, epistemic) are involved.

The rule-following sceptic is sharpened into a non-existence claim, revealing what would be needed to refute it.

The modal argument against type-identity in philosophy of mind is reconstructible as: if pain and C-fibre firing are both rigid and identical, then necessarily so, but there appear to be possible worlds where they come apart.

Where It Diverges

Kripke insists his lectures are intuitive and informal; the heavy modal apparatus of possible-world semantics is one model among others, and Kripke himself is often careful about its commitments.

The causal theory of reference faces well-known problems (Madagascar cases, qua-problem, twin earth) the algebraic chain does not automatically solve.

The treatment of natural-kind terms as rigid is more controversial than the proper-name case, and the algebra glosses the distinction.

Kripkenstein is presented as an interpretation of Wittgenstein, whose own status as Wittgenstein-exegesis Kripke explicitly brackets; the algebra makes no such hermeneutic gesture.

The intuitive force of Kripke's modal arguments depends on responsiveness to particular thought-experiments (Nixon, Goldbach, gold); the algebra renders the conclusions but not the dialectical pull.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Kripke's \(\mathcal{H}\) is the category of possible worlds with accessibility morphisms, enriched by baptismal contexts and causal chains of transmission. Rigid designators are exactly constant global sections β: the name's referent remains fixed while descriptions vary by world. μ separates epistemic evidence from modal necessity, and χ appears in rule-following as the community horizon that fixes practice, if anything does.

This is T15 for names and T14 for rules: rigidity is trans-horizonal stability, while finite past use fails to pull back into a unique future \(\sigma\).

Eastern

Nāgārjuna

c. 150–250 · Madhyamaka Buddhism, emptiness

The Position

Nāgārjuna's Mūlamadhyamakakārikā argues that all phenomena are empty (śūnya) of intrinsic nature (svabhāva): nothing exists in and of itself; every entity is dependently originated in relation to causes, conditions, and conventional designations. The two truths — conventional (saṃvṛti) and ultimate (paramārtha) — coexist: conventional discourse is functionally adequate, while ultimate analysis dissolves all foundationalist metaphysics. The tetralemma (catuṣkoṭi) — \(P\), \(\neg P\), \(P \wedge \neg P\), \(\neg(P \vee \neg P)\) — exposes the inadequacy of all four positions on metaphysical questions.

The Instantiation

Emptiness is the universal claim \(\forall I:\ \nexists\, S\ (\sigma(I,S)=\top \wedge S=\text{intrinsic-nature})\); no idea has an intrinsic-nature relation to itself. Each \(I\) is constituted by its \(\sigma\)-network with other \(I\)s, not by a standalone svabhāva.

Dependent origination: \(I\)'s identity is exhausted by its position in the relational graph: \(I = \{\sigma(I,J) : J \in \mathcal{I}\}\). Remove the relations and \(I\) is nothing.

Two truths: conventional \(\eta_{\text{conv}}\) operates within the algebra and yields useful determinations; ultimate \(\eta_{\text{ult}}\) operates by reflexive dissolution: \(\eta_{\text{ult}}(I)\) yields the meta-claim that \(I\) lacks svabhāva, and applied to itself yields the same.

Tetralemma: for a metaphysical proposition \(P\), Madhyamaka analysis shows that none of \(P\), \(\neg P\), \(P\wedge\neg P\), \(\neg(P\vee\neg P)\) holds with intrinsic determinacy; the \(\sigma\)-relation between any of these and the world is conventional, not ultimate.

Emptiness of emptiness: the algebra's own emptiness-claim is itself empty: applying \(\eta_{\text{ult}}\) to "all is empty" yields that this very claim is conventionally useful but not a substantive metaphysical thesis.

\[ \forall I:\ \neg\,\mathrm{svabhāva}(I);\quad I = \{\sigma(I,J)\}_{J};\quad \eta_{\text{ult}}(\eta_{\text{ult}})=\eta_{\text{ult}} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

T-Nāgārjuna-1 (emptiness). No \(I\) has intrinsic existence; all are relationally constituted.

T-Nāgārjuna-2 (dependent origination). The identity of \(I\) is the totality of its \(\sigma\)-relations; no residue remains.

T-Nāgārjuna-3 (two truths). Conventional and ultimate analyses coexist; conventional operates within the algebra, ultimate dissolves foundationalist claims about it.

T-Nāgārjuna-4 (tetralemma). For metaphysical \(P\), no member of \(\{P,\neg P,P\wedge\neg P,\neg(P\vee\neg P)\}\) is ultimately true.

T-Nāgārjuna-5 (emptiness of emptiness). The very claim of emptiness is itself empty, preventing emptiness from becoming a new svabhāva.

What the Formalism Adds

It exhibits emptiness as a precise denial of self-standing predication, distinguishing Madhyamaka from nihilism (which would deny existence outright).

Dependent origination becomes a graph-theoretic identity condition, making explicit the relational constitution of every entity.

The two truths are rendered as two operators on the same algebra rather than two ontologies, clarifying their compatibility.

The tetralemma is captured as a four-fold exhaustion test, exposing what Nāgārjuna's analysis demands.

The reflexive emptiness of emptiness is a fixed-point property of \(\eta_{\text{ult}}\), preserving the doctrine from self-refutation.

Where It Diverges

Madhyamaka is a soteriological project: emptiness liberates from suffering by undoing clinging. The algebra captures the metaphysics but not the practice.

The tetralemma is sometimes interpreted non-classically (as rejecting bivalence) and sometimes classically (as a dialectical exhaustion); the algebra's rendering is compatible with both but commits to neither.

The interlocutors of the Mūlamadhyamakakārikā are specific Abhidharma schools whose svabhāva-realism gives Nāgārjuna's polemic its force; abstracted, the polemic loses its target.

Buddhist commitments — karma, rebirth, the path — are background conditions for emptiness-doctrine that the algebra does not invoke.

The performative dimension of Madhyamaka argument — the way each refutation is meant to dissolve the disputant's grasping — cannot be reproduced by a formal apparatus that does not address a clinging mind.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Nāgārjuna's \(\mathcal{H}\) has at least the conventional and ultimate horizons, but neither is a final substance-view. Morphisms of Madhyamaka analysis translate any asserted svabhāva into dependent origination and then dissolve the analysis itself. R is decisive in emptiness of emptiness as fixed-point self-application of ξ, δ prevents positive essence, and ι is conventional designation without intrinsic target.

This is T14 plus R: the two truths are horizon-levels of the presheaf, and every attempt to make emptiness invariant as a doctrine fails reflexively.

Confucius (Kǒng Fūzǐ)

551–479 BCE · Ritual ethics, the Analects

The Position

Confucian ethics centres on rén (humaneness, benevolence) cultivated through lǐ (ritual propriety), yì (rightness), and xiào (filial piety). The jūnzǐ (exemplary person) embodies these virtues through deliberate practice, beginning in the family and extending outward through concentric relations to society and government. The Way (dào) is realised by tradition-mediated cultivation, not by metaphysical insight or divine command. The rectification of names (zhèng míng) holds that social order requires each role-name (ruler, father, son) to be lived out according to its proper content.

The Instantiation

Each social role \(R\) (ruler, father, friend) is an idea with a normative content \(C_R\) given by the tradition. A person \(p\) standing in role \(R\) acts well iff \(\sigma(p,C_R)=\top\) — iff their conduct realises the role's content. The rectification of names is the demand that role-name \(R\) and content \(C_R\) align.

Ritual \(\lambda\) is an operator that, performed in context \(c\), shapes both the actor and the relation: \(\lambda(p,c)\) modifies \(p\) toward \(C_R\) (cultivation) and modifies the relation \(\sigma(p,q)\) toward harmony. Ritual is not empty form; it is formative practice.

Rén is the limit virtue: a measure \(\mu_{\text{rén}}(p)\) on persons increasing as \(p\) reliably acts according to \(C_R\) for every role \(p\) occupies, weighted by the depth of the relations involved (family more heavily than stranger, then extended by analogy).

Filial piety is the seed: \(\mu_{\text{rén}}\) bootstraps from \(\sigma(\text{child},\text{parent})\) — the parent-child relation is the original training ground for all later rén-cultivation.

The jūnzǐ is the asymptotic state: \(\mu_{\text{rén}}(p) \to \max\) and the conduct of \(p\) becomes spontaneous (non-deliberative) without ceasing to be ritually correct: \(\lambda\) and authentic motion coincide.

\[ \sigma(p,C_R)=\top\Rightarrow p\text{ acts as }R;\quad \mu_{\text{rén}}(p)=\sum_R w_R\cdot\mathrm{conformity}(p,C_R) \]

Central Claim as Theorem

T-Confucius-1 (rectification of names). Social harmony requires \(\sigma(p,C_R)=\top\) for every \(p\) in role \(R\); divergence between name and content produces disorder.

T-Confucius-2 (ritual as formation). Repeated ritual practice \(\lambda^n(p,c)\) cultivates \(p\) toward \(C_R\); virtue is acquired by practice, not by insight alone.

T-Confucius-3 (concentric extension). \(\mu_{\text{rén}}\) extends from family outward by analogy; one becomes humane to strangers by first being humane to one's kin.

T-Confucius-4 (governance by virtue). A ruler high in \(\mu_{\text{rén}}\) effects social order by example more reliably than by law and punishment; \(\sigma(\text{ruler},\text{people})\) propagates rén downward.

T-Confucius-5 (jūnzǐ as integration). At the asymptote, ritual correctness and spontaneous desire coincide: the exemplary person follows the heart's desire without overstepping the bounds.

What the Formalism Adds

It exhibits roles as ideas with normative contents, separating the descriptive role from its normative discharge.

Ritual is rendered as a formative operator rather than a static prescription, capturing the Confucian insight that practice shapes the practitioner.

Rén receives a measure with concentric weighting, making the family-priority structure explicit.

Governance by virtue becomes a propagation claim about \(\sigma\)-structure, exposing what Confucius proposes as an alternative to legalism.

The integration of rule and spontaneity in the jūnzǐ is captured as an asymptotic coincidence, distinguishing Confucian sagehood from mere rule-following.

Where It Diverges

The Analects is fragmentary and dialogical; its wisdom lives in particular exchanges with named disciples that the algebra cannot reproduce.

The aesthetic and musical dimensions of cultivation (the Book of Odes, the rites of music) are constitutive for Confucius and abstracted away by the operator \(\lambda\).

The depth of the parent-child bond as both natural fact and cultural achievement resists capture by a weight \(w_R\) on the rén measure.

Confucian appeal to ancient sages (Yao, Shun, the Duke of Zhou) gives normative force the algebra cannot internally generate.

The Confucian-Mohist-Daoist debates that shape early Chinese thought give Confucius' positions their dialectical specificity; in isolation they appear as one ethical theory among others.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Confucius' \(\mathcal{H}\) is the nested field of family, ritual, office, music, education, and political order. Morphisms extend cultivated conduct from parent-child relations to ruler-minister, friend-friend, and public governance, preserving role-propriety while widening its scope. π is the role-bearing person, χ is tradition, ψ is ritual attunement, and μ measures trustworthiness or humane reliability across contexts.

This is T13: social harmony is the sheaf-gluing of role-local acts of lǐ into a coherent dào embodied by the jūnzǐ.

Zhuāngzǐ

c. 4th c. BCE · Daoist, perspectival skepticism

The Position

The Zhuāngzǐ uses parable, paradox, and humor to undercut fixed perspectives: the great-and-small, useful-and-useless, this-and-that distinctions are perspectival and reversible. Skill flows from wú-wéi (non-coercive action) attuned to the dào: the cook Ding carves the ox along the natural lines and never dulls his blade. The butterfly dream raises the question whether Zhuāng dreams the butterfly or the butterfly dreams Zhuāng — without supplying an answer. Sages release fixation on names and live in spontaneous responsiveness.

The Instantiation

Each judgement \(J\) of "this/that", "good/bad", "useful/useless" is indexed by a perspective \(\pi\): \(J=J_\pi\). The Zhuangzian thesis is that for every \(J_\pi\) there is a \(\pi'\) with \(J_{\pi'}=\neg J_\pi\), and no \(\pi\) is privileged: \(\sigma(J_\pi,\text{absolute-truth})\) is undefined.

Wú-wéi is an action operator \(W\) such that \(W(p,c)\) flows along the contours of \(c\) without imposing rigid plans: \(W\) minimises friction with \(c\), in contrast to coercive \(C\) which imposes a fixed pattern regardless of \(c\).

Skill (gōng) is the asymptote of repeated \(W\): \(W^n(p,c) \to\) "knack-state" where the actor's \(\sigma\)-perception of \(c\) is so fine that action-and-context become a single flow; explicit deliberation \(\eta\) drops out.

The butterfly dream is a fixed-point puzzle: if \(\pi_{\text{Zhuang}}\) and \(\pi_{\text{butterfly}}\) are equally available perspectives, the question "which is real?" has no answer in the algebra; the perspectives are mutually nesting.

Forgetting (zuò-wàng) is an operator \(F\) that strips away accumulated names and judgements: \(F(\eta) \to \eta_{\text{open}}\) where \(\eta_{\text{open}}\) is responsive to context without prejudice; it is the Daoist counterpart to philosophical insight.

\[ J_\pi \neq J_{\pi'};\ \nexists\,\pi^*\,(J_{\pi^*}\text{ absolute});\quad W^n(p,c)\to\text{knack};\quad F:\eta\to\eta_{\text{open}} \]

Central Claim as Theorem

T-Zhuangzi-1 (perspectivism). For every evaluative judgement \(J_\pi\) there is a reversing perspective \(\pi'\); no perspective is metaphysically privileged.

T-Zhuangzi-2 (wú-wéi efficacy). \(W\) outperforms coercive \(C\) on the long run in contexts that admit flow; \(W\) preserves both actor and material.

T-Zhuangzi-3 (skill from non-action). At the limit \(W^n\), action becomes effortless and accurate; the deliberative \(\eta\) is internalised and drops below conscious access.

T-Zhuangzi-4 (dream undecidability). The reality-question between mutually nesting perspectives is undecidable from inside the algebra; one can only relinquish the question.

T-Zhuangzi-5 (forgetting as method). Insight is not the addition of correct \(\eta\)-relations but the subtraction of fixed ones; \(F\) is the Daoist philosophical operation.

What the Formalism Adds

Perspective-indexing makes the relativist thesis precise without collapsing into "anything goes": there is structure to which perspectives reverse which judgements.

Wú-wéi receives a definite contrast with coercive action and a flow-minimisation criterion, distinguishing it from passivity.

Skill from non-action is exhibited as an asymptotic limit of practice, joining Zhuangzian gōng to a wider class of skill-acquisition phenomena.

The dream puzzle becomes a structural undecidability rather than a riddle, exposing what kind of question it is.

Forgetting becomes a definite operator, distinguishing Daoist subtraction from mere ignorance.

Where It Diverges

The Zhuāngzǐ is literary; its humor, its strange characters (Cripple Shu, the Penumbra, the talking trees) are the philosophy, and the algebra renders only the doctrine.

The dào itself, as the source from which all things and perspectives arise, is gestured at but not modelled — it is not an element of the algebra, but its condition.

The relation between Zhuāngzǐ's perspectivism and his apparent commitment to the value of wú-wéi over coercion is delicate: the formal symmetry of perspectives sits uneasily with normative preference, and the algebra does not resolve the tension.

The political and social satire of the text — its mockery of Confucian seriousness, of utilitarian valuation — is part of its philosophical content and abstracted away.

The contemplative-practical tradition in which the Zhuāngzǐ functions (not as treatise but as transformative reading) cannot be reproduced by analysis of theorems.

Horizon Structure (ξ)

Zhuāngzǐ's \(\mathcal{H}\) is a flat plurality of creaturely, linguistic, dream, and sage perspectives with no privileged terminal object. Morphisms are playful transformations—butterfly to human, useful to useless, disputant to wanderer—that reveal the contingency of each distinction. π is perspectival standpoint, χ is deliberately loosened, ψ is ease or wandering, and R appears when distinctions about distinctions are themselves dissolved.

This is maximal T14: every fixed discrimination fails to pull back across wandering perspectives, so wisdom is skillful movement through non-privileged horizons.
